Apex Core — Interview Questions
22 questions with complete answers. Click any question to expand the full answer.
What are Salesforce Governor Limits? Give the most important ones by number. APEX ▼

Governor limits are runtime limits enforced by the Salesforce platform to ensure no single Apex execution consumes too many shared resources across the multitenant environment. These limits apply per transaction — meaning per trigger execution, per Apex class call, per batch execute() chunk, etc.

Critical limits to memorize:

  • SOQL queries per transaction: 100 (synchronous), 200 (async)
  • DML statements per transaction: 150
  • Records per DML statement: 10,000
  • Total heap size: 6 MB (sync), 12 MB (async)
  • CPU time: 10,000 ms (sync), 60,000 ms (async)
  • SOSL queries per transaction: 20
  • Callouts per transaction: 100
  • Records returned by SOQL: 50,000
  • Records returned by Database.QueryLocator (Batch): 50,000,000
  • Future methods per transaction: 50
  • Queueable jobs added per transaction: 50
  • Email invocations: 10
How to answer this in interview

Don't just list numbers — explain WHY limits exist. Say: "Salesforce is a multitenant platform. If one customer's code ran unlimited SOQL queries, it would slow down all other customers on the same server. Governor limits are Salesforce's way of enforcing fair resource usage across all tenants."

What is bulkification and how do you implement it correctly? APEX ▼

Bulkification means writing Apex code that handles batches of records efficiently instead of processing one record at a time. This is critical because Salesforce can pass up to 200 records to a trigger in a single execution. If your code runs a SOQL query inside a loop over 200 records, you hit 200 SOQL calls — which exceeds the 100-query limit.

The anti-pattern (WRONG — will fail in production):

// BAD CODE - DO NOT DO THIS
for(Contact c : Trigger.new) {
    // SOQL inside loop = governor limit error!
    Account acc = [SELECT Id, Name FROM Account WHERE Id = :c.AccountId];
    c.Description = acc.Name;
}

The correct bulkified pattern:

// GOOD CODE - Bulkified properly
// Step 1: Collect all IDs into a Set
Set<Id> accountIds = new Set<Id>();
for(Contact c : Trigger.new) {
    if(c.AccountId != null) accountIds.add(c.AccountId);
}

// Step 2: ONE SOQL query outside the loop
Map<Id, Account> accMap = new Map<Id, Account>(
    [SELECT Id, Name FROM Account WHERE Id IN :accountIds]
);

// Step 3: Loop using Map - no SOQL inside loop
for(Contact c : Trigger.new) {
    if(accMap.containsKey(c.AccountId)) {
        c.Description = accMap.get(c.AccountId).Name;
    }
}
Key rule to remember

Collect in a Set → Query once with IN clause → Process from a Map. This pattern: one SOQL query handles 200 records. Never put SOQL or DML inside a for loop over sObjects.

Explain List, Set, and Map in Apex — when do you use each? APEX ▼
CollectionOrdered?Duplicates?Key-Value?Best Used For
ListYes (index)YesNoOrdered collection of records, Trigger.new is a List
SetNoNo (unique only)NoCollecting unique IDs for IN clause in SOQL
MapNoKeys uniqueYesKey-value lookup by Id — fast O(1) access in loops
// List - ordered, allows duplicates
List<Account> accounts = new List<Account>();
List<String> names = new List<String>{'Alice', 'Bob', 'Alice'}; // 3 items

// Set - unordered, unique values only
Set<Id> contactIds = new Set<Id>();
for(Opportunity o : Trigger.new) contactIds.add(o.ContactId__c);
// Perfect for: WHERE Id IN :contactIds

// Map - key-value lookup, instant access by Id
Map<Id, Account> accMap = new Map<Id, Account>(
    [SELECT Id, Name, Phone FROM Account WHERE Id IN :accountIds]
);
Account a = accMap.get(someId); // instant lookup - no loop needed
Interview tip

When asked this, always give a concrete real-world use case. Say: "I use Set to collect Account IDs from a list of Contacts before querying, because IN clause requires a Set. Then I put query results in a Map for fast lookup inside a processing loop."

What are the different types of Apex exceptions and how do you handle them? APEX ▼

Common built-in exception types:

  • DmlException — thrown when DML operation fails (required field missing, duplicate rule, etc.)
  • QueryException — thrown when SOQL returns no records for assignment to single sObject
  • NullPointerException — accessing property on null variable
  • LimitException — governor limit exceeded (cannot be caught)
  • CalloutException — HTTP callout failure, timeout
  • SObjectException — field not in query accessed on sObject
  • TypeException — invalid type cast
// Always use specific exception types, not generic Exception
try {
    Account a = [SELECT Id FROM Account WHERE Name = 'Test' LIMIT 1];
    insert a;
} catch(QueryException qe) {
    // No records returned by SOQL
    System.debug('No account found: ' + qe.getMessage());
} catch(DmlException de) {
    // DML failed - get field and message details
    for(Integer i = 0; i < de.getNumDml(); i++) {
        System.debug(de.getDmlMessage(i));
        System.debug(de.getDmlFields(i));
    }
} catch(Exception e) {
    // Catch-all for unexpected errors
    System.debug('Unexpected: ' + e.getMessage() + ' at ' + e.getStackTraceString());
}

// Custom exception class - very common in senior dev code
public class LoanProcessingException extends Exception {}

if(loan.Status__c == 'Denied') {
    throw new LoanProcessingException('Loan cannot be submitted in Denied status');
}
Never do this

Never use an empty catch block. Never catch LimitException (it cannot be caught). Never swallow exceptions silently — always log or rethrow.

What is the difference between Database.insert() and just using insert keyword? APEX ▼

insert keyword: all-or-nothing. If even ONE record fails validation, the entire list rolls back and a DmlException is thrown.

Database.insert(list, allOrNone): when allOrNone=false, partial success is allowed. Records that pass insert succeed; failed records are captured in SaveResult. No exception is thrown for individual failures.

// Using Database.insert with partial DML - production pattern
List<Database.SaveResult> results = Database.insert(contactList, false);

for(Integer i = 0; i < results.size(); i++) {
    if(!results[i].isSuccess()) {
        for(Database.Error err : results[i].getErrors()) {
            System.debug('Failed: ' + contactList[i].LastName 
                + ' Reason: ' + err.getMessage()
                + ' Fields: ' + err.getFields());
        }
    } else {
        System.debug('Inserted: ' + results[i].getId());
    }
}
When to use which

Use allOrNone=false (Database.insert) when processing bulk records where partial failure is acceptable — like mass data loads, batch jobs. Use plain insert when all-or-nothing consistency is required, like financial transactions.

What are @AuraEnabled, @InvocableMethod, @RemoteAction, @future annotations — explain each? APEX ▼
  • @AuraEnabled — exposes method to LWC/Aura components. Add cacheable=true only on read-only methods — enables client-side caching. Cannot be used on methods that do DML with cacheable=true.
  • @AuraEnabled(cacheable=true) — required for @wire adapter in LWC. Returns cached data without server call on subsequent requests.
  • @InvocableMethod — exposes Apex method to Flow, Process Builder. Parameters must use List<T>. Required to run in bulk for Flow interviews.
  • @future — runs method asynchronously in separate transaction. Cannot accept sObject parameters (only primitive types or collections of primitives). Good for callouts from triggers and Mixed DML.
  • @RemoteAction — legacy annotation for Visualforce JavaScript Remoting. Rarely used in new development.
  • @TestVisible — makes private method accessible to test class without changing access modifier.
  • @ReadOnly — allows querying up to 1,000,000 rows in a single transaction. Only for reporting.
@AuraEnabled(cacheable=true)
public static List<Account> getAccounts(String nameFilter) {
    return [SELECT Id, Name FROM Account WHERE Name LIKE :('%'+nameFilter+'%')];
}

@InvocableMethod(label='Send Loan Approval Email' description='Sends email to borrower')
public static void sendApprovalEmail(List<Id> loanIds) {
    // Flow calls this - must be List parameter
}
Explain abstract class vs interface in Apex — when do you use each? APEX ▼
FeatureAbstract ClassInterface
Can have implementationYes — can have concrete methodsNo — only method signatures
Can have member variablesYesNo
Multiple inheritanceNo (single extends)Yes (implement multiple)
ConstructorYesNo
Access modifiers on methodsYesAll public by default
// Abstract class - use when sharing partial implementation
public abstract class LoanProcessor {
    public abstract void processLoan(Loan__c loan);  // must override
    public void logActivity(String msg) {  // shared implementation
        System.debug('[LoanProcessor] ' + msg);
    }
}
public class FHALoanProcessor extends LoanProcessor {
    public override void processLoan(Loan__c loan) { /* FHA logic */ }
}

// Interface - use when defining a contract with no shared code
public interface IDocumentSender {
    Boolean sendDocument(Id recordId, String templateName);
}
public class DocuSignSender implements IDocumentSender {
    public Boolean sendDocument(Id recordId, String templateName) {
        // DocuSign implementation
    }
}
Rule of thumb

Use abstract class when subclasses share some common behavior. Use interface when you want to define a contract that multiple unrelated classes can fulfill — like a DocuSign sender, an email sender, and an SMS sender all implementing ISender.

How do you write a test class properly — @isTest, @TestSetup, Test.startTest()? APEX ▼

Test classes in Salesforce require 75% overall code coverage to deploy to production. But good test classes verify behavior, not just coverage.

@isTest
public class LoanTriggerTest {
    
    // @TestSetup runs ONCE before all test methods — faster, more efficient
    @TestSetup
    static void makeData() {
        Account broker = new Account(Name='Test Broker', Type='Broker');
        insert broker;
        Contact officer = new Contact(
            FirstName='Test', LastName='Officer', AccountId=broker.Id
        );
        insert officer;
    }
    
    @isTest
    static void testLoanApproval() {
        Contact officer = [SELECT Id FROM Contact LIMIT 1];
        Loan__c loan = new Loan__c(
            LoanAmount__c = 250000, Status__c = 'Application', LoanOfficer__c = officer.Id
        );
        
        // Test.startTest() resets governor limits for the test scope
        Test.startTest();
        insert loan;
        Test.stopTest(); // Forces async processes (future, batch) to complete
        
        // Re-query to check trigger effects
        Loan__c updatedLoan = [SELECT Id, Status__c FROM Loan__c WHERE Id = :loan.Id];
        System.assertEquals('Processing', updatedLoan.Status__c, 'Status should be Processing after insert');
    }
    
    @isTest
    static void testBulkLoanInsert() {
        // Always test with 200 records — simulates real bulk trigger
        List<Loan__c> loans = new List<Loan__c>();
        for(Integer i = 0; i < 200; i++) {
            loans.add(new Loan__c(LoanAmount__c = 100000 + i, Status__c = 'Application'));
        }
        Test.startTest();
        insert loans;
        Test.stopTest();
        System.assertEquals(200, [SELECT COUNT() FROM Loan__c]);
    }
}
Key rules

1. Always use Test.startTest/stopTest to get fresh limits and complete async. 2. Always test bulk (200 records). 3. Use System.assertEquals(expected, actual, 'message') — the message tells you what failed. 4. Use @TestSetup for shared data — it runs once and is rolled back between test methods. 5. SeeAllData=false by default (test data isolation). Never use SeeAllData=true unless absolutely necessary.

SOQL Deep Dive — Interview Questions
14 questions with complete answers and code examples
What is SOQL injection and how do you prevent it? Show vulnerable code and the fix. SOQL ▼

SOQL injection is a security vulnerability where a malicious user inputs text that manipulates a dynamic SOQL query to expose unauthorized data — similar to SQL injection in traditional databases.

// VULNERABLE CODE - Never do this
public static List<Account> searchAccounts(String name) {
    String query = 'SELECT Id, Name FROM Account WHERE Name = \'' + name + '\'';
    return Database.query(query);
    // If name = "test' OR Name != '", this returns ALL accounts!
}

// FIX 1: escapeSingleQuotes() - escapes the user input
public static List<Account> searchAccounts(String name) {
    String safeName = String.escapeSingleQuotes(name);
    String query = 'SELECT Id, Name FROM Account WHERE Name = \'' + safeName + '\'';
    return Database.query(query);
}

// FIX 2: Bind variables in dynamic SOQL (best approach)
public static List<Account> searchAccounts(String name) {
    // :name is a bind variable - Salesforce handles escaping automatically
    return Database.query('SELECT Id, Name FROM Account WHERE Name = :name');
}

// FIX 3: Use static SOQL instead of dynamic when possible
return [SELECT Id, Name FROM Account WHERE Name = :name];
Best practice order

1. Use static SOQL (not dynamic) whenever possible. 2. If dynamic SOQL is needed, use bind variable :name syntax. 3. If you must concatenate, always use escapeSingleQuotes(). 4. Validate and whitelist the input (if filtering by Status__c, verify the value is in an allowed list).

Write a SOQL query to get all Contacts with their Account name and parent Account. Explain relationship queries. SOQL ▼

Salesforce has two types of relationship queries:

1. Child-to-Parent (traversing up): Use dot notation. You traverse from the child up to the parent. The relationship name depends on the field.

// Child-to-Parent: Contact → Account → Account's Parent
List<Contact> contacts = [
    SELECT Id, 
           FirstName, 
           LastName,
           Account.Name,                  // parent Account name
           Account.Phone,                 // parent Account phone
           Account.Parent.Name,           // grandparent Account name
           Account.Parent.Industry        // grandparent field (up to 5 levels)
    FROM Contact
    WHERE Account.Type = 'Broker'
];

// Accessing in Apex:
for(Contact c : contacts) {
    System.debug(c.Account.Name);          // parent account name
    System.debug(c.Account.Parent.Name);   // grandparent name
}

2. Parent-to-Child (subquery - traversing down): Use relationship name in a subquery. For standard objects the plural of the child object name. For custom, use __r (relationship) name.

// Parent-to-Child: Account with all its Contacts (subquery)
List<Account> accounts = [
    SELECT Id, Name, 
           (SELECT Id, FirstName, LastName, Email FROM Contacts),       // standard child
           (SELECT Id, CloseDate, Amount FROM Opportunities WHERE StageName != 'Closed Lost'),
           (SELECT Id, LoanAmount__c FROM Loans__r)                     // custom child (__r)
    FROM Account
    WHERE Id IN :accountIds
];

// Accessing child records:
for(Account a : accounts) {
    for(Contact c : a.Contacts) {  // iterating over subquery results
        System.debug(c.Email);
    }
}
Explain aggregate functions in SOQL — GROUP BY, HAVING, AggregateResult SOQL ▼ 
// COUNT, SUM, MIN, MAX, AVG - aggregate functions
List<AggregateResult> results = [
    SELECT Account.Name accName, 
           COUNT(Id) loanCount,
           SUM(LoanAmount__c) totalAmount,
           AVG(LoanAmount__c) avgAmount,
           MAX(LoanAmount__c) maxLoan
    FROM Loan__c
    WHERE Status__c != 'Denied'
    GROUP BY Account.Name
    HAVING COUNT(Id) > 5          // HAVING filters on aggregate result (like WHERE for GROUP BY)
    ORDER BY SUM(LoanAmount__c) DESC
    LIMIT 20
];

// Accessing AggregateResult - use alias or field name as string
for(AggregateResult ar : results) {
    String name = (String) ar.get('accName');
    Integer count = (Integer) ar.get('loanCount');
    Decimal total = (Decimal) ar.get('totalAmount');
    System.debug(name + ': ' + count + ' loans, total: ' + total);
}

// COUNT() vs COUNT(Id)
// COUNT() - returns Integer, no GROUP BY needed, no field needed
Integer total = [SELECT COUNT() FROM Account WHERE Industry = 'Financial'];
// COUNT(Id) - returns AggregateResult, can be used with GROUP BY
HAVING vs WHERE

WHERE filters individual records BEFORE grouping. HAVING filters the grouped results AFTER aggregation. You cannot use WHERE on an aggregate function — use HAVING for that.

What is WITH SECURITY_ENFORCED and WITH USER_MODE — what is the difference? SOQL ▼

Both enforce field-level security in SOQL queries, but they work differently:

FeatureWITH SECURITY_ENFORCEDWITH USER_MODE
Available sinceAPI 48.0 (Spring '20)API 56.0 (Spring '23)
If inaccessible field queriedThrows QueryException — all or nothingStrips inaccessible fields silently, no error
DML enforcementNoYes — also enforces CRUD on DML
UsageIn SOQL query clauseAfter Database.query() or as class modifier
// WITH SECURITY_ENFORCED - throws QueryException if any field is not accessible
List<Account> accs = [SELECT Id, Name, AnnualRevenue 
                       FROM Account WITH SECURITY_ENFORCED];
// Limitation: does NOT work in subqueries, does NOT work with GROUP BY

// WITH USER_MODE - more flexible, newer approach
List<Account> accs = [SELECT Id, Name, AnnualRevenue 
                       FROM Account WITH USER_MODE];

// WITH USER_MODE can also be applied to an entire class
public with sharing class AccountService {
    public static List<Account> getAccounts() {
        return Database.query('SELECT Id, Name FROM Account', AccessLevel.USER_MODE);
    }
}
Recommendation

Prefer WITH USER_MODE for new code. It's more flexible, supports subqueries, and also enforces CRUD during DML. WITH SECURITY_ENFORCED is older and has limitations with subqueries and aggregate functions.

What is SOQL FOR UPDATE — row locking? When do you use it? SOQL ▼

FOR UPDATE locks the queried records in the database, preventing other transactions from modifying them until your current transaction completes. It is pessimistic locking — assume conflict and prevent it.

// FOR UPDATE - locks records while you process them
List<Loan__c> loans = [SELECT Id, Status__c, LoanAmount__c 
                        FROM Loan__c 
                        WHERE Status__c = 'Underwriting'
                        FOR UPDATE]; // Locked! No one else can modify these

// Process and update - other transactions will wait or fail
for(Loan__c l : loans) {
    l.Status__c = 'Approved';
    l.ApprovedDate__c = Date.today();
}
update loans; // Lock released after DML completes

When to use FOR UPDATE:

  • Financial transactions where double-processing must be prevented
  • Status transitions that should only happen once (e.g., loan approval)
  • Counter/accumulator fields updated by multiple concurrent users
Deadlock risk

If two transactions lock different sets of records and then try to access each other's locked records, a deadlock occurs. Salesforce will throw a QueryException. Always lock records in a consistent order and keep locked transactions short.

Triggers & Handler Patterns
14 questions with complete code — the #1 interview topic for Salesforce Senior Devs
Walk me through your trigger handler pattern — show complete code TRIGGERS ▼

The trigger handler pattern separates the trigger (routing) from the business logic (handler class). The trigger file should have ZERO business logic — it only calls the handler. The handler class has methods for each trigger event.

// STEP 1: The Trigger File (ContactTrigger.trigger)
// This file has NO logic - only routing
trigger ContactTrigger on Contact (
    before insert, before update, before delete,
    after insert, after update, after delete, after undelete
) {
    ContactTriggerHandler handler = new ContactTriggerHandler();
    handler.run();
}

// STEP 2: Base Handler Class (TriggerHandler.cls)
// Reusable base class for all triggers in the org
public virtual class TriggerHandler {
    
    private static Set<String> bypassedHandlers = new Set<String>();
    
    public void run() {
        if(isBypassed(getHandlerName())) return;
        
        switch on Trigger.operationType {
            when BEFORE_INSERT  { beforeInsert(); }
            when BEFORE_UPDATE  { beforeUpdate(); }
            when BEFORE_DELETE  { beforeDelete(); }
            when AFTER_INSERT   { afterInsert(); }
            when AFTER_UPDATE   { afterUpdate(); }
            when AFTER_DELETE   { afterDelete(); }
            when AFTER_UNDELETE { afterUndelete(); }
        }
    }
    
    protected virtual void beforeInsert() {}
    protected virtual void beforeUpdate() {}
    protected virtual void beforeDelete() {}
    protected virtual void afterInsert() {}
    protected virtual void afterUpdate() {}
    protected virtual void afterDelete() {}
    protected virtual void afterUndelete() {}
    
    public static void bypass(String handlerName) { bypassedHandlers.add(handlerName); }
    public static Boolean isBypassed(String handlerName) { return bypassedHandlers.contains(handlerName); }
    private String getHandlerName() { return String.valueOf(this).substring(0, String.valueOf(this).indexOf(':')); }
}

// STEP 3: Specific Handler (ContactTriggerHandler.cls)
public class ContactTriggerHandler extends TriggerHandler {
    
    private List<Contact> newContacts;
    private Map<Id, Contact> oldContactMap;
    
    public ContactTriggerHandler() {
        newContacts = Trigger.new;
        oldContactMap = (Map<Id, Contact>) Trigger.oldMap;
    }
    
    protected override void beforeInsert() {
        ContactService.preventDuplicates(newContacts);
        ContactService.populateDefaultValues(newContacts);
    }
    
    protected override void afterUpdate() {
        ContactService.syncPhoneToAccount(newContacts, oldContactMap);
    }
}
Interview answer tip

Explain the separation of concerns: trigger = routing only, handler = event dispatching, service = business logic. This makes code testable, maintainable, and allows bypass logic for data migrations. Always mention: "One trigger per object — all events in one file."

How do you prevent recursive triggers? What are the pitfalls? TRIGGERS ▼

Recursive trigger = trigger fires, performs DML on same object, which fires the trigger again → infinite loop → CPU limit exceeded.

// Method 1: Static Boolean Flag (Simple but imperfect)
public class TriggerRecursionPrevention {
    public static Boolean isFirstRun = true;
}

trigger AccountTrigger on Account (after update) {
    if(TriggerRecursionPrevention.isFirstRun) {
        TriggerRecursionPrevention.isFirstRun = false;
        // logic here
    }
}
// PROBLEM: Only runs once per transaction even for different records

// Method 2: Set of processed IDs (Better - handles bulk correctly)
public class TriggerRecursionHelper {
    public static Set<Id> processedIds = new Set<Id>();
}

protected override void afterUpdate() {
    List<Contact> toProcess = new List<Contact>();
    for(Contact c : newContacts) {
        if(!TriggerRecursionHelper.processedIds.contains(c.Id)) {
            toProcess.add(c);
            TriggerRecursionHelper.processedIds.add(c.Id);
        }
    }
    if(!toProcess.isEmpty()) {
        ContactService.syncPhone(toProcess);
    }
}
Pitfall

Static variables persist for the entire transaction, not just one trigger call. In a batch job processing 200 records at a time, your static flag from chunk 1 still exists in chunk 2 — this can cause logic to be skipped entirely for later chunks. Design with this in mind.

What is the difference between before and after trigger — when do you use each? TRIGGERS ▼
FeatureBefore TriggerAfter Trigger
Record has Id?No (insert), Yes (update/delete)Yes — always has Id
Can modify record directly?Yes — no DML neededNo — must use DML (additional DML)
Database committed yet?No — record not in DB yetYes — record is in DB
Use forValidation, field population/defaultingCross-object updates, callouts, related record creation
// BEFORE INSERT — validate and set fields (no DML needed)
protected override void beforeInsert() {
    for(Loan__c loan : Trigger.new) {
        // Validation - field directly modifiable
        if(loan.LoanAmount__c > 2000000 && loan.LoanType__c == 'FHA') {
            loan.addError('FHA loans cannot exceed $2,000,000');
        }
        // Field default - set directly, no update DML needed
        if(loan.Status__c == null) loan.Status__c = 'Application';
    }
}

// AFTER INSERT — create related records (record now has Id)
protected override void afterInsert() {
    List<LoanTask__c> tasks = new List<LoanTask__c>();
    for(Loan__c loan : Trigger.new) {
        // Now we have loan.Id (not available in before insert)
        tasks.add(new LoanTask__c(Loan__c = loan.Id, Type__c = 'Initial Review'));
    }
    if(!tasks.isEmpty()) insert tasks;
}
Async Apex — Interview Questions
Future, Queueable, Batch, Scheduled — complete with code and comparison
What is the difference between Future, Queueable, Batch, and Scheduled Apex? When do you use each? ASYNC ▼
Feature@futureQueueableBatchScheduled
ChainableNoYesFrom finish()Via Schedulable
sObject paramNoYesYesYes
Job monitoringNoYes (AsyncApexJob)YesYes (CronTrigger)
Records limitUnlimited (careful!)Unlimited50M (QueryLocator)N/A
CalloutsYes (callout=true)YesYes (AllowsCallouts)Via inner class
Use forSimple async ops, Mixed DML fixComplex chained jobsMass data processingRecurring jobs
// @future - simple, no sObject params, not chainable
@future(callout=true)
public static void sendToExternalSystem(List<Id> recordIds) {
    // Only Id (primitive) - not full sObject
    List<Loan__c> loans = [SELECT Id, LoanAmount__c FROM Loan__c WHERE Id IN :recordIds];
    // Make callout here
}

// Queueable - complex async, chainable, sObject allowed
public class CreditCheckQueueable implements Queueable, Database.AllowsCallouts {
    private List<Loan__c> loans;
    public CreditCheckQueueable(List<Loan__c> loans) { this.loans = loans; }
    
    public void execute(QueueableContext ctx) {
        // Process loans, make callout to Experian
        // Chain another job if needed:
        System.enqueueJob(new OFACCheckQueueable(loans));  // chaining!
    }
}
// To start: System.enqueueJob(new CreditCheckQueueable(loans));

// Batch - large data volumes
public class LoanStatusBatch implements Database.Batchable<SObject>, Database.Stateful {
    public Integer successCount = 0; // Stateful - preserved between chunks
    public Database.QueryLocator start(Database.BatchableContext bc) {
        return Database.getQueryLocator('SELECT Id, Status__c FROM Loan__c WHERE Status__c = \'Active\'');
    }
    public void execute(Database.BatchableContext bc, List<SObject> scope) {
        // Process up to 200 records per chunk
        successCount += scope.size();
    }
    public void finish(Database.BatchableContext bc) {
        System.debug('Total processed: ' + successCount);
    }
}
// To run: Database.executeBatch(new LoanStatusBatch(), 200);
What is Mixed DML error and how do you fix it? ASYNC ▼

Mixed DML occurs when you try to do DML on a setup object (like User, Profile, PermissionSet) AND a non-setup object (like Account, Contact) in the same transaction. Salesforce does not allow this.

Setup objects: User, UserRole, Profile, PermissionSet, PermissionSetAssignment, GroupMember, etc.

// This throws: MIXED_DML_OPERATION error
User u = new User(ProfileId = adminProfileId, Username = '[email protected]'...);
insert u;  // Setup object DML

Account a = new Account(Name = 'Test');
insert a;  // Non-setup DML in same transaction = ERROR

// FIX 1: Use @future for the non-setup DML
@future
public static void createAccountAsync(String name) {
    insert new Account(Name = name);
}
// @future runs in a NEW transaction - no Mixed DML

// FIX 2: In tests, use System.runAs() to separate transactions
@isTest
static void testUserCreation() {
    User newUser; 
    System.runAs(new User(Id = UserInfo.getUserId())) {
        newUser = createTestUser(); // Setup DML here
    }
    System.runAs(newUser) {
        insert new Account(Name = 'Test'); // Non-setup DML here
    }
}
Real scenario from your projects

In the Mortgage platform, when creating a new loan officer (User) and their first Loan record, you'd hit Mixed DML. Solution: insert User first, then use @future to create the Loan record in a separate transaction.

Lightning Web Components — Interview Questions
20 questions with complete answers and code examples
What is the difference between @api, @track, and @wire — explain with code LWC ▼
DecoratorPurposeAccessReactive?
@apiPublic property — accessible from parent component or Lightning App BuilderExternal — parent can set itYes
@trackReactive private property — triggers re-render when value changesInternal onlyYes (deep object/array)
@wireDeclarative data fetching — connects to wire adapters or cacheable ApexInternal, auto-populatedYes (auto-refires)
// Import decorators from lwc
import { LightningElement, api, track, wire } from 'lwc';
import getLoans from '@salesforce/apex/LoanController.getLoans';
import { getRecord, getFieldValue } from 'lightning/uiRecordApi';
import LOAN_AMOUNT_FIELD from '@salesforce/schema/Loan__c.LoanAmount__c';

export default class LoanDetails extends LightningElement {
    
    // @api - parent passes this in, re-renders when parent changes it
    @api recordId;          // Required for record pages
    @api loanType = 'FHA'; // With default value
    
    // @track - for deeply nested object changes
    // Note: in current LWC, plain properties ARE reactive for reassignment
    // @track only needed when mutating nested object/array properties
    @track filterCriteria = { minAmount: 0, maxAmount: 1000000, status: 'Active' };
    
    isLoading = false; // Plain property - reactive for reassignment (no @track needed)
    
    // @wire with Apex - requires cacheable=true on Apex method
    @wire(getLoans, { loanType: '$loanType' }) // '$loanType' = reactive param
    wiredLoans({ error, data }) {
        if(data) {
            this.loans = data;
            this.error = undefined;
        } else if(error) {
            this.error = error;
            this.loans = undefined;
        }
    }
    
    // @wire with standard adapter
    @wire(getRecord, { recordId: '$recordId', fields: [LOAN_AMOUNT_FIELD] })
    loanRecord;
    
    get loanAmount() {
        return getFieldValue(this.loanRecord.data, LOAN_AMOUNT_FIELD);
    }
}
Key distinction interviewers test

@track is no longer needed for primitive reassignment in modern LWC — all properties are reactive by default for assignment. @track is only needed when you mutate a nested property like this.obj.nestedProp = 'value' without reassigning the top-level object.

How do you communicate between LWC components — all 3 patterns with code LWC ▼

Pattern 1: Parent → Child (via @api property or method call)

// Parent HTML - sets child @api property
<c-child-component loan-type={selectedType} loan-id={currentLoanId}></c-child-component>
<lightning-button label="Reset Child" onclick={resetChild}></lightning-button>

// Parent JS - call child method using querySelector
resetChild() {
    this.template.querySelector('c-child-component').resetForm(); // calls child's @api method
}

// Child JS - receives @api property
@api loanType;
@api resetForm() { // callable by parent
    this.formData = {};
    this.errors = [];
}

Pattern 2: Child → Parent (via CustomEvent)

// Child JS - dispatch event with data
handleSave() {
    const event = new CustomEvent('loansaved', {
        detail: { loanId: this.loanId, status: 'Approved' },
        bubbles: true,   // propagates up through DOM
        composed: false  // stays within component tree (false = default)
    });
    this.dispatchEvent(event);
}

// Parent HTML - listen for the event
<c-loan-form onloansaved={handleLoanSaved}></c-loan-form>

// Parent JS - handle event
handleLoanSaved(event) {
    const { loanId, status } = event.detail;
    this.showToast('Success', 'Loan ' + loanId + ' saved', 'success');
}

Pattern 3: Unrelated Components → Lightning Message Service (LMS)

// Step 1: Create MessageChannel metadata file
// LoanStatusChannel.messageChannel-meta.xml

// Step 2: Publisher component
import { publish, MessageContext } from 'lightning/messageService';
import LOAN_STATUS_CHANNEL from '@salesforce/messageChannel/LoanStatusChannel__c';

@wire(MessageContext) messageContext;

publishLoanUpdate(loanId, status) {
    publish(this.messageContext, LOAN_STATUS_CHANNEL, { loanId, status });
}

// Step 3: Subscriber component
import { subscribe, unsubscribe, APPLICATION_SCOPE, MessageContext } from 'lightning/messageService';
import LOAN_STATUS_CHANNEL from '@salesforce/messageChannel/LoanStatusChannel__c';

subscription = null;

connectedCallback() {
    this.subscription = subscribe(this.messageContext, LOAN_STATUS_CHANNEL, 
        (msg) => this.handleLoanUpdate(msg), { scope: APPLICATION_SCOPE });
}
disconnectedCallback() {
    unsubscribe(this.subscription); // Always unsubscribe to prevent memory leaks
}
handleLoanUpdate(message) {
    this.currentLoanId = message.loanId;
    this.loanStatus = message.status;
}
LWC lifecycle hooks — what fires in what order? LWC ▼

Order of lifecycle hooks: constructor → connectedCallback → render → renderedCallback

export default class LoanComponent extends LightningElement {
    
    constructor() {
        super(); // ALWAYS call super() first - mandatory
        // Fires: When component is instantiated
        // Cannot: Access DOM (not rendered yet), access @api properties
        // Can: Set private properties, initialize collections
        this.loans = [];
    }
    
    connectedCallback() {
        // Fires: When component is inserted into the DOM
        // Can: Access template (careful - may not be fully rendered)
        // Use for: Initialization logic, subscriptions, imperative Apex calls
        this.loadInitialData();
        this.registerListeners(); // LMS subscriptions go here
    }
    
    renderedCallback() {
        // Fires: After every render (initial and re-renders)
        // WARNING: Can cause infinite loop if you modify state here without a guard
        // Use hasRendered flag to run once:
        if(this.hasRendered) return;
        this.hasRendered = true;
        // Third-party JS initialization (charts, maps) goes here - DOM is ready
        this.initializeChart();
    }
    
    disconnectedCallback() {
        // Fires: When component is removed from DOM
        // Use for: Cleanup - unsubscribe LMS, clear intervals/timeouts
        unsubscribe(this.subscription);
        clearInterval(this.pollingInterval);
    }
    
    errorCallback(error, stack) {
        // Fires: When child component throws an error
        // Use for: Graceful error handling, error boundary pattern
        this.errorMessage = error.message;
    }
}
Wire vs Imperative Apex — when do you use each? Show both patterns. LWC ▼
Feature@wire (Declarative)Imperative (JS function call)
Apex methodMust have cacheable=trueAny @AuraEnabled method
When it firesAutomatically on component load and when params changeYou control exactly when (button click, connectedCallback)
CachingYes — client-side caching by defaultNo — fresh call every time
DML ApexNo — cacheable methods cannot do DMLYes — can do any operation
Use forReading data, auto-refresh on param changeUser-triggered actions, DML, conditional calls
// WIRE - declarative, auto-fires, cached
@wire(getLoansByStatus, { status: '$selectedStatus' }) // fires when selectedStatus changes
wiredLoans({ data, error }) {
    this.isLoading = false;
    if(data) { this.loans = data; this.error = undefined; }
    else if(error) { this.error = error.body.message; }
}

// Refresh wire data after DML:
import { refreshApex } from '@salesforce/apex';
handleSave() {
    saveLoan({ loanData: this.loanRecord })
        .then(() => refreshApex(this.wiredLoans)) // force wire to refetch
        .catch(error => this.handleError(error));
}

// IMPERATIVE - user-triggered, no caching, more control
handleSearch() {
    this.isLoading = true;
    searchLoans({ searchTerm: this.searchTerm, minAmount: this.minAmount })
        .then(result => {
            this.loans = result;
            this.isLoading = false;
        })
        .catch(error => {
            this.error = error.body.message;
            this.isLoading = false;
        });
}

// Or with async/await:
async handleSaveLoan() {
    try {
        this.isLoading = true;
        const result = await saveLoan({ loan: this.loanData });
        this.showToast('Success', 'Loan saved: ' + result.Id, 'success');
        await refreshApex(this.wiredLoans);
    } catch(error) {
        this.showToast('Error', error.body.message, 'error');
    } finally {
        this.isLoading = false;
    }
}
Integration & APIs — Interview Questions
18 questions with complete answers — covers REST, SOAP, Named Credentials, and your project integrations
How do you make an authenticated REST API callout in Apex? Show complete code with Named Credentials. INTEGRATION ▼ 
// Named Credential: Created in Setup → Security → Named Credentials
// Name: ExperianCreditAPI, URL: https://api.experian.com
// Auth: OAuth 2.0 configured here (no credentials in code)

public class ExperianCreditService {
    
    public static CreditResponse pullCreditReport(String ssn, String lastName) {
        
        // Build request using Named Credential (callout:ExperianCreditAPI)
        HttpRequest req = new HttpRequest();
        req.setEndpoint('callout:ExperianCreditAPI/v1/credit-report'); // Named Cred prefix
        req.setMethod('POST');
        req.setHeader('Content-Type', 'application/json');
        req.setHeader('Accept', 'application/json');
        req.setTimeout(30000); // 30 seconds max
        
        // Build request body
        Map<String, Object> body = new Map<String, Object>{
            'ssn' => ssn,
            'lastName' => lastName,
            'requestType' => 'FULL'
        };
        req.setBody(JSON.serialize(body));
        
        // Execute callout
        Http http = new Http();
        HttpResponse res;
        
        try {
            res = http.send(req);
        } catch(CalloutException e) {
            throw new CreditServiceException('Callout failed: ' + e.getMessage());
        }
        
        // Handle response by status code
        if(res.getStatusCode() == 200) {
            return (CreditResponse) JSON.deserialize(res.getBody(), CreditResponse.class);
        } else if(res.getStatusCode() == 401) {
            throw new CreditServiceException('Authentication failed - check Named Credential');
        } else if(res.getStatusCode() == 429) {
            throw new CreditServiceException('Rate limit exceeded - retry after delay');
        } else {
            throw new CreditServiceException('API Error ' + res.getStatusCode() + ': ' + res.getBody());
        }
    }
    
    // Response wrapper class
    public class CreditResponse {
        public Integer ficoScore;
        public String riskCategory;
        public List<TradelineItem> tradelines;
    }
}

// Test class using HttpCalloutMock
@isTest
public class ExperianCreditServiceTest {
    
    @isTest
    static void testSuccessfulCreditPull() {
        // Mock the HTTP callout response
        Test.setMock(HttpCalloutMock.class, new ExperianMockCallout());
        Test.startTest();
        ExperianCreditService.CreditResponse result = 
            ExperianCreditService.pullCreditReport('123-45-6789', 'Doe');
        Test.stopTest();
        System.assertEquals(720, result.ficoScore);
    }
    
    public class ExperianMockCallout implements HttpCalloutMock {
        public HttpResponse respond(HttpRequest req) {
            HttpResponse res = new HttpResponse();
            res.setStatusCode(200);
            res.setHeader('Content-Type', 'application/json');
            res.setBody('{"ficoScore": 720, "riskCategory": "Good"}');
            return res;
        }
    }
}
Use your Experian experience here

Tell the interviewer: "In my Mortgage Origination Platform project, I used Named Credentials to authenticate with Experian's credit bureau API. This way, the client credentials were managed by Salesforce admins in Named Credentials setup — not hardcoded in the Apex class. This follows security best practices and makes credential rotation easy."

Why can't you make callouts from triggers? How do you work around it? INTEGRATION ▼

Triggers run synchronously within a database transaction. If a callout is made during a transaction and the external system takes 30 seconds, the database transaction locks those records for 30 seconds — this is a major problem in a multitenant environment.

Salesforce enforces: You cannot make an HTTP callout from within a trigger (or any code that runs in a transaction that has already performed DML).

// This throws: System.CalloutException: You have uncommitted work pending
trigger LoanTrigger on Loan__c (after insert) {
    for(Loan__c loan : Trigger.new) {
        ExperianService.pullCredit(loan.Id); // ERROR - DML already happened
    }
}

// SOLUTION 1: @future with callout=true
trigger LoanTrigger on Loan__c (after insert) {
    Set<Id> loanIds = Trigger.newMap.keySet();
    ExperianService.pullCreditAsync(loanIds); // fires after transaction commits
}

public class ExperianService {
    @future(callout=true)
    public static void pullCreditAsync(Set<Id> loanIds) {
        // This runs AFTER the trigger transaction completes
        // Safe to make callouts here
        for(Id loanId : loanIds) {
            pullCreditReport(loanId); // callout now safe
        }
    }
}

// SOLUTION 2: Queueable (better - sObject support, chainable)
trigger LoanTrigger on Loan__c (after insert) {
    System.enqueueJob(new CreditCheckQueueable(Trigger.new));
}

// SOLUTION 3: Platform Events (best for complex workflows)
// Trigger publishes event → separate subscriber trigger handles callout
Platform Events & Change Data Capture
From your Mortgage Origination Platform — use your real project experience here
What are Platform Events? How do you publish and subscribe? Show complete code. PLATFORM EVENTS ▼

Platform Events are a messaging solution built on Salesforce's streaming infrastructure. They enable event-driven architecture — loosely coupled components communicate through events rather than direct calls. The publisher doesn't know who the subscribers are.

// 1. Define Platform Event: Loan_Status_Update__e
// Fields: LoanId__c (Text), NewStatus__c (Text), UpdatedBy__c (Text), Timestamp__c (DateTime)

// 2. PUBLISH from Apex (trigger or service class)
public class LoanStatusEventPublisher {
    
    public static void publishStatusChange(List<Loan__c> updatedLoans) {
        List<Loan_Status_Update__e> events = new List<Loan_Status_Update__e>();
        
        for(Loan__c loan : updatedLoans) {
            events.add(new Loan_Status_Update__e(
                LoanId__c = loan.Id,
                NewStatus__c = loan.Status__c,
                UpdatedBy__c = UserInfo.getUserId(),
                Timestamp__c = DateTime.now()
            ));
        }
        
        // Publish and check results
        List<Database.SaveResult> results = EventBus.publish(events);
        for(Database.SaveResult sr : results) {
            if(!sr.isSuccess()) {
                System.debug('Event publish failed: ' + sr.getErrors()[0].getMessage());
            }
        }
    }
}

// 3. SUBSCRIBE via Apex trigger
trigger LoanStatusUpdateTrigger on Loan_Status_Update__e (after insert) {
    List<Loan_Status_Update__e> events = Trigger.new;
    // Trigger.new contains the events - NOT database records
    
    List<Id> loanIds = new List<Id>();
    for(Loan_Status_Update__e event : events) {
        loanIds.add(event.LoanId__c);
    }
    
    // Now do whatever needs to happen when loan status changes
    // e.g., notify DocuSign, update related records, send email
    DocuSignNotificationService.notifyOnStatusChange(loanIds, events);
}

// 4. Publish Behavior: IMPORTANT DISTINCTION
// PublishImmediately: event fires even if transaction ROLLS BACK
// PublishAfterCommit (default): event fires only if transaction COMMITS successfully
// For financial systems: ALWAYS use PublishAfterCommit (default)
Your project story

"In our Mortgage Origination Platform, we used Platform Events to decouple the loan status update process from DocuSign document generation. When a loan moved to 'Approved' status, a Platform Event was published. A separate trigger subscribed to that event and triggered the DocuSign envelope. This meant our loan processing code didn't need to know about DocuSign — clean separation of concerns."

What is Change Data Capture (CDC) — how is it different from Platform Events?PLATFORM EVENTS▼
FeaturePlatform EventsChange Data Capture
Who creates itYou publish via EventBus.publish()Salesforce auto-generates on record DML
What triggers itYour explicit publish callAny Insert/Update/Delete/Undelete on subscribed object
PayloadCustom fields you defineChangeEventHeader: changed fields, change type, record IDs
Use forBusiness process events, decoupling, notificationsReal-time data sync to external systems
Retention72 hours3 days
SetupCreate custom PE objectEnable per-object in Setup → Change Data Capture
// Subscribe to Loan__c CDC changes in Apex trigger
trigger LoanCDCTrigger on LoanChangeEvent (after insert) {
  for (LoanChangeEvent evt : Trigger.new) {
    String changeType = evt.ChangeEventHeader.changeType; // CREATE UPDATE DELETE UNDELETE
    List<String> changedFields = evt.ChangeEventHeader.changedFields;
    List<Id> recordIds = evt.ChangeEventHeader.recordIds;

    if (changeType == 'UPDATE' && changedFields.contains('Status__c')) {
      System.enqueueJob(new StatusSyncQueueable(recordIds[0], evt.Status__c));
    }
  }
}
What is the ReplayId in Platform Events — how does event replay work?PLATFORM EVENTS▼

Every Platform Event gets a monotonically increasing ReplayId. Subscribers use it to resume from a specific point in the 72-hour retention window — critical for disaster recovery.

// empApi in LWC — replay options
// -1 = new events only (default)
// -2 = replay ALL retained events from earliest available
// specific number = replay from that event onward

import { subscribe } from 'lightning/empApi';
subscribe('/event/Loan_Status_Change__e', -2, callback); // replay all retained
subscribe('/event/Loan_Status_Change__e', 12345, callback); // replay from event 12345

// Pattern: store last processed ReplayId in Custom Setting
// On system restart: retrieve stored ReplayId → subscribe from that point
// Ensures no events are missed during downtime (within 72-hour window)
Disaster recovery

If your external system is down for 2 hours it can resubscribe with the last processed ReplayId and receive every missed event. This is the killer advantage of Platform Events over custom polling tables.

Publish Immediately vs Publish After Commit — which to use and when?PLATFORM EVENTS▼
Publish ImmediatelyPublish After Commit
When firesThe moment EventBus.publish() is calledOnly when enclosing transaction commits successfully
If tx rolls backEvent still published — subscribers may act on rolled-back dataEvent NOT published — consistent with data
Set wherePlatform Event definition → Publish BehaviorSame
Use forLogging, monitoring, non-critical notificationsBusiness events tied to data state changes
// Trigger with Publish After Commit PE (correct for data-driven events)
trigger LoanTrigger on Loan__c (after update) {
  List<Loan_Status_Change__e> events = new List<Loan_Status_Change__e>();
  for (Loan__c loan : Trigger.new) {
    if (loan.Status__c != Trigger.oldMap.get(loan.Id).Status__c)
      events.add(new Loan_Status_Change__e(Loan_Id__c=loan.Id, New_Status__c=loan.Status__c));
  }
  EventBus.publish(events); // fires ONLY if update commits — no phantom events
}
How do you subscribe to Platform Events in LWC using empApi?PLATFORM EVENTS▼
import { LightningElement } from 'lwc';
import { subscribe, unsubscribe, onError } from 'lightning/empApi';

export default class LoanStatusMonitor extends LightningElement {
  subscription = {};

  async connectedCallback() {
    // Subscribe — -1 means new events only
    this.subscription = await subscribe('/event/Loan_Status_Change__e', -1, (response) => {
      const payload = response.data.payload;
      this.handleUpdate(payload.Loan_Id__c, payload.New_Status__c);
    });

    onError(error => console.error('Streaming error:', error));
  }

  handleUpdate(loanId, status) {
    // Update UI in real time — no page refresh
    this.dispatchEvent(new CustomEvent('statuschange', { detail: { loanId, status } }));
  }

  disconnectedCallback() {
    unsubscribe(this.subscription, response => console.log('Unsubscribed:', response));
  }
}

Daily volume (Unlimited)500,000 / day

// Always check publish results
List<Loan_Status_Change__e> events = buildEvents(loans);
List<Database.SaveResult> results = EventBus.publish(events);
for (Integer i = 0; i < results.size(); i++) {
  if (!results[i].isSuccess())
    insert new Error_Log__c(
      Message__c = 'Event publish failed: ' + results[i].getErrors()[0].getMessage()
    );
}
How do you use Platform Events to decouple a trigger from complex post-processing?PLATFORM EVENTS▼

Classic pattern: trigger publishes a Platform Event → separate subscriber trigger handles downstream work. The original trigger stays thin and fast.

// Thin trigger — just publishes, no logic
trigger LoanTrigger on Loan__c (after update) {
  List<Loan_Event__e> evts = new List<Loan_Event__e>();
  for (Loan__c l : Trigger.new) {
    if (l.Status__c != Trigger.oldMap.get(l.Id).Status__c)
      evts.add(new Loan_Event__e(Loan_Id__c=l.Id, Status__c=l.Status__c));
  }
  EventBus.publish(evts);
}

// Email observer trigger — independent, can be deployed/removed without touching loan trigger
trigger LoanEmailObserver on Loan_Event__e (after insert) {
  LoanEmailService.processEvents(Trigger.new);
}

// LOS sync observer trigger — another independent subscriber
trigger LoanLOSObserver on Loan_Event__e (after insert) {
  for (Loan_Event__e e : Trigger.new)
    System.enqueueJob(new LOSSyncQueueable(e.Loan_Id__c, e.Status__c));
}
What is the Streaming API — what are all its event types?PLATFORM EVENTS▼
  • PushTopic Events (legacy) — define a SOQL query, subscribers notified when matching records change. Being replaced by CDC.
  • Generic Streaming Events — lightweight, schema-less JSON messages. No retention. Good for simple real-time notifications.
  • Platform Events — schematized, 72-hour retention, high volume. Subscribe via Apex trigger, Flow, LWC empApi, or external CometD client.
  • Change Data Capture (CDC) — auto-generated per object. 3-day retention. Best for external system data sync.
  • Monitoring Events (Event Monitoring) — read-only. LoginEvent, ApiEvent, ListViewEvent for security audit/monitoring.

// Testing publishing — verify events were enqueued
