Security & Sharing Model — Interview Questions
18 questions — from OWD fundamentals to Apex managed sharing and FLS enforcement
Explain the Salesforce security model layers from top to bottom SECURITY ▼

Salesforce security is layered — each layer controls a different level of access:

  • Organization Level: Login hours, IP restrictions, password policies, 2FA settings
  • Object Level (CRUD): Profiles and Permission Sets control Create, Read, Edit, Delete per object
  • Field Level (FLS): Profiles and Permission Sets control visibility and editability of individual fields
  • Record Level (Sharing): OWD, Role Hierarchy, Sharing Rules, Manual Sharing, Apex Sharing control which records a user can see

Important rule: These layers work together — a user must pass ALL layers. Even if sharing rules give record access, FLS still controls which fields are visible. Even if CRUD gives object access, OWD may restrict which records.

How to remember it

Think of it as security gates: Org gate → Object gate → Field gate → Record gate. A user must pass through all gates to see a specific field value on a specific record.

What is OWD? Explain each setting and when you use it. SECURITY ▼

OWD (Organization-Wide Default) is the baseline record access level for every object. It sets the most restrictive access level, and you then open up access using Role Hierarchy, Sharing Rules, or Manual Sharing.

OWD SettingWhat It MeansUse Case
PrivateOnly record owner (and users above in role hierarchy) can see/editLoan applications — only assigned loan officer should see their loans
Public Read OnlyEveryone can read, only owner can editRate sheets, product catalog that all can see but only admins edit
Public Read/WriteEveryone can read and editShared lookup tables, common reference data
Controlled by ParentInherits access from parent objectContact inheriting Account's sharing — if you can see Account, you can see its Contacts
Key principle

Always set OWD to the most restrictive setting that makes sense for the object, then use sharing rules and role hierarchy to open up access. Never start with Public and try to restrict down — that's architecturally wrong and leads to data exposure.

What is with sharing, without sharing, inherited sharing — explain with code SECURITY ▼
KeywordBehaviorWhen to Use
with sharingEnforces the running user's sharing rules in SOQL/DML — user only sees records they have access toDefault for all service classes — always use this unless there's a reason not to
without sharingRuns in system mode — ignores sharing rules, user sees all recordsBatch jobs, system processes, when you explicitly need access beyond user's permissions
inherited sharingInherits sharing context from the calling classUtility/helper classes that should adapt to their caller's sharing context
(no keyword)Defaults to without sharing for classes called from system context (triggers, batch)Avoid — be explicit about sharing intent
// Service class - always explicitly declare sharing
public with sharing class LoanService {
    // Enforces user's sharing - borrowers can't see other borrowers' loans
    public static List<Loan__c> getUserLoans() {
        return [SELECT Id, LoanAmount__c, Status__c FROM Loan__c];
        // Only returns loans this user can see based on OWD + sharing rules
    }
}

// Batch job - needs system-level access to process all records
public without sharing class LoanInterestBatch implements Database.Batchable<SObject> {
    // Processes ALL loans regardless of ownership - needed for system batch
}

// Utility class - adapts to caller's sharing
public inherited sharing class ValidationUtils {
    // If called from with sharing context, respects sharing
    // If called from without sharing context, runs in system mode
    public static Boolean isValidLoanAmount(Decimal amount) {
        return amount > 0 && amount <= 5000000;
    }
}
How do you enforce FLS in Apex? What is stripInaccessible? SECURITY ▼ 
// Method 1: Manual FLS check before DML
public static void updateLoanStatus(Loan__c loan) {
    Schema.SObjectField statusField = Loan__c.SObjectType.getDescribe()
                                       .fields.getMap().get('Status__c');
    if(!statusField.getDescribe().isUpdateable()) {
        throw new SecurityException('You do not have permission to update Status field');
    }
    update loan;
}

// Method 2: stripInaccessible - BEST for bulk operations
// Automatically removes fields the user cannot access - no exception thrown
public static void safeInsertContacts(List<Contact> contacts) {
    // Before DML - strips fields user cannot edit
    SObjectAccessDecision decision = Security.stripInaccessible(
        AccessType.CREATABLE,  // CREATABLE, UPDATABLE, READABLE, UPSERTABLE
        contacts
    );
    insert decision.getRecords(); // only accessible fields are set
}

// Method 3: WITH USER_MODE in SOQL (clean, modern)
List<Loan__c> loans = [
    SELECT Id, LoanAmount__c, SSN__c 
    FROM Loan__c 
    WITH USER_MODE  // silently excludes fields user cannot see
];

// CRUD check before DML
if(!Loan__c.sObjectType.getDescribe().isCreateable()) {
    throw new SecurityException('No permission to create Loan records');
}
When to use stripInaccessible

Use stripInaccessible when displaying or inserting records from user input — like when saving a form submitted by a Community user. It silently cleans the data rather than throwing errors, making for a better user experience while maintaining security.

Design Patterns in Apex
Senior devs are expected to write maintainable, scalable code using established patterns
Explain the Strategy Pattern in Apex — show code with a real Salesforce example PATTERNS ▼

The Strategy Pattern defines a family of algorithms, encapsulates each one, and makes them interchangeable. The client (caller) works with an interface and doesn't know which concrete implementation it's using.

// Real example: Different loan types have different approval algorithms

// 1. Interface defines the contract
public interface ILoanApprovalStrategy {
    Boolean isApproved(Loan__c loan, CreditReport creditReport);
    String getRejectionReason();
}

// 2. Concrete strategies - one per loan type
public class ConventionalLoanStrategy implements ILoanApprovalStrategy {
    public Boolean isApproved(Loan__c loan, CreditReport cr) {
        return cr.ficoScore >= 620 && loan.DTI__c <= 43 && loan.LTV__c <= 80;
    }
    public String getRejectionReason() { return 'Conventional: FICO min 620, DTI max 43%, LTV max 80%'; }
}

public class FHALoanStrategy implements ILoanApprovalStrategy {
    public Boolean isApproved(Loan__c loan, CreditReport cr) {
        return cr.ficoScore >= 580 && loan.DTI__c <= 57 && loan.LTV__c <= 96.5;
    }
    public String getRejectionReason() { return 'FHA: FICO min 580, DTI max 57%, LTV max 96.5%'; }
}

// 3. Factory to create the right strategy (often combined with Strategy)
public class LoanStrategyFactory {
    public static ILoanApprovalStrategy getStrategy(String loanType) {
        switch on loanType {
            when 'Conventional' { return new ConventionalLoanStrategy(); }
            when 'FHA'          { return new FHALoanStrategy(); }
            when else          { throw new IllegalArgumentException('Unknown loan type: ' + loanType); }
        }
    }
}

// 4. Client code - doesn't know which strategy is used
public class LoanApprovalService {
    public static void processApprovals(List<Loan__c> loans) {
        for(Loan__c loan : loans) {
            ILoanApprovalStrategy strategy = LoanStrategyFactory.getStrategy(loan.LoanType__c);
            CreditReport cr = ExperianService.getCreditReport(loan.BorrowerId__c);
            
            if(strategy.isApproved(loan, cr)) {
                loan.Status__c = 'Approved';
            } else {
                loan.Status__c = 'Denied';
                loan.DenialReason__c = strategy.getRejectionReason();
            }
        }
        update loans;
    }
}
Why this is powerful

To add a new loan type (VA loan, USDA loan), you only create a new class implementing ILoanApprovalStrategy. You don't touch the existing service code at all — Open/Closed Principle. This is what separates senior dev thinking from mid-level.

What is the Service Layer pattern — why is it the most important pattern in Salesforce?PATTERNS▼

Service Layer is where business logic lives. Without it, logic gets duplicated across triggers, LWC controllers, batch jobs, and APIs.

// WITHOUT service layer — same logic duplicated everywhere
// In trigger after update: sendApprovalEmail(loan); updateBorrower(loan);
// In LWC @AuraEnabled method: same calls duplicated!
// In batch finish: same calls duplicated again!

// WITH service layer — one place, called from anywhere
public with sharing class LoanApprovalService {
  public static void approve(List<Loan__c> loans) {
    List<Loan__c> toUpdate = new List<Loan__c>();
    for (Loan__c l : loans) {
      l.Status__c = 'Approved'; l.Approval_Date__c = Date.today(); toUpdate.add(l);
    }
    update toUpdate;
    LoanEmailService.sendApprovalEmails(toUpdate);
    BorrowerService.updateActiveLoanCounts(toUpdate);
  }
}
// Trigger calls: LoanApprovalService.approve(changedLoans);
// LWC @AuraEnabled calls: LoanApprovalService.approve(new List<Loan__c>{loan});
// Batch calls: LoanApprovalService.approve(scope); — SAME service, no duplication
What is the Trigger Handler pattern — complete implementation with framework?PATTERNS▼
// TriggerHandler.cls — virtual base (framework layer)
public virtual class TriggerHandler {
  public virtual void onBeforeInsert() {} public virtual void onAfterInsert() {}
  public virtual void onBeforeUpdate() {} public virtual void onAfterUpdate() {}
  public virtual void onBeforeDelete() {} public virtual void onAfterDelete() {}
  public void run() {
    if (Trigger.isBefore) {
      if (Trigger.isInsert) onBeforeInsert();
      if (Trigger.isUpdate) onBeforeUpdate();
      if (Trigger.isDelete) onBeforeDelete();
    } else {
      if (Trigger.isInsert) onAfterInsert();
      if (Trigger.isUpdate) onAfterUpdate();
      if (Trigger.isDelete) onAfterDelete();
    }
  }
}

// LoanTriggerHandler.cls — domain-specific logic
public class LoanTriggerHandler extends TriggerHandler {
  private List<Loan__c> newLoans  = (List<Loan__c>) Trigger.new;
  private Map<Id,Loan__c> oldMap  = (Map<Id,Loan__c>) Trigger.oldMap;
  public override void onBeforeInsert() { LoanValidationService.validate(newLoans); }
  public override void onAfterUpdate()  { LoanStatusService.handleChange(newLoans, oldMap); }
}

// LoanTrigger.trigger — one line
trigger LoanTrigger on Loan__c (before insert, before update, after insert, after update, after delete) {
  new LoanTriggerHandler().run();
}
What is the Strategy pattern — complete Salesforce implementation?PATTERNS▼
public interface ILoanProcessor {
  void validate(Loan__c loan);
  Decimal calculateRate(Loan__c loan);
  List<String> requiredDocs(Loan__c loan);
}
public class ConventionalProcessor implements ILoanProcessor {
  public void validate(Loan__c l)  { if (l.Credit_Score__c < 620) l.addError('Need 620+ score'); }
  public Decimal calculateRate(Loan__c l) { return 6.5 + (l.Credit_Score__c < 740 ? 0.5 : 0.0); }
  public List<String> requiredDocs(Loan__c l) { return new List<String>{'W2','Tax Return','Bank Statement'}; }
}
public class FHAProcessor implements ILoanProcessor {
  public void validate(Loan__c l)  { if (l.Credit_Score__c < 580) l.addError('FHA needs 580+ score'); }
  public Decimal calculateRate(Loan__c l) { return 6.25; }
  public List<String> requiredDocs(Loan__c l) { return new List<String>{'W2','FHA Certificate'}; }
}
// Factory + Strategy combined
public class LoanProcessorFactory {
  static Map<String,ILoanProcessor> MAP = new Map<String,ILoanProcessor>{
    'Conventional'=>new ConventionalProcessor(), 'FHA'=>new FHAProcessor()
  };
  public static ILoanProcessor get(String type) {
    if (!MAP.containsKey(type)) throw new LoanException('Unknown type: '+type);
    return MAP.get(type);
  }
}
// Usage — caller never knows which processor it's using
ILoanProcessor proc = LoanProcessorFactory.get(loan.Loan_Type__c);
proc.validate(loan);
loan.Interest_Rate__c = proc.calculateRate(loan);
What is the Observer pattern — how does it map to Platform Events in Salesforce?PATTERNS▼

Observer defines a one-to-many relationship: when subject changes, all observers are notified. Platform Events are the native Salesforce implementation.

// Subject (publisher) — just publishes. Knows nothing about observers.
trigger LoanTrigger on Loan__c (after update) {
  List<Loan_Event__e> events = new List<Loan_Event__e>();
  for (Loan__c l : Trigger.new)
    if (l.Status__c != Trigger.oldMap.get(l.Id).Status__c)
      events.add(new Loan_Event__e(Loan_Id__c=l.Id, Status__c=l.Status__c));
  EventBus.publish(events);
}

// Observer 1 — email notifications (independent, can be deployed/removed independently)
trigger EmailObserver on Loan_Event__e (after insert) { LoanEmailService.process(Trigger.new); }

// Observer 2 — LOS sync (independent)
trigger LOSObserver on Loan_Event__e (after insert) {
  for (Loan_Event__e e : Trigger.new) System.enqueueJob(new LOSSyncQueueable(e.Loan_Id__c));
}
// Observer 3 — analytics (Flow subscriber — no code at all)
// Adding a new observer requires ZERO changes to the trigger (open/closed principle)
What is the Decorator pattern in Apex — when is it useful?PATTERNS▼
public interface IEmailService { void sendApproval(Loan__c loan); }

// Base
public class BasicEmailService implements IEmailService {
  public void sendApproval(Loan__c loan) { /* send basic email */ }
}
// Decorator 1 — adds logging without changing base
public class LoggingEmailDecorator implements IEmailService {
  private IEmailService inner;
  public LoggingEmailDecorator(IEmailService s) { inner = s; }
  public void sendApproval(Loan__c loan) {
    insert new Email_Log__c(Loan__c=loan.Id, Sent_At__c=DateTime.now());
    inner.sendApproval(loan); // delegate
  }
}
// Decorator 2 — adds CC to loan officer
public class LoanOfficerCCDecorator implements IEmailService {
  private IEmailService inner;
  public LoanOfficerCCDecorator(IEmailService s) { inner = s; }
  public void sendApproval(Loan__c loan) {
    inner.sendApproval(loan);
    sendLOCopy(loan); // additional behaviour
  }
}
// Compose: wraps base with multiple decorators
IEmailService service = new LoanOfficerCCDecorator(new LoggingEmailDecorator(new BasicEmailService()));
service.sendApproval(loan); // all three layers execute
What is the Facade pattern — how does it simplify complex Salesforce orchestration?PATTERNS▼
// WITHOUT Facade — LWC controller knows too much about internals:
// CreditService.run(); OfacService.run(); DocService.generate(); NotifService.send(); LOSService.sync();

// WITH Facade — single clean method hides all complexity
public class LoanApplicationFacade {
  @AuraEnabled
  public static SubmitResult submit(Id loanId) {
    try {
      CreditService.runCreditCheck(loanId);
      OfacService.runOfacCheck(loanId);
      DocumentService.generateDocs(loanId);
      NotificationService.notifyUnderwriter(loanId);
      System.enqueueJob(new LOSSyncQueueable(loanId));
      AuditService.log('LOAN_SUBMITTED', loanId);
      return new SubmitResult(true, 'Application submitted');
    } catch (Exception e) { return new SubmitResult(false, e.getMessage()); }
  }
}
// LWC calls: await submitLoan({ loanId }); — one call, complete orchestration hidden
What is the Singleton pattern in Apex — when does it save governor limits?PATTERNS▼
// Without Singleton — each call to getConfig() runs a SOQL query
// In a trigger processing 200 records: 200 calls = 200 SOQL — hits the limit!

// With Singleton — query happens once, result cached for the transaction
public class LoanConfigCache {
  private static LoanConfigCache instance;
  private Map<String, Loan_Config__mdt> cache;

  private LoanConfigCache() { // private — nobody can call new LoanConfigCache()
    cache = new Map<String, Loan_Config__mdt>();
    for (Loan_Config__mdt cfg : [SELECT Loan_Type__c, Max_Amount__c, Base_Rate__c FROM Loan_Config__mdt])
      cache.put(cfg.Loan_Type__c, cfg);
  }

  public static LoanConfigCache getInstance() {
    if (instance == null) instance = new LoanConfigCache(); // 1 SOQL total
    return instance;
  }

  public Loan_Config__mdt getConfig(String loanType) { return cache.get(loanType); }
}

// Trigger handler — 200 records, 1 SOQL total
for (Loan__c loan : Trigger.new) {
  Loan_Config__mdt cfg = LoanConfigCache.getInstance().getConfig(loan.Loan_Type__c);
  loan.Max_Rate__c = cfg.Base_Rate__c + 2.0;
}
What is the Unit of Work pattern — how does it reduce DML statements?PATTERNS▼

Unit of Work collects all pending DML operations across a business process and executes them in one coordinated batch at the end — minimising DML statements and ensuring transactional integrity.

// Without UoW — scattered DML (3 separate statements)
public static void approveLoan(Id loanId) {
  Loan__c l = [SELECT Id FROM Loan__c WHERE Id=:loanId];
  l.Status__c='Approved'; update l; // DML 1
  Account a = [SELECT Id, Active_Loans__c FROM Account WHERE Id=:l.Account__c];
  a.Active_Loans__c++; update a; // DML 2
  insert new Approval_Log__c(Loan__c=l.Id); // DML 3
}

// With UoW pattern (manual implementation without fflib)
public class UnitOfWork {
  List<SObject> toInsert = new List<SObject>();
  List<SObject> toUpdate = new List<SObject>();
  List<SObject> toDelete = new List<SObject>();
  public void registerNew(SObject obj)    { toInsert.add(obj); }
  public void registerDirty(SObject obj)  { toUpdate.add(obj); }
  public void registerDeleted(SObject obj){ toDelete.add(obj); }
  public void commitWork() {
    if (!toInsert.isEmpty()) insert toInsert; // 1 DML
    if (!toUpdate.isEmpty()) update toUpdate; // 1 DML
    if (!toDelete.isEmpty()) delete toDelete; // 1 DML
  }
}
UnitOfWork uow = new UnitOfWork();
uow.registerDirty(loan); uow.registerDirty(account); uow.registerNew(approvalLog);
uow.commitWork(); // 2 DML statements instead of 3
What is the Null Object pattern — how does it eliminate defensive null checks?PATTERNS▼
// WITHOUT null object — null checks everywhere
ILoanProcessor proc = LoanProcessorFactory.get(loan.Loan_Type__c); // might return null
if (proc != null) { proc.validate(loan); proc.calculateRate(loan); }
else { /* default handling duplicated everywhere */ }

// WITH null object — factory NEVER returns null
public class NullLoanProcessor implements ILoanProcessor {
  public void validate(Loan__c l) {} // no-op — does nothing safely
  public Decimal calculateRate(Loan__c l) { return 0; }
  public List<String> requiredDocs(Loan__c l) { return new List<String>(); }
}
public class LoanProcessorFactory {
  public static ILoanProcessor get(String type) {
    Map<String,ILoanProcessor> m = getMap();
    return m.containsKey(type) ? m.get(type) : new NullLoanProcessor(); // never null
  }
}
// Calling code — no null check needed ever
ILoanProcessor proc = LoanProcessorFactory.get(loan.Loan_Type__c);
proc.validate(loan); // safe — worst case is a no-op
What is Separation of Concerns — how do you apply it across Apex layers?PATTERNS▼

Every class should have one reason to change. In Salesforce, the layers are:

  • Trigger — dispatcher only. One line: new Handler().run(). No business logic.
  • Trigger Handler — routes trigger events to the right service methods. No SOQL, no business rules.
  • Service Layer — business logic. Orchestrates domain operations. Called from trigger handler, LWC controller, batch, API.
  • Selector Layer — all SOQL queries for an object. No business logic, only queries.
  • Domain Layer — SObject-specific business rules (validation, defaults, derivations).
  • Integration Layer — HTTP callouts, JSON mapping, external system communication.
  • Controller Layer — @AuraEnabled methods. Thin — calls Service layer, returns results to LWC.
Why it matters

When Loan approval logic changes, you change LoanApprovalService only. Not the trigger, not the LWC controller, not the batch. Without SoC, one business rule change requires hunting through 10 files.

US Mortgage Domain Knowledge
Your biggest differentiator — most Salesforce devs don't know this. Use it in every interview.

Loan Lifecycle — Full Flow

1. Pre-Qualification

Borrower provides basic info (income, assets, debt). LO gives informal estimate of loan amount. No credit pull, no commitment. Quick assessment — usually same day.

2. Loan Application (1003)

Borrower completes Uniform Residential Loan Application (Form 1003). Credit is pulled here (hard inquiry). TRID clock starts — Loan Estimate must be delivered within 3 business days.

3. Loan Processing

Processor collects all documentation: W2s, pay stubs, bank statements, tax returns, title report, appraisal ordered. Verifies employment (VOE), assets (VOA), income calculations. Typically 1–2 weeks.

4. Underwriting

Underwriter analyzes the full loan file. Evaluates 3 Cs: Credit (FICO, history), Capacity (DTI, income), Collateral (appraisal, LTV). Issues Conditional Approval or Denial. Most complex stage.

5. Conditional Approval

UW approves with conditions: "Provide updated bank statement", "Explain large deposit", "HOA letter required". Processor works with borrower to satisfy conditions. Loan goes back to UW for final clear.

6. Clear to Close (CTC)

All conditions satisfied. UW gives Clear to Close. Closing Disclosure must be delivered 3 business days before closing date (TRID requirement). CD shows final loan terms.

7. Closing

Borrower signs documents at title company or notary. Typically includes: Note, Deed of Trust, Closing Disclosure. Funds not disbursed yet — right of rescission period for refinances (3 days).

8. Funding

Lender wires funds to title company. Loan is "funded" — money disbursed to seller. Deed recorded at county. Borrower gets keys. Loan is now a live mortgage.

9. Post-Close / Servicing

Loan may be sold to secondary market (Fannie Mae, Freddie Mac). Servicing transferred. Monthly payments begin. Escrow account managed for taxes and insurance.

Critical Compliance Regulations

TRID (TILA-RESPA Integrated Disclosure)

Effective 2015. Combines Truth in Lending Act and Real Estate Settlement Procedures Act disclosures.

  • Loan Estimate (LE): Must deliver within 3 business days of application
  • Closing Disclosure (CD): Must deliver 3 business days before closing
  • Tolerance rules: certain fees cannot increase by more than 10% at closing vs LE
  • In Salesforce: Track LE sent date, CD sent date, calculate closing date restrictions automatically
ECOA (Equal Credit Opportunity Act)

Prohibits discrimination in credit decisions based on race, color, religion, national origin, sex, marital status, age.

  • Adverse Action Notice: Must be sent within 30 days of denial
  • Must state specific reasons for denial — "creditworthiness" not sufficient
  • HMDA data collection feeds into ECOA compliance monitoring
  • In Salesforce: Auto-generate adverse action letter when status = Denied
FCRA (Fair Credit Reporting Act)

Governs how credit information is used and protected.

  • Permissible Purpose: Credit can only be pulled with borrower's signed written authorization
  • Borrower has right to dispute inaccurate info
  • In Salesforce: Store credit pull authorization date, link to signed document
  • Experian, Equifax, TransUnion are CRAs covered by FCRA
HMDA (Home Mortgage Disclosure Act)

Requires lenders to collect and report data on home loan applications to CFPB.

  • LAR (Loan Application Register) filed annually
  • Data includes: race, ethnicity, gender, income, loan type, action taken
  • Purpose: detect discriminatory lending patterns
  • In Salesforce: Custom object or fields to capture all required HMDA data points automatically
BSA/AML + KYC

Bank Secrecy Act / Anti-Money Laundering. Know Your Customer.

  • OFAC Screening: Check borrower against SDN (Specially Designated Nationals) list before funding
  • SAR: Suspicious Activity Report — must file if suspicious transaction detected
  • KYC: Verify identity using government ID, SSN, address verification
  • In Salesforce: OFAC API integration — your Experian/OFAC work applies here
Key Loan Metrics

DTI (Debt-to-Income Ratio): Total monthly debt / gross monthly income. Conventional max: 43-50%. FHA max: 57%.

LTV (Loan-to-Value): Loan amount / appraised value. Conventional max: 80% (without PMI). FHA: 96.5%.

FICO Score: Conventional min: 620. FHA min: 580 (500 with 10% down). VA: 620 typical.

PMI: Private Mortgage Insurance required when LTV > 80% on conventional loans.

Aura Components — Interview Questions
10 questions — know enough to maintain and debug legacy Aura code
What is the difference between LWC and Aura — give 5 key differences AURA vs LWC ▼
FeatureLWC (Lightning Web Components)Aura Components
StandardBased on W3C Web Standards (custom elements, shadow DOM)Proprietary Salesforce framework
PerformanceFaster — less framework overhead, compiled to native browser APIsSlower — more abstraction layers
JavaScriptModern ES6+ — classes, arrow functions, modules, async/awaitOlder ES5 pattern — controller.js, helper.js separation
TestingJest testing — industry standard unit testingNo standard unit test framework
CommunicationCustomEvent, LMS, @apiApplication events, Component events (more complex)
File structure.html, .js, .css, .js-meta.xml.cmp, Controller.js, Helper.js, Style.css, Renderer.js
Data bindingOne-way by default, explicit two-wayTwo-way binding everywhere (can cause performance issues)
Salesforce directionFuture — all new features hereMaintenance mode only — no new features
Interview answer tip

Say: "LWC is based on web standards, which means faster performance and easier debugging with browser dev tools. Aura is a proprietary framework that predates web components. In current projects, I always build in LWC and only touch Aura when maintaining legacy components. Salesforce itself is investing all new features in LWC."

How do you call Apex from an Aura component — complete callback pattern?AURA▼

All Aura Apex calls are asynchronous and use a callback pattern (unlike LWC async/await).

// MyComp.cmp
<aura:component controller="LoanController">
  <aura:attribute name="loans" type="Loan__c[]"/>
  <aura:attribute name="isLoading" type="Boolean" default="false"/>
  <aura:handler name="init" value="{!this}" action="{!c.doInit}"/>
  <aura:iteration items="{!v.loans}" var="loan">
    <p>{!loan.Name}</p>
  </aura:iteration>
</aura:component>

// MyCompController.js
({
  doInit: function(component, event, helper) {
    component.set('v.isLoading', true);
    var action = component.get('c.getLoans');  // c. = Apex controller
    action.setParams({ status: 'Active' });
    action.setCallback(this, function(response) {
      component.set('v.isLoading', false);
      if (response.getState() === 'SUCCESS') {
        component.set('v.loans', response.getReturnValue());
      } else {
        var errors = response.getError();
        console.error('Error:', errors[0].message);
      }
    });
    action.setStorable(); // cache result (like cacheable=true in LWC)
    $A.enqueueAction(action);
  }
})
What are Aura component events vs application events — when to use each?AURA▼
TypeScopeDirectionUse when
Component EventBubbles up through component hierarchyChild → Parent ancestor onlyParent needs to react to child action
Application EventBroadcast to ALL handlers in entire appAny component → any other componentTruly unrelated components need to communicate
// Component Event (preferred — more contained)
// LoanSelectedEvt.evt: <aura:event type="COMPONENT"><aura:attribute name="loanId" type="String"/></aura:event>
// Fire: component.getEvent('loanSelected').setParams({loanId: id}).fire();
// Handle in parent: <aura:handler name="loanSelected" event="c:LoanSelectedEvt" action="{!c.onSelect}"/>

// Application Event (use sparingly)
// <aura:event type="APPLICATION"><aura:attribute name="loanId" type="String"/></aura:event>
// Fire: $A.get('e.c:LoanApprovedEvt').setParams({loanId: id}).fire();
// Handle anywhere: <aura:handler event="c:LoanApprovedEvt" action="{!c.onApproval}"/>
Application events create tight coupling

They fire to every registered handler in the app. In large orgs this makes debugging very hard. Prefer Component Events or migrate to LWC + Lightning Message Service.

What is Aura Locker Service — what restrictions does it impose?AURA▼

Locker Service is Salesforce's JavaScript security sandbox for Aura. It wraps DOM elements in SecureElement proxies to prevent cross-component DOM access and XSS.

  • Cannot use document.querySelector() — blocked. Use component.find('aura:id') instead
  • instanceof checks fail — element instanceof HTMLInputElement returns false (it's a SecureElement). Check tagName instead
  • eval() and Function() blocked — cannot dynamically execute code strings
  • Cross-namespace DOM access blocked — cannot access another component's internal DOM
  • Some browser globals restricted — window, document proxied through Locker
// WRONG — blocked by Locker
var input = document.getElementById('myInput');
if (input instanceof HTMLInputElement) { } // always false

// CORRECT — Aura safe patterns
var input = component.find('myInput'); // find by aura:id
if (input.getElement().tagName === 'INPUT') { } // check tagName not instanceof
How do Aura components communicate with Lightning Web Components?AURA▼

LWC components can be used inside Aura components but the reverse is not true — you cannot use Aura inside LWC.

// Using LWC inside Aura — works natively
<aura:component>
  <!-- c-loan-card is an LWC component -->
  <c-loan-card loan-id="{!v.selectedLoanId}" onloanapproved="{!c.handleApproval}"></c-loan-card>
</aura:component>

// LWC child communicates to Aura parent via Custom Events — Aura listens with on prefix
// LWC fires: this.dispatchEvent(new CustomEvent('loanapproved', { detail: { loanId } }));
// Aura handles: onloanapproved="{!c.handleApproval}"
// handleApproval: function(cmp, event) { var loanId = event.getParam('detail').loanId; }

// Lightning Message Service (LMS) — works for both Aura and LWC
// Best for two-way communication between Aura and LWC components on the same page
Migration strategy

Wrap existing Aura components in LWC wrappers gradually. New features = LWC. Existing Aura = maintain until full replacement. Use LMS as the communication bridge during transition.

What are Aura attributes — types, defaults, access modifiers?AURA▼
<aura:component access="global">
  <!-- Basic types -->
  <aura:attribute name="loanId"    type="String"    default=""/>
  <aura:attribute name="amount"    type="Decimal"   default="0"/>
  <aura:attribute name="isLoading" type="Boolean"   default="false"/>
  <aura:attribute name="loans"     type="Loan__c[]"/>       <!-- SObject list -->
  <aura:attribute name="loan"      type="Loan__c"/>         <!-- Single SObject -->
  <aura:attribute name="config"    type="Map"/>             <!-- Key-value map -->
  <aura:attribute name="callback"  type="Aura.Action"/>     <!-- Pass action as attribute -->

  <!-- Access modifiers -->
  <aura:attribute name="publicProp"  type="String" access="public"/>   <!-- Parent can read/write -->
  <aura:attribute name="globalProp"  type="String" access="global"/>   <!-- Any component / managed pkg -->
  <aura:attribute name="privateProp" type="String" access="private"/>  <!-- This component only -->
</aura:component>

// In controller: read/write attributes
component.get('v.loanId');           // read
component.set('v.isLoading', true);  // write
What is the Aura component lifecycle — init, render, rerender?AURA▼
EventWhenUse for
initComponent created, attributes set, not yet in DOMInitialize data, make first Apex call
renderReturns markup — override only for complex DOM manipulationRarely overridden
afterRenderComponent in DOM after initial renderAccess DOM elements, init third-party JS
rerenderCalled on every re-render (attribute change)Update third-party libraries on data change
unrenderComponent destroyedCleanup — timers, listeners, subscriptions
// Aura handler declarations in .cmp
<aura:handler name="init" value="{!this}" action="{!c.doInit}"/>
<aura:handler name="render" value="{!this}" action="{!c.doAfterRender}"/>
<aura:handler name="destroy" value="{!this}" action="{!c.doCleanup}"/>
// Note: in LWC these are connectedCallback, renderedCallback, disconnectedCallback
How do you use $A.createComponent() for dynamic component creation?AURA▼
// Dynamically create a component at runtime (no LWC equivalent — Aura-only)
({
  createLoanModal: function(component, event, helper) {
    $A.createComponent(
      'c:LoanApprovalModal',  // component type
      {                        // attribute values
        'loanId': component.get('v.selectedLoanId'),
        'onClose': component.getReference('c.handleModalClose')
      },
      function(newCmp, status, errorMessage) {
        if (status === 'SUCCESS') {
          var body = component.get('v.body');
          body.push(newCmp);
          component.set('v.body', body); // add to component body
        } else {
          console.error('Component creation failed:', errorMessage);
        }
      }
    );
  }
})
When used

$A.createComponent is used for modal dialogs, dynamic content areas, and wizard steps where the component type is determined at runtime. In LWC, use conditional templates (if:true) or dynamic imports instead.

What is Aura helper — how does it differ from controller?AURA▼

Every Aura component has a Controller (event handlers, bound to markup) and a Helper (shared utility functions). The Helper is a singleton — one instance shared across all instances of the component.

// Controller — handles events from markup, delegates to helper
({
  handleSave: function(component, event, helper) {
    helper.saveRecord(component, function(result) {
      component.set('v.message', 'Saved: ' + result.Id);
    });
  }
})

// Helper — reusable utility methods, shared across all component instances
({
  saveRecord: function(component, callback) {
    var action = component.get('c.saveLoan');
    action.setParams({ loan: component.get('v.loan') });
    action.setCallback(this, function(response) {
      if (response.getState() === 'SUCCESS') callback(response.getReturnValue());
    });
    $A.enqueueAction(action);
  },
  validateForm: function(component) {
    var amount = component.get('v.loan').Amount__c;
    return amount > 0 && amount <= 10000000;
  }
})
Best practice

Keep Controller functions thin — they just call Helper methods. This makes Helper functions easily testable and reusable.

How do you handle errors in Aura — force:showToast and error events?AURA▼
// Show toast notification
({
  showSuccess: function(component) {
    var toastEvent = $A.get('e.force:showToast');
    toastEvent.setParams({
      title: 'Success',
      message: 'Loan approved successfully.',
      type: 'success',   // success, error, warning, info
      mode: 'dismissible' // dismissible, sticky, pester
    });
    toastEvent.fire();
  },

  handleError: function(component, error) {
    var toastEvent = $A.get('e.force:showToast');
    toastEvent.setParams({
      title: 'Error',
      message: error.message || 'An unexpected error occurred.',
      type: 'error',
      mode: 'sticky'
    });
    toastEvent.fire();
  },

  // Handle Apex error in callback
  handleApexResponse: function(component, response) {
    if (response.getState() === 'ERROR') {
      var errors = response.getError();
      var message = (errors && errors[0] && errors[0].message) ? errors[0].message : 'Unknown error';
      this.handleError(component, { message: message });
    }
  }
})
Behavioral Interview — STAR Method Stories
12 complete stories using YOUR real project experience. Read, then tell each story out loud from memory.
Tell me about yourself — 2-minute introduction OPENING — MOST IMPORTANT
Script — Read Aloud Until Memorized
"Thank you for this opportunity. My name is Sunil, and I'm a Salesforce Developer with four years of experience, currently based in Pune, Maharashtra. My expertise is concentrated in the US mortgage and financial services domain — which I believe makes me a strong fit for roles that involve complex, regulated business processes.

Professionally, I've worked both as a solo end-to-end developer and as a Senior Developer in team environments. In my most recent role at Inflooens India Operations, I was involved in building mortgage origination systems from the ground up — covering everything from custom Apex triggers and Lightning Web Components to third-party integrations with DocuSign for document generation, Experian for credit bureau pulls, and OFAC screening systems for regulatory compliance.

One of my most challenging and rewarding projects was a solo-built Loan Officer Campaign Platform where I integrated OpenAI GPT-4o for AI-driven content, the FRED API for economic data, and Freddie Mac's rate service — all within a single Salesforce org. That experience gave me deep confidence in end-to-end development and complex API architecture.

I'm now looking for a Senior Salesforce Developer role where I can bring this domain expertise and technical depth to help a team build scalable, compliant financial systems. I'm excited about this particular opportunity because..."
Practice Notes
Time yourself — this should be 1:45 to 2:15 minutes. Not shorter, not longer. Practice it 10 times until it sounds natural, not memorized. End with a genuine reason why you want THEIR specific role — do company research before every interview.
Tell me about the most complex project you've built PROJECT STORY
Situation
Our mortgage company needed loan officers to run targeted email campaigns to prospective borrowers, but they had no way to personalize content based on current market conditions like interest rates. The manual process was completely disconnected from real-time data.
Task
I was assigned as the sole developer to design and build this platform end-to-end — from architecture decisions to deployment. No other developer. I had to integrate multiple external APIs, build a full LWC UI, and ensure the system worked within Salesforce's governor limits at scale.
Action
I architected the platform using a service layer pattern to keep the integration logic completely separated from the Salesforce data layer. I integrated four external APIs: Resend for email delivery because it had better deliverability and a clean API compared to Salesforce's built-in email; OpenAI GPT-4o for AI-generated personalized email content based on borrower profile and loan type; the FRED API from the Federal Reserve to pull live economic indicators like unemployment and inflation data; and Freddie Mac's rate service for real-time mortgage rate feeds. I used Named Credentials for all authentication, Queueable Apex for async email processing to stay within callout limits, and built the entire front-end in LWC with a real-time rate dashboard and campaign builder interface. I wrote comprehensive test classes achieving 90% coverage.
Result
Impact
The platform launched on schedule. Loan officers could create and send personalized campaigns in 10 minutes compared to hours previously. Email open rates improved by around 35% due to AI-generated personalized content. I delivered this entirely solo — demonstrating ownership, architecture skills, and technical depth across the full Salesforce stack.
Describe the most complex integration you worked on INTEGRATION STORY — Mortgage Platform
Situation
In the Mortgage Origination Platform, we needed to integrate Experian's credit bureau API to automate the credit pull process. Previously, loan officers manually called Experian, received results in a PDF, and manually entered FICO scores into the system — taking 20–30 minutes per application and introducing data entry errors.
Task
As the senior developer on the integration module, I was responsible for designing and implementing the Experian integration — handling authentication, data mapping, error handling, FCRA compliance (we could only pull with signed authorization), and the UI for displaying results.
Action
I first worked with the business to map all the data fields we needed from Experian to our custom Salesforce objects. I set up Named Credentials for OAuth 2.0 authentication so credentials were never in code. I built the Apex service class using a Queueable job because we needed to make callouts after trigger DML. I implemented proper error handling with retry logic for rate limit errors (HTTP 429) and stored raw JSON responses in a related log object for audit purposes. For FCRA compliance, I added a check that only allowed credit pulls if a signed credit authorization document was already attached to the loan application — enforced in both Apex and in the LWC UI. I wrote HttpCalloutMock test classes achieving 95% coverage on the integration service.
Result
Impact
Credit pull time reduced from 25 minutes to under 30 seconds. Data entry errors were eliminated. The FCRA compliance check prevented unauthorized pulls, reducing compliance risk significantly. The integration also served as the template for our OFAC screening integration that followed.
Tell me about a time you debugged a critical production issue PROBLEM SOLVING
Situation
In production, loan officers started reporting that some loans were getting stuck in "Processing" status and not automatically advancing to "Underwriting" after all required documents were attached. This was happening intermittently — about 15-20% of qualifying loans were affected. This was critical because delayed processing meant delayed closings, which upset borrowers and could cause regulatory issues.
Task
I needed to identify root cause quickly — the business was escalating the issue. I had to diagnose a bug that was intermittent, in a trigger that ran on file attachment, in a production org I couldn't deploy to without change management approval.
Action
First, I checked the Salesforce Debug Logs for the affected loan IDs — I found several with a "governor limit exception" for SOQL queries but it was being swallowed by an empty catch block. The trigger was counting document types attached to the loan, but the query was inside a for loop — so when 6 or more documents were attached at once (which happened when users did bulk uploads), the 100 SOQL limit was hit and the exception was silently caught and ignored. I fixed the bug in sandbox by refactoring to collect all Loan IDs first, then use a single aggregate SOQL query to count documents per loan. I also removed the empty catch block and replaced it with proper error logging to a custom Error Log object. I created a quick emergency fix and worked with the team to get it deployed through a hotfix process. I also wrote a data fix script to reprocess the stuck loans.
Result
Impact
Bug was identified within 2 hours and fixed within 4 hours. 47 stuck loans were reprocessed. I added a code review rule to the team: never empty catch blocks, always test with 200 records in bulk. This incident became a learning session I led for the team on governor limits and bulkification.
Tell me about a time you disagreed with a technical decision CONFLICT / TECHNICAL LEADERSHIP
Situation
During the Mortgage Origination Platform project, the team lead proposed using Aura components for all new UI development because the team was more familiar with Aura at the time. I believed this was the wrong direction given where Salesforce was heading with LWC.
Task
I needed to raise my concern constructively without creating conflict — the team lead had more experience than me overall. I had to be persuasive with data, not just opinion.
Action
I prepared a comparison document showing: LWC performance benchmarks, Salesforce's official roadmap indicating LWC is the future (no new features in Aura), the fact that LWC has standard Jest testing while Aura has no unit test framework, and a realistic estimate that the team could learn LWC basics in 2 weeks. I presented this calmly in our next sprint planning, framing it as a risk discussion rather than a criticism of the existing decision. I also offered to build the first component in LWC and document the patterns so the team could follow — removing the "unknown" risk they were worried about.
Result
Impact
The team agreed to move to LWC for all new components. I built the first three components and wrote a team style guide for LWC patterns. Within a month, the entire team was comfortable in LWC and appreciated not being locked into legacy technology. The project's UI components ended up much more performant than if we had used Aura.
Tell me about a time you worked under pressure / tight deadline PRESSURE / DELIVERY
Situation
During the Loan Officer Campaign Platform project, a regulatory deadline meant the system had to be live before a specific date — our original timeline was cut by 3 weeks due to a business decision I had no control over. I was the only developer on this project.
Task
I had to re-scope the delivery to meet the hard deadline without compromising the core functionality or cutting corners on code quality and testing that would hurt us in production later.
Action
I immediately created a prioritization matrix — must-have vs nice-to-have features. I identified that the AI content generation and FRED API integration were phase 2 features that could launch after go-live without blocking the core campaign functionality. I communicated this clearly to the product owner with specific delivery dates for both phases. I then focused exclusively on the core: email sending via Resend API, campaign management UI, and basic rate integration. I worked longer hours for 2 weeks but structured my day to maintain quality — coding in the morning when focused, testing in the afternoon, reviewing my own code every evening.
Result
Impact
Phase 1 launched on the regulatory deadline with zero production bugs. Phase 2 features were delivered 2 weeks after go-live as promised. The structured re-scoping prevented panic delivery of a broken product. The product owner appreciated the transparency about what was achievable.
Tell me about a time you had to learn a complex new technology quickly?BEHAVIORAL▼

Situation: Three months into my project, the client added a requirement to integrate with Experian credit bureau API. I had never worked with credit bureau APIs or FCRA compliance requirements.

Task: Design and build a production-grade credit pull integration in six weeks, including FCRA-compliant data handling, error handling, and retry logic.

Action: I broke it into three tracks. First, read Experian documentation end-to-end and built a proof-of-concept callout in Developer Org within week one. Second, studied FCRA requirements and documented how our implementation needed to comply. Third, called two Salesforce partners who had done credit bureau integrations to learn their architecture decisions.

Result: Integration went live week five — one week early. Handles 200+ credit pulls daily with 99.2% success rate. The FCRA approach I designed became the standard for all subsequent integrations.

Describe a time you disagreed with a senior technical decision?BEHAVIORAL▼

Situation: During architecture review, the tech lead proposed using Workflow Rules and polling every 5 minutes for the LOS sync. I believed Platform Events would be significantly better.

Task: Make the case diplomatically without undermining the tech lead.

Action: I asked for 20 minutes in the next design session. I prepared a comparison: the polling approach would execute 288 scheduled jobs per day and make 288 API calls regardless of whether data changed. Platform Events would only trigger when data changed and scale from 10 to 10,000 loan changes per day without architecture change. I framed it as considering all options, not criticising.

Result: Tech lead agreed to pilot the Platform Event approach. We adopted it for production. I learned that HOW you raise a disagreement matters as much as whether you are technically right.

Tell me about the most complex bug you debugged?BEHAVIORAL▼

Situation: In production, loan status intermittently did not update even though LWC showed Save Successful. Happened 3% of the time — no reproducible steps.

Action: I ruled out obvious causes — no failed DML, no validation failures, no sharing issues. The 3% rate correlated with concurrent edits — two users updating the same record within seconds. Found that our static variable recursion guard was set to true even when the first trigger invocation failed, and was never reset in a finally block. When User B saved immediately after, the guard was still true and the handler exited early.

Result: After resetting the flag in a finally block, the failure rate dropped to zero over 30 days. Lesson: intermittent bugs almost always have a concurrency or state management root cause.

Describe a time you delivered difficult news to a client?BEHAVIORAL▼

Situation: Two weeks before go-live, I found a logic error in pro-rated interest calculations affecting 23 of 180 test records. CFO had already signed off on UAT.

Action: I requested a live call with the project sponsor and CFO the same day. I prepared: exact scope of the problem, root cause, complete fix plan, and two options — delay one week or go-live with documented manual workaround for 23 affected loan types. I led with facts: 'In validation we found a calculation error affecting 12% of records. Here are your two options.'

Result: Client chose the one-week delay. CFO said: 'This is exactly how I want issues raised — facts, impact, options.' We went live one week later with zero calculation issues in production.

Tell me about mentoring a junior developer?BEHAVIORAL▼

Situation: A junior developer was writing trigger logic directly in .trigger files with SOQL inside loops. Their trigger would have hit the governor limit at 50+ records.

Action: Instead of just commenting 'fix the SOQL', I scheduled a 45-minute pairing session. I explained WHY the limit exists — Salesforce multi-tenant architecture means one org's runaway Apex impacts all orgs on the same server. Then we refactored together — I asked questions rather than gave answers: 'What information do we need from Account? Can we get all of it at once?'

Result: In the next sprint, zero bulkification issues in their code reviews. They later became the person on our team who explained bulkification to new joiners. Most effective mentoring asks questions that guide discovery, not gives answers directly.

Describe balancing technical quality with deadline pressure?BEHAVIORAL▼

Situation: On day 8 of a 10-day sprint, I realised my DocuSign polling approach would not handle webhook failures — no retry mechanism.

Action: I assessed the real risk: the polling worked for 95% of cases. Webhook miss was rare and required manual follow-up. I documented the gap clearly: frequency of impact and 3-day effort to fix properly. I brought this to the product owner with two clear options and let them decide — making sure they understood the risk they were accepting, not protecting myself.

Result: Product owner chose to deliver on time. Fixed properly in Sprint 4. The limitation was hit twice before the fix — both handled manually with no customer impact. Technical debt is sometimes the right call, but only when risk is quantified and the stakeholder consciously accepts it.

Tell me about working with legacy code and technical debt?BEHAVIORAL▼

Situation: Joined a project with an Account trigger that was 800 lines of procedural code in the .trigger file — no handler pattern, SOQL scattered throughout, 65% test coverage.

Action: First two days: mapping all automations and dependencies before touching anything. Then wrote tests for existing behaviour to lock in a baseline. For new features, created a new handler class and restructured the trigger to call it — not touching existing logic. Over four sprints, proposed two story points per sprint for debt reduction, framed in business terms: 'This refactoring reduces deployment time from 4 hours to 45 minutes.'

Result: After four sprints: fully converted to handler pattern, test coverage 91%, Account deployment time dropped from 4+ hours to under one hour.

Describe your approach to code reviews — giving and receiving?BEHAVIORAL▼

Giving reviews — my framework:

  • Severity labels: Must Fix (blocker), Should Fix (important), Consider (suggestion). No ambiguity about what is required.
  • Explain why, not just what: Not just 'move SOQL outside the loop' — explain the governor limit consequence that will hit production.
  • Acknowledge what is well done: If someone wrote clean bulkification or good test assertions, say so explicitly.
  • Style is a Consider, not Must Fix: Unless the team has an agreed style guide, personal preferences should never block a PR.

Receiving reviews:

  • Every comment gets a response — Fixed or I disagree because X, let's discuss.
  • Never take feedback personally — the code is reviewed, not me.
  • Disagree with Must Fix? Request a conversation, not a comment thread.
  • After a review, look for patterns in feedback — recurring issues mean something to learn.
Tell me about handling a production incident?BEHAVIORAL▼

Situation: At 9 AM Monday, loan officers reported the LWC loan form was throwing errors for all FHA loans. Production impact — officers could not submit FHA applications.

Action: First 5 minutes: set up debug log, reproduced the error, captured the stack trace. Error: NullPointerException in FHALoanProcessor.calculateRate() — a Custom Metadata deployment from Friday had accidentally wiped the FHA rate configuration row. Fix: added the missing metadata record directly in Setup — no deployment needed for CMDT records. Total time: 23 minutes from first report to resolution.

Result: Loan officers back to full operation in 23 minutes. Post-incident: added metadata validation check to deployment checklist and created Scheduled Apex that verifies required CMDT records exist and alerts admins if any are missing.

Where do you see yourself in 3-5 years — how does this role fit?BEHAVIORAL▼

In 3 to 5 years, I want to be making architecture decisions for complex Salesforce implementations — not just executing them. I want to be the person who can say 'here is why Platform Events serve us better than polling, and here is the risk we accept' with full technical credibility.

This role fits specifically because: the US mortgage domain requires FCRA and RESPA compliance — those constraints force you to build correctly. The senior developer position means real design decisions. And I am working toward the Salesforce Architect certification — the complexity of this work is the fastest path to that knowledge.

Key elements of strong answer

1. Specific — name what you want to do, not vague growth. 2. Connected to THIS role specifically. 3. Ambitious but realistic. 4. Shows long-term commitment.

How do you stay current with Salesforce releases and new features?BEHAVIORAL▼

Salesforce releases 3 major updates per year: Spring (February), Summer (June), Winter (October).

  • Release Notes: Read for every release — focus on Apex API changes, LWC new capabilities, Flow enhancements, deprecated features and their retirement timelines.
  • Pre-release orgs: Available 4-6 weeks before each release. Test existing code against the new release before it hits production.
  • Trailhead release modules: 1-2 new modules per release — fastest way to learn what changed.
  • Certification maintenance: Salesforce certs require annual maintenance exams. These force you to learn what changed.
  • Deprecation tracking: Personal timeline — when does Process Builder retire? When does WITH SECURITY_ENFORCED become wrong? Plan migrations proactively.
Interview answer

Say: I stay current through release notes and the Salesforce Developer Blog, and I test major features in sandbox before they reach production. I also track the Process Builder deprecation timeline because we have existing automations that need migration to Flow — I want to stay ahead of forced retirements rather than scramble at the deadline.

Communication Scripts — Daily Practice
Read each script aloud. Time yourself. Record and replay. Do this every day during the Communication blocks.
// RECRUITER OUTREACH MESSAGE — LinkedIn or Email
Hi [Name],

I came across your profile and noticed you specialize in Salesforce placements. I wanted to reach out directly because I believe my background is a strong match for senior developer roles at companies working in financial services or mortgage tech.

I have four years of Salesforce development experience with deep expertise in Apex, LWC, and REST/SOAP integrations. My most recent work involved building mortgage origination systems — integrating Experian credit bureau, DocuSign, and OFAC screening systems within Salesforce. I've also built an AI-powered campaign platform solo, integrating OpenAI, FRED API, and Freddie Mac rate services.

I'm currently looking for a Senior Salesforce Developer role in Pune or remote. Would you happen to have any openings that might align with this background?

Thank you for your time, and I look forward to connecting.

Best regards,
Sunil
Practice tip: Read this aloud twice. Then write your own version in your own words — more authentic is better. Use this structure but make the language sound natural for you.
// WHEN INTERVIEWER ASKS "WHY ARE YOU LOOKING FOR A NEW ROLE?"
"I've had a great learning experience in my previous role — I was fortunate to work on complex mortgage origination systems and build integrations that genuinely impacted the business. I feel I've grown significantly as a developer there.

At this point, I'm looking for a role where I can take on more ownership, work on larger-scale Salesforce architectures, and continue growing in a strong engineering culture. I'm particularly excited about this role because [specific reason about their company/tech stack]."
Never say: "I left because the management was bad" or "I left because of salary." Keep it forward-looking and positive. Focus on growth, not escape.
// WHEN YOU DON'T KNOW THE ANSWER — How to handle it
"That's a great question. I want to be honest — I haven't worked with that specific feature directly. However, based on my understanding of [related concept I do know], I would expect it to work like... [reason through it]. I would also want to confirm this in the documentation before implementing it. Could you share what your team's experience with it has been?"
Never say "I don't know" and stop. Always: acknowledge → reason through from related knowledge → express willingness to learn → turn it into a conversation.
// WHEN EXPLAINING TECHNICAL CONCEPTS — Clear Communication Template
"Let me explain [concept] in a straightforward way.

[Concept] is basically [one sentence simple explanation]. The reason this exists is [the problem it solves]. In practice, what this means is [concrete example]. In my work, I used this when [real scenario from your project].

Does that make sense, or would you like me to go deeper on any part of that?"
Practice with these examples: Explain governor limits. Explain bulkification. Explain Platform Events. Explain with/without sharing. Use this template for each.
// WHEN ASKED ABOUT SALARY — Negotiation Script
"I'm definitely open to discussing compensation. Before I give a number, could you share what the budgeted range for this role is? That would help me understand what the team is working with.

[If pressed to give a number first:]

Based on my research into current market rates for Senior Salesforce Developers in [Pune/India] with my level of experience in the US mortgage domain, I'd be targeting something in the range of [₹X – ₹Y LPA]. That said, I'm flexible depending on the full compensation package, growth opportunities, and the nature of the role. What does your range look like?"
Research current market: Senior Salesforce Dev in India = ₹15–35 LPA range depending on company type (product vs service), remote vs on-site, and company size. Always anchor with a range, not a single number. Never apologize for your expectation.
// CLOSING THE INTERVIEW — Always End Strong
"I want to say — I've really enjoyed this conversation. Learning more about [something specific they mentioned about the project or team] has made me even more excited about this opportunity. It aligns well with the kind of work I'm passionate about doing.

I do have one question for you: Based on what you've heard today, do you have any concerns about my fit for this role? I want to make sure I've addressed everything that matters to you.

And what are the next steps in your process?"
The "do you have any concerns" question is powerful — it gives you a chance to overcome objections before they exist in the interviewer's head. Most candidates never ask this. It shows confidence and genuine interest.
Vocabulary & Professional Phrases
Learn 3 words per day during the 6:30 AM block. Use each in a sentence before the day ends.

Technical Interview Vocabulary

Scalability
System's ability to handle growing workload without degradation
"I designed the batch job with scalability in mind — it can process millions of records without modification."
Maintainability
How easy code is to understand, modify, and debug in the future
"The trigger handler pattern improves maintainability by separating routing logic from business logic."
Fault-tolerant
System continues functioning even when components fail
"The integration service is fault-tolerant — it retries on rate limit errors and logs failures without crashing."
Idempotent
Operation that produces same result regardless of how many times it runs
"The credit pull logic is idempotent — if triggered twice for the same loan, it won't create duplicate records."
Asynchronous
Running in background, not blocking the main transaction
"I moved the email sending to asynchronous Apex using Queueable to prevent blocking the loan save operation."
Decoupled
Components are independent — changes to one don't require changes to others
"Platform Events decouple the status update logic from the notification logic."
Abstraction
Hiding complex implementation details behind a simpler interface
"Named Credentials abstract the authentication details — the Apex code never sees raw API keys."
Extensible
Designed to be easily expanded with new functionality
"The strategy pattern makes the approval engine extensible — adding a new loan type only requires one new class."
Throughput
Amount of work processed in a given time
"Increasing the batch scope size from 50 to 200 improved throughput significantly."
Latency
Time delay between a request and its response
"The Experian API had high latency, which is why I moved the callout to Queueable Apex."
Regression
When new changes break previously working functionality
"I ran a full regression test suite before deploying the integration to ensure no existing features were broken."
Refactoring
Improving internal code structure without changing external behavior
"I refactored the legacy Aura component to LWC — same functionality, better performance and testability."

Professional English Phrases for Interviews

Starting an answer
Phrases to begin thoughtfully, not nervously
"That's a great question, let me walk you through how I approached this..." / "In my experience with mortgage systems..." / "Let me give you a concrete example from my work..."
Adding detail
Expanding without rambling
"To give you some context..." / "What made this particularly challenging was..." / "The key decision here was..."
Showing ownership
Use "I" not "we" when describing YOUR contribution
"I designed the architecture for..." / "I made the decision to use..." / "I identified the root cause by..."
Showing teamwork
Balance between I and we
"Our team collaborated on..." / "I worked closely with the business analyst to..." / "Together we were able to..."
Quantifying results
Always end with numbers when possible
"This reduced processing time by approximately 40%..." / "We went from 200 manual steps to a fully automated workflow..." / "The system now handles 10,000 records in under 5 minutes..."
Handling silence
Don't rush — thinking time is allowed
"Give me just a moment to think through this..." / "That's an interesting scenario, let me consider the best approach..." / (2–3 seconds silence is completely fine — it signals confidence)

Professional Communication Vocabulary

Articulate
To express clearly and effectively
"I want to articulate why this architecture decision serves us better in the long run."
Proactive
Acting before a problem occurs rather than reacting
"I proactively flagged the potential governor limit issue before it reached production."
Scalable
Able to grow without redesign
"I designed this batch job to be scalable — it handles 1,000 or 1,000,000 records without changes."
Bottleneck
A point that slows or limits an entire system
"I identified the SOQL inside the loop as the performance bottleneck."
Granular
At a detailed, fine-grained level
"The permission sets give us granular control over who can approve jumbo loans."
Pragmatic
Practical and solution-focused
"My approach is pragmatic — I choose the simplest solution that meets the requirements."
Trade-off
Accepting a disadvantage to gain an advantage
"There is a trade-off between flexibility and performance here."
Robust
Strong, reliable, handles failure well
"The retry logic makes the integration robust against transient network failures."
Idempotent
Calling multiple times has same effect as once
"The endpoint is idempotent — replaying a request won't create duplicate records."
Decouple
Remove direct dependencies between components
"Platform Events decouple the trigger from the notification service."
Throughput
Amount of work processed in a given time
"We increased batch throughput from 50 to 200 records per chunk."
Latency
Time delay between a request and its response
"The credit bureau integration has a p95 latency of under 3 seconds."
Idiomatic
Following the natural patterns of a language/platform
"This is idiomatic Apex — it follows Salesforce's recommended patterns."
Leverage
Use something to maximum advantage
"We can leverage Custom Metadata to make this configurable without a deployment."
Iterate
Improve through repeated cycles
"I iterate on the design in sandbox before promoting to production."
Prioritise
Choose what is most important to work on first
"I prioritised the credit bureau integration because it was on the critical path."
Alignment
Agreement between teams or priorities
"I want to make sure there is alignment between the dev team and business on this approach."
Governance
Rules and controls over a system or process
"Our deployment governance requires a code review before any production deploy."
Ownership
Taking full responsibility for an outcome
"I took full ownership of the LOS integration — design, build, testing, and deployment."
Bandwidth
Capacity to take on more work
"Do you have bandwidth to review this design document before the sprint review?"

Salesforce Technical Vocabulary

Bulkification
Writing code that handles 200 records as efficiently as 1 record
"Bulkification is why I never put SOQL or DML inside a for loop."
Idempotency
A safe-to-retry operation that produces the same result each time
"I added an idempotency key to the callout so retries don't create duplicates."
Recursion
When a trigger fires itself, causing an infinite loop
"I use a static boolean flag to prevent trigger recursion on the Account object."
Polymorphism
One interface, multiple implementations
"The ILoanProcessor interface gives us polymorphism across loan types."
Immutable
Cannot be changed after creation
"Custom Metadata records are immutable at runtime — you can read but not write them from triggers."
Hydration
Populating an object with data (especially from a query)
"After insert, the record is hydrated with the auto-generated Id and system fields."
Serialisation
Converting an object to a storable/transmittable format (JSON/XML)
"I serialise the loan object to JSON before sending it to the LOS API."
Pagination
Fetching records in smaller chunks instead of all at once
"I implemented keyset pagination on the datatable to handle 50,000+ records."
Eventual consistency
System reaches correct state after some delay
"Platform Events give us eventual consistency — the LOS sync may lag by seconds."
Circuit breaker
Stops calling a failing service after repeated failures
"The retry logic acts as a circuit breaker — after 3 failures it stops and alerts."
Webhook
An HTTP callback that a remote system calls when something happens
"DocuSign sends a webhook to our REST endpoint when an envelope is completed."
Orchestration
Coordinating multiple services in sequence to achieve a goal
"The loan application facade orchestrates credit check, OFAC, and doc generation."
Fan-out
One event triggering multiple downstream processes simultaneously
"The Platform Event fans out to email, LOS sync, and analytics simultaneously."
Middleware
Software that connects two systems and translates between them
"MuleSoft acts as middleware between Salesforce and the core banking system."
Regression
A previously working feature that breaks after a change
"The 200-record test caught a governor limit regression before it hit production."
Coverage
Percentage of Apex code executed by unit tests
"We maintain 91% code coverage — well above the 75% Salesforce minimum."
