Advanced Apex — Senior Level Questions
18 deeper questions — these come up in final rounds and architecture discussions
What is the difference between Database.Stateful and without Database.Stateful in Batch Apex? When does it matter? ADVANCED APEX▼

By default, each execute() chunk of a batch job runs in a fresh, isolated transaction. All instance variables are reset between chunks. Database.Stateful changes this — instance variables are preserved across all execute() calls for the lifetime of the batch job.

FeatureWithout Stateful (default)With Database.Stateful
Instance variables between chunksReset to null/defaultPreserved
Memory usageLowerHigher (state held in memory)
Use forIndependent chunk processingCounters, error lists, accumulators
Heap limit12 MB per chunk12 MB but state counts toward it
public class LoanReportBatch implements Database.Batchable<SObject>, Database.Stateful {

    // These variables survive across ALL execute() calls
    public Integer totalProcessed = 0;
    public Integer totalApproved = 0;
    public List<String> errorMessages = new List<String>();

    public Database.QueryLocator start(Database.BatchableContext bc) {
        return Database.getQueryLocator('SELECT Id, Status__c, LoanAmount__c FROM Loan__c');
    }

    public void execute(Database.BatchableContext bc, List<SObject> scope) {
        for(Loan__c loan : (List<Loan__c>) scope) {
            totalProcessed++; // persists across chunks - won't reset!
            if(loan.Status__c == 'Approved') totalApproved++;
        }
    }

    public void finish(Database.BatchableContext bc) {
        // totalProcessed now has the REAL total across ALL chunks
        System.debug('Processed: ' + totalProcessed + ', Approved: ' + totalApproved);
        EmailService.sendReport(totalProcessed, totalApproved, errorMessages);
    }
}
Without Stateful — the bug trap

Without Stateful, if you have a counter variable and increment it in execute(), it resets to 0 for every chunk. Your finish() method sees 0 or only the count from the last chunk — not the true total. This is a very common production bug for developers who don't understand batch state.

Explain Apex CPU time optimisation — what consumes CPU and how do you reduce it? ADVANCED APEX▼

CPU time limit is 10,000ms synchronous / 60,000ms async. It measures actual Apex execution time — not waiting for SOQL or callouts (those don't count toward CPU). The most common causes of CPU limit errors:

  • String concatenation in loops — String is immutable in Apex. Concatenating in a loop creates a new String object every iteration. Use List and String.join() instead.
  • Nested loops over large collections — O(n²) complexity. Replace inner loop lookups with a Map for O(1).
  • JSON serialization of large objects — JSON.serialize() on deep object graphs is expensive. Serialize only what you need.
  • Regex operations in loops — Pattern.compile() is expensive. Compile once, reuse the Matcher.
  • Complex formula fields in SOQL result loops — Avoid querying formula fields when you don't need them.
// BAD - String concatenation in loop = new object every iteration
String result = '';
for(Loan__c l : loans) { result += l.LoanAmount__c + ','; } // CPU killer

// GOOD - List + join = one String operation at end
List<String> parts = new List<String>();
for(Loan__c l : loans) { parts.add(String.valueOf(l.LoanAmount__c)); }
String result = String.join(parts, ','); // single operation

// BAD - nested loop = O(n²)
for(Loan__c l : loans) {
    for(Account a : accounts) { // checks every account for every loan
        if(a.Id == l.AccountId__c) { l.BrokerName__c = a.Name; }
    }
}

// GOOD - Map lookup = O(1) per loan
Map<Id, Account> accMap = new Map<Id, Account>(accounts);
for(Loan__c l : loans) {
    if(accMap.containsKey(l.AccountId__c)) l.BrokerName__c = accMap.get(l.AccountId__c).Name;
}

// BAD - compile regex every iteration
for(String s : inputs) { if(Pattern.compile('\\d{3}-\\d{2}-\\d{4}').matcher(s).matches()) {...} }

// GOOD - compile once, reuse
Pattern ssnPattern = Pattern.compile('\\d{3}-\\d{2}-\\d{4}');
for(String s : inputs) { if(ssnPattern.matcher(s).matches()) {...} }
What is Apex Managed Sharing — when and how do you implement it? ADVANCED APEX▼

Apex Managed Sharing allows you to programmatically grant record access beyond what OWD and sharing rules provide, using a custom RowCause. This is more powerful than manual sharing because it persists across record ownership changes and can be fully automated.

// Every custom object gets a __Share object automatically
// Loan__c → Loan__Share
// Fields: ParentId (record), UserOrGroupId (who gets access), AccessLevel, RowCause

public class LoanSharingService {

    // RowCause must be defined as a Sharing Reason on the object in setup
    private static final String ROW_CAUSE = Schema.Loan__Share.RowCause.BrokerAccess__c;

    public static void shareLoanWithBroker(Id loanId, Id brokerUserId) {
        Loan__Share share = new Loan__Share();
        share.ParentId = loanId;               // the loan record
        share.UserOrGroupId = brokerUserId;    // the broker user
        share.AccessLevel = 'Read';           // Read, Edit (not Full on custom)
        share.RowCause = ROW_CAUSE;            // custom reason - required for apex sharing

        Database.SaveResult sr = Database.insert(share, false);
        if(!sr.isSuccess()) {
            System.debug('Share failed: ' + sr.getErrors()[0].getMessage());
        }
    }

    public static void removeShareForBroker(Id loanId, Id brokerUserId) {
        List<Loan__Share> toDelete = [
            SELECT Id FROM Loan__Share
            WHERE ParentId = :loanId
            AND UserOrGroupId = :brokerUserId
            AND RowCause = :ROW_CAUSE
        ];
        if(!toDelete.isEmpty()) delete toDelete;
    }
}
Real project use case

In the Mortgage Origination Platform, when a loan was assigned to a broker partner, we used Apex Managed Sharing to give that broker's user Read access to the loan. When the broker was replaced, the old share was deleted and a new one created. Standard sharing rules could not handle this dynamic, per-record assignment logic.

Explain the difference between addError() on a field vs on the record — and when you use each ADVANCED APEX▼ 
// addError() on the RECORD - shows error at the top of the page
for(Loan__c loan : Trigger.new) {
    if(loan.LoanAmount__c < 50000) {
        loan.addError('Loan amount must be at least $50,000 for this loan type.');
        // Shows as a red banner at top of the record form
    }
}

// addError() on a FIELD - highlights the specific field in red
for(Loan__c loan : Trigger.new) {
    if(loan.LoanAmount__c < 50000) {
        loan.LoanAmount__c.addError('Minimum loan amount is $50,000');
        // Highlights the LoanAmount field specifically - better UX
    }
}

// When to use which:
// Field-level error: when the problem is clearly one field (validation)
// Record-level error: when the error involves multiple fields or is a business rule
// that doesn't map cleanly to one field (e.g., "DTI ratio exceeds limit based on
// income + all debts combined")

// escape=false parameter - renders HTML in error message
loan.addError('<b>TRID Violation:</b> Closing date cannot be within 3 days of CD delivery', false);
What is Queueable chaining — how do you chain 3 jobs and handle failures in the chain? ADVANCED APEX▼ 
// Chain: CreditCheck → OFACScreening → DocuSignTrigger
// Each job chains the next only if it succeeds

public class CreditCheckJob implements Queueable, Database.AllowsCallouts {
    private Id loanId;

    public CreditCheckJob(Id loanId) { this.loanId = loanId; }

    public void execute(QueueableContext ctx) {
        try {
            Loan__c loan = [SELECT Id, SSN__c, LastName__c FROM Loan__c WHERE Id = :loanId];
            CreditResult result = ExperianService.pullCredit(loan.SSN__c, loan.LastName__c);

            loan.FicoScore__c = result.ficoScore;
            loan.CreditStatus__c = result.ficoScore >= 620 ? 'Passed' : 'Failed';
            update loan;

            if(loan.CreditStatus__c == 'Passed') {
                // Chain next job only on success
                System.enqueueJob(new OFACScreeningJob(loanId));
            } else {
                // Mark loan as needing manual review - don't chain
                LoanStatusService.flagForReview(loanId, 'Credit score below minimum');
            }
        } catch(Exception e) {
            // Log error, set error status on loan - don't let exception kill silently
            ErrorLogger.log('CreditCheckJob', loanId, e.getMessage(), e.getStackTraceString());
            LoanStatusService.setError(loanId, 'Credit check failed: ' + e.getMessage());
        }
    }
}

public class OFACScreeningJob implements Queueable, Database.AllowsCallouts {
    private Id loanId;
    public OFACScreeningJob(Id loanId) { this.loanId = loanId; }
    public void execute(QueueableContext ctx) {
        // OFAC check logic here, then chain DocuSign if passes
        System.enqueueJob(new DocuSignTriggerJob(loanId));
    }
}

// To start the chain:
System.enqueueJob(new CreditCheckJob(loan.Id));
Use your project story

Say: "In our mortgage platform, after a loan application was submitted I used a Queueable chain — credit check first, then OFAC screening, then DocuSign envelope generation. Each step only chained the next if the current step passed. This way a failed credit check stopped the chain early without triggering document signing for a loan that would be denied."

What are custom metadata types vs custom settings vs custom objects — when do you use each? ADVANCED APEX▼
FeatureCustom Metadata TypesCustom SettingsCustom Objects
Deployable via changesets/SFDXYes — full metadataNo — data not deployedObject yes, data no
Available in formulas/flowsYesYes (Hierarchy type)No
Queried in ApexYes — no SOQL limitYes — no SOQL limit (cached)Yes — counts toward SOQL limit
PackagableYes with dataYes, no dataYes, no data
Use forConfiguration, mappings, feature flagsOrg-wide or user-level configBusiness data, transactional records
// Custom Metadata Type - query with NO SOQL limit
// Created in: Setup → Custom Metadata Types → Loan_Config__mdt
Loan_Config__mdt config = [
    SELECT Min_FICO_Score__c, Max_DTI_Ratio__c, Max_Loan_Amount__c
    FROM Loan_Config__mdt
    WHERE DeveloperName = 'FHA_Standard'
];
// Doesn't count toward 100 SOQL limit - can query in loops safely

// Custom Setting - cached, hierarchy supports user/profile/org level
Loan_Settings__c settings = Loan_Settings__c.getOrgDefaults();
String apiEndpoint = settings.Experian_Endpoint__c;

// Or user/profile specific value (Hierarchy type)
Loan_Settings__c userSettings = Loan_Settings__c.getInstance(); // gets for current user
Recommendation for interviews

Say: "I prefer Custom Metadata Types for configuration data because they're deployable as metadata — they move between sandboxes and production automatically. In our mortgage platform, we stored loan type configurations (min FICO, max DTI, max LTV) as Custom Metadata records. This meant threshold changes were a metadata deploy, not a data load."

Advanced LWC — Senior Level Questions
14 deeper questions on performance, architecture, and testing in LWC
How do you optimise a slow LWC component — what are the common performance problems? ADVANCED LWC▼

Common LWC performance problems and fixes:

  • Too many wire calls on load — multiple wire adapters firing simultaneously. Fix: lazy load non-critical data using imperative calls triggered by user scroll or tab switch.
  • Re-renders on every property change — computed getters that return new object/array references each time. Fix: cache computed values, only recalculate when source data changes.
  • Large datatable without pagination — rendering 1000+ rows crashes browsers. Fix: implement server-side pagination with OFFSET or cursor-based pagination.
  • Calling refreshApex unnecessarily — triggers a server round trip. Fix: only refresh when actual DML happened. Use optimistic UI updates for better perceived performance.
  • Event listeners not cleaned up — memory leaks over time. Fix: always unsubscribe LMS, clear intervals/timeouts in disconnectedCallback.
// Problem: getter creates new array every render = unnecessary re-renders
get approvedLoans() {
    return this.loans.filter(l => l.Status__c === 'Approved'); // new array every call
}

// Fix: cache the computed value, recalculate only when loans changes
_approvedLoans = [];
set loans(value) {
    this._loans = value;
    this._approvedLoans = value ? value.filter(l => l.Status__c === 'Approved') : [];
}
get loans() { return this._loans; }
get approvedLoans() { return this._approvedLoans; } // stable reference

// Pagination pattern
currentPage = 1;
pageSize = 20;

get pagedLoans() {
    const start = (this.currentPage - 1) * this.pageSize;
    return this.allLoans.slice(start, start + this.pageSize);
}

handleNextPage() { this.currentPage++; }
handlePrevPage() { if(this.currentPage > 1) this.currentPage--; }
How do you test LWC components using Jest — show a complete test example ADVANCED LWC▼ 
// __tests__/loanCard.test.js
import { createElement } from 'lwc';
import LoanCard from 'c/loanCard';
import getLoanDetails from '@salesforce/apex/LoanController.getLoanDetails';

// Mock the Apex call
jest.mock('@salesforce/apex/LoanController.getLoanDetails', () => ({
    default: jest.fn()
}), { virtual: true });

describe('c-loan-card', () => {

    afterEach(() => {
        while(document.body.firstChild) document.body.removeChild(document.body.firstChild);
    });

    it('displays loan amount when data loads', async () => {
        // Mock wire response
        getLoanDetails.mockResolvedValue({ Id: '001', LoanAmount__c: 250000, Status__c: 'Approved' });

        const element = createElement('c-loan-card', { is: LoanCard });
        element.recordId = '001xxx';
        document.body.appendChild(element);

        await Promise.resolve(); // wait for async wire

        const amountEl = element.shadowRoot.querySelector('.loan-amount');
        expect(amountEl.textContent).toBe('$250,000');
    });

    it('shows error when apex fails', async () => {
        getLoanDetails.mockRejectedValue({ body: { message: 'Record not found' } });

        const element = createElement('c-loan-card', { is: LoanCard });
        element.recordId = 'badId';
        document.body.appendChild(element);

        await Promise.resolve();

        const errorEl = element.shadowRoot.querySelector('.error-message');
        expect(errorEl).not.toBeNull();
        expect(errorEl.textContent).toContain('Record not found');
    });

    it('fires loansaved event on save button click', async () => {
        const element = createElement('c-loan-card', { is: LoanCard });
        document.body.appendChild(element);

        const handler = jest.fn();
        element.addEventListener('loansaved', handler);

        element.shadowRoot.querySelector('lightning-button').click();
        await Promise.resolve();

        expect(handler).toHaveBeenCalledTimes(1);
        expect(handler.mock.calls[0][0].detail.loanId).toBeDefined();
    });
});
Interview tip

If asked about LWC testing, say: "LWC uses Jest for unit testing — which is the industry standard JavaScript testing framework. You mock Apex calls and wire adapters using jest.fn() and mockResolvedValue. Tests are run locally with npm run test:unit — no Salesforce org needed. This is a major advantage over Aura which had no unit test framework."

What is the Shadow DOM in LWC — how does it affect CSS styling? ADVANCED LWC▼

Shadow DOM is a web standard that encapsulates a component's internal DOM and CSS. It creates a boundary — styles defined inside a component do NOT leak out to the parent, and parent styles do NOT reach inside a child component. LWC uses a synthetic Shadow DOM (and native in some contexts).

  • CSS scoping: Each component's .css file only applies to elements in THAT component's template. You cannot style a child component's internals from a parent's CSS.
  • :host selector: Styles the root element of the component itself from within the component's CSS file.
  • CSS custom properties (variables): The only way to style across shadow boundaries. Parent defines a variable, child uses it.
  • SLDS tokens: Salesforce's design system uses CSS variables for this exact reason — they cross shadow boundaries.
/* Parent component CSS - CANNOT style inside child */
/* This does NOT work: */
c-loan-card .title { color: red; } /* blocked by shadow DOM */

/* SOLUTION: CSS custom properties cross the boundary */
/* In parent CSS: */
c-loan-card {
    --loan-title-color: red;   /* define the variable on the host */
}

/* In child (loanCard.css): */
.title {
    color: var(--loan-title-color, black); /* use the variable with fallback */
}

/* :host - style the component's root element from inside */
:host {
    display: block;
    border-radius: 8px;
    overflow: hidden;
}
:host(.highlighted) { border: 2px solid blue; } /* when parent adds class to component */

How does Lightning Data Service (LDS) work — when do you use it vs Apex?ADV LWC▼

LDS is Salesforce's client-side data caching layer. It shares one cache across all LWC/Aura components on the page — if two components query the same record, only one network call is made.

// getRecord — LDS wire adapter (uses LDS cache)
import { getRecord, getFieldValue, updateRecord, deleteRecord } from 'lightning/uiRecordApi';
import { refreshApex } from '@salesforce/apex';
import LOAN_NAME from '@salesforce/schema/Loan__c.Name';
import LOAN_STATUS from '@salesforce/schema/Loan__c.Status__c';

@wire(getRecord, { recordId: '$recordId', fields: [LOAN_NAME, LOAN_STATUS] })
loan;

// Update via LDS — no Apex needed, respects FLS automatically
async handleSave() {
  const fields = { Id: this.recordId, Status__c: 'Approved' };
  try {
    await updateRecord({ fields });
    // LDS automatically invalidates cache — all other components refresh
  } catch (e) { this.showError(e); }
}
ScenarioUse LDSUse Apex
Single record CRUD✓ getRecord, updateRecord—
Multi-record queries with WHERE—✓ SOQL in @AuraEnabled
Multi-object transaction—✓ Apex handles atomicity
Needs FLS auto-enforcement✓ LDS enforces itManually use WITH SECURITY_ENFORCED
Complex business logic on save—✓ Apex service layer
Real-time cache across components✓ LDS shared cache— (each Apex call is independent)
What is the Lightning Navigation Service — how do you navigate in LWC?ADV LWC▼
import { LightningElement, api } from 'lwc';
import { NavigationMixin } from 'lightning/navigation';

export default class LoanNavigator extends NavigationMixin(LightningElement) {
  @api recordId;

  // Navigate to a record page
  goToRecord() {
    this[NavigationMixin.Navigate]({ type: 'standard__recordPage',
      attributes: { recordId: this.recordId, objectApiName: 'Loan__c', actionName: 'view' }});
  }

  // Navigate to new record form
  createLoan() {
    this[NavigationMixin.Navigate]({ type: 'standard__objectPage',
      attributes: { objectApiName: 'Loan__c', actionName: 'new' }});
  }

  // Navigate to a custom tab
  goToTab() {
    this[NavigationMixin.Navigate]({ type: 'standard__navItemPage',
      attributes: { apiName: 'Loan_Dashboard' }});
  }

  // Navigate to external URL
  openDocuSign() {
    this[NavigationMixin.Navigate]({ type: 'standard__webPage',
      attributes: { url: 'https://app.docusign.com/envelopes/' + this.envelopeId }});
  }

  // Generate URL without navigating (for href or sharing)
  async getLoanUrl() {
    const url = await this[NavigationMixin.GenerateUrl]({
      type: 'standard__recordPage',
      attributes: { recordId: this.recordId, actionName: 'view' }
    });
    return url;
  }
}
How do you use Lightning Message Service (LMS) for cross-component communication?ADV LWC▼

LMS is the modern replacement for Aura application events and PubSub patterns. Works across LWC, Aura, and Visualforce components on the same page.

// 1. Create message channel: force-app/main/default/messageChannels/LoanChannel__c.messageChannel-meta.xml
// <LightningMessageChannel xmlns="..."><masterLabel>Loan Channel</masterLabel></LightningMessageChannel>

// 2. Publisher component
import { LightningElement, wire } from 'lwc';
import { publish, MessageContext } from 'lightning/messageService';
import LOAN_CHANNEL from '@salesforce/messageChannel/LoanChannel__c';

export default class LoanList extends LightningElement {
  @wire(MessageContext) messageContext;

  handleLoanSelect(event) {
    publish(this.messageContext, LOAN_CHANNEL, {
      loanId: event.detail.loanId,
      loanName: event.detail.loanName,
      action: 'SELECTED'
    });
  }
}

// 3. Subscriber component (anywhere on same Lightning page)
import { subscribe, unsubscribe, MessageContext } from 'lightning/messageService';
import LOAN_CHANNEL from '@salesforce/messageChannel/LoanChannel__c';

export default class LoanDetail extends LightningElement {
  @wire(MessageContext) messageContext;
  subscription = null;

  connectedCallback() {
    this.subscription = subscribe(this.messageContext, LOAN_CHANNEL, (msg) => {
      if (msg.action === 'SELECTED') this.loadLoan(msg.loanId);
    });
  }

  disconnectedCallback() { unsubscribe(this.subscription); }
}
How do you implement real-time UI updates using Platform Events in LWC?ADV LWC▼
import { LightningElement } from 'lwc';
import { subscribe, unsubscribe, onError } from 'lightning/empApi';

export default class LoanStatusBoard extends LightningElement {
  subscription = {};
  loans = [];

  async connectedCallback() {
    this.subscription = await subscribe(
      '/event/Loan_Status_Change__e',
      -1, // -1 = new events only; -2 = replay all retained
      (response) => {
        const payload = response.data.payload;
        this.updateLoanStatus(payload.Loan_Id__c, payload.New_Status__c, payload.Borrower_Name__c);
      }
    );
    onError(error => { console.error('Streaming error:', error); this.reconnect(); });
  }

  updateLoanStatus(loanId, status, borrower) {
    // Update the specific loan in the list without a full refresh
    this.loans = this.loans.map(loan =>
      loan.Id === loanId ? { ...loan, Status__c: status } : loan
    );
    // Show toast for important transitions
    if (status === 'Approved') {
      this.dispatchEvent(new ShowToastEvent({ title: 'Loan Approved!',
        message: borrower + ''s loan was approved', variant: 'success' }));
    }
  }

  disconnectedCallback() { unsubscribe(this.subscription); }
}
How do you implement a custom LWC data table with sorting and filtering?ADV LWC▼
// loanTable.js
import { LightningElement, track, wire } from 'lwc';
import getLoans from '@salesforce/apex/LoanController.getLoans';

export default class LoanTable extends LightningElement {
  @track loans = [];
  @track filteredLoans = [];
  sortField = 'Name'; sortDirection = 'asc'; filterText = '';

  columns = [
    { label:'Loan', fieldName:'Name', type:'text', sortable:true },
    { label:'Status', fieldName:'Status__c', type:'text', sortable:true },
    { label:'Amount', fieldName:'Amount__c', type:'currency', sortable:true },
    { label:'Date', fieldName:'CloseDate__c', type:'date', sortable:true },
  ];

  @wire(getLoans)
  wiredLoans({ data }) { if (data) { this.loans = data; this.applyFilter(); } }

  handleSort(event) {
    this.sortField = event.detail.fieldName;
    this.sortDirection = event.detail.sortDirection;
    this.applyFilter();
  }

  handleFilter(event) { this.filterText = event.target.value; this.applyFilter(); }

  applyFilter() {
    let result = [...this.loans];
    // Filter
    if (this.filterText) {
      const term = this.filterText.toLowerCase();
      result = result.filter(l => l.Name?.toLowerCase().includes(term) || l.Status__c?.toLowerCase().includes(term));
    }
    // Sort
    result.sort((a, b) => {
      const valA = a[this.sortField] ?? ''; const valB = b[this.sortField] ?? '';
      const cmp = valA > valB ? 1 : valA < valB ? -1 : 0;
      return this.sortDirection === 'asc' ? cmp : -cmp;
    });
    this.filteredLoans = result;
  }

  get totalCount() { return this.filteredLoans.length; }
}
How do you handle file uploads in LWC — upload to Salesforce Files and to external storage?ADV LWC▼
// Upload to Salesforce Files
<!-- Template -->
<lightning-file-upload label="Upload Documents" accept=".pdf,.docx"
  record-id={recordId} onuploadfinished={handleUpload}></lightning-file-upload>

// JS handler
handleUpload(event) {
  const files = event.detail.files; // [{ documentId, name, size }]
  const fileIds = files.map(f => f.documentId);
  this.dispatchEvent(new CustomEvent('filesadded', { detail: { fileIds } }));
  this.dispatchEvent(new ShowToastEvent({ title:'Uploaded', message:files.length+' file(s)', variant:'success'}));
}

// Custom file upload with progress (for large files or external storage)
async handleCustomUpload(event) {
  const file = event.target.files[0];
  const reader = new FileReader();
  reader.onload = async () => {
    const base64 = reader.result.split(',')[1];
    try {
      this.uploadProgress = 0;
      // Call Apex with base64 — max 4.5MB before base64 encoding
      const result = await uploadDocument({ fileName: file.name, base64Content: base64, recordId: this.recordId });
      this.uploadProgress = 100;
    } catch(e) { this.showError(e.body.message); }
  };
  reader.readAsDataURL(file);
}
What are LWC slots — how do you build reusable container components?ADV LWC▼
// Card container component using slots
// cardContainer.html
<template>
  <div class="card">
    <div class="card-header">
      <slot name="header">Default Header</slot>  <!-- named slot -->
    </div>
    <div class="card-body">
      <slot></slot>  <!-- default slot -- receives all un-named slotted content -->
    </div>
    <div class="card-footer">
      <slot name="footer"></slot>  <!-- named slot for footer actions -->
    </div>
  </div>
</template>

// Parent uses the container
<c-card-container>
  <span slot="header">Loan Application #LOAN-001</span>  <!-- fills named slot -->

  <!-- Default slot content -->
  <p>Borrower: John Smith</p>
  <p>Amount: $250,000</p>

  <div slot="footer">  <!-- fills footer slot -->
    <lightning-button label="Approve" onclick={handleApprove}></lightning-button>
    <lightning-button label="Decline" variant="destructive" onclick={handleDecline}></lightning-button>
  </div>
</c-card-container>
When to use slots

Slots are for layout/container components where the parent provides content. Examples: modal dialogs, card containers, tab panels, wizard steps. The container handles layout; the parent provides the specific content.

How do you implement a wizard / multi-step form in LWC?ADV LWC▼
// loanApplicationWizard.js
import { LightningElement, track } from 'lwc';

export default class LoanApplicationWizard extends LightningElement {
  @track currentStep = 1;
  @track loanData = { borrowerInfo: {}, loanDetails: {}, documents: [] };
  totalSteps = 4;
  steps = ['Borrower Info','Loan Details','Documents','Review & Submit'];

  get progressPercent() { return Math.round((this.currentStep / this.totalSteps) * 100); }
  get isFirstStep() { return this.currentStep === 1; }
  get isLastStep() { return this.currentStep === this.totalSteps; }
  get currentStepLabel() { return this.steps[this.currentStep - 1]; }

  // Each step fires an event with its data when valid
  handleStepComplete(event) {
    const stepData = event.detail;
    if (this.currentStep === 1) this.loanData.borrowerInfo = stepData;
    if (this.currentStep === 2) this.loanData.loanDetails = stepData;
    if (this.currentStep === 3) this.loanData.documents = stepData.fileIds;
    if (this.currentStep < this.totalSteps) this.currentStep++;
  }

  handleBack() { if (this.currentStep > 1) this.currentStep--; }

  async handleSubmit() {
    try {
      await submitLoanApplication({ loanData: JSON.stringify(this.loanData) });
      this.dispatchEvent(new ShowToastEvent({ title:'Application Submitted', variant:'success' }));
    } catch(e) { this.showError(e); }
  }
}
How do you use dynamic components in LWC — lwc:component and lazy loading?ADV LWC▼
// Dynamic component — component type determined at runtime
// loanActionPanel.html
<template>
  <lwc:component lwc:is={dynamicComponent} loan-id={recordId} onactioncomplete={handleComplete}></lwc:component>
</template>

// loanActionPanel.js
import { LightningElement, api } from 'lwc';

export default class LoanActionPanel extends LightningElement {
  @api recordId;
  @api actionType; // 'approve', 'decline', 'request-docs'

  // Lazy load the component based on actionType
  get dynamicComponent() {
    switch(this.actionType) {
      case 'approve':       return import('c/loanApprovalForm');
      case 'decline':       return import('c/loanDeclineForm');
      case 'request-docs':  return import('c/documentRequestForm');
      default: return null;
    }
  }
  handleComplete(event) {
    this.dispatchEvent(new CustomEvent('actioncomplete', { detail: event.detail }));
  }
}
Dynamic import benefit

The component bundle is only downloaded from Salesforce servers when needed. This reduces initial page load time — a loan officer who never declines loans never downloads the decline form code.

How do you implement retry logic for a failed API callout in Apex? ADVANCED INTG▼ 
public class RetryableCalloutService {

    private static final Integer MAX_RETRIES = 3;
    private static final Integer RETRY_DELAY_MS = 2000; // 2 seconds

    public static HttpResponse callWithRetry(HttpRequest req) {
        Http http = new Http();
        HttpResponse res;
        Exception lastError;

        for(Integer attempt = 1; attempt <= MAX_RETRIES; attempt++) {
            try {
                res = http.send(req);

                // Retry on 429 (rate limit) or 503 (service unavailable)
                if(res.getStatusCode() == 429 || res.getStatusCode() == 503) {
                    System.debug('Attempt ' + attempt + ' got ' + res.getStatusCode() + '. Retrying...');
                    if(attempt < MAX_RETRIES) {
                        // Wait before retry (exponential backoff)
                        Long waitTime = RETRY_DELAY_MS * attempt;
                        Long endTime = System.currentTimeMillis() + waitTime;
                        while(System.currentTimeMillis() < endTime) {} // busy wait (limited use)
                        continue;
                    }
                }

                // Success or non-retryable error
                return res;

            } catch(CalloutException e) {
                lastError = e;
                System.debug('Attempt ' + attempt + ' callout exception: ' + e.getMessage());
                if(attempt == MAX_RETRIES) throw e;
            }
        }
        return res;
    }
}

// Better pattern for production: use a custom object to track retry attempts
// and a scheduled job to re-process failed callouts
public class CalloutRetryQueue {
    // 1. On first failure, insert a Callout_Retry__c record with the payload
    // 2. Scheduled job runs every 15 min, finds pending retries
    // 3. Retries up to max_attempts, marks success or permanent_failure
    // This is more scalable than busy-waiting in a synchronous transaction
}
How do you expose an Apex class as a REST API endpoint — complete code ADVANCED INTG▼ 
// URL: https://[instance]/services/apexrest/v1/loans/
@RestResource(urlMapping='/v1/loans/*')
global with sharing class LoanRestService {

    @HttpGet
    global static LoanResponse getLoan() {
        RestRequest req = RestContext.request;
        String loanId = req.requestURI.substring(req.requestURI.lastIndexOf('/')+1);

        try {
            Loan__c loan = [SELECT Id, LoanAmount__c, Status__c, BorrowerName__c
                            FROM Loan__c WHERE Id = :loanId WITH USER_MODE];
            return new LoanResponse(true, loan, null);
        } catch(QueryException e) {
            RestContext.response.statusCode = 404;
            return new LoanResponse(false, null, 'Loan not found: ' + loanId);
        }
    }

    @HttpPost
    global static LoanResponse createLoan() {
        try {
            String body = RestContext.request.requestBody.toString();
            LoanRequest loanReq = (LoanRequest) JSON.deserialize(body, LoanRequest.class);

            Loan__c newLoan = new Loan__c(
                LoanAmount__c = loanReq.loanAmount,
                BorrowerName__c = loanReq.borrowerName,
                LoanType__c = loanReq.loanType,
                Status__c = 'Application'
            );
            insert newLoan;

            RestContext.response.statusCode = 201; // Created
            return new LoanResponse(true, newLoan, null);
        } catch(Exception e) {
            RestContext.response.statusCode = 400; // Bad Request
            return new LoanResponse(false, null, e.getMessage());
        }
    }

    @HttpDelete
    global static LoanResponse deleteLoan() {
        String loanId = RestContext.request.requestURI.substringAfterLast('/');
        delete [SELECT Id FROM Loan__c WHERE Id = :loanId];
        RestContext.response.statusCode = 204; // No Content
        return null;
    }

    global class LoanRequest {
        global Decimal loanAmount;
        global String borrowerName;
        global String loanType;
    }

    global class LoanResponse {
        global Boolean success;
        global Object data;
        global String error;
        global LoanResponse(Boolean s, Object d, String e) { success=s; data=d; error=e; }
    }
}
How do you implement OAuth 2.0 JWT Bearer Flow for server-to-server integrations?ADV INTEGRATION▼

JWT Bearer Flow allows system-to-system authentication without any user interaction — the app proves its identity with a signed certificate.

// Setup: Connected App → Use Digital Signature → upload certificate
// Pre-authorize integration user: Connected App → Manage → Add Profile/User

// Generate JWT and exchange for access token
public class JWTBearerAuth {
  public static String getAccessToken() {
    // Build JWT header
    String header = EncodingUtil.base64Encode(Blob.valueOf('{"alg":"RS256","typ":"JWT"}'));

    // Build JWT claims
    Long now = DateTime.now().getTime() / 1000;
    String claims = JSON.serialize(new Map<String,Object>{
      'iss' => 'YOUR_CONNECTED_APP_CLIENT_ID',
      'sub' => '[email protected]', // pre-authorized user
      'aud' => 'https://login.salesforce.com',
      'exp' => now + 300 // expires in 5 minutes
    });
    String claimsEncoded = EncodingUtil.base64Encode(Blob.valueOf(claims));

    // Sign with private key from Certificate and Key Store
    String signingInput = header + '.' + claimsEncoded;
    Blob signature = Crypto.sign('RSA-SHA256', Blob.valueOf(signingInput),
      EncodingUtil.base64Decode(getCertificatePrivateKey()));
    String jwt = signingInput + '.' + EncodingUtil.base64Encode(signature);

    // Exchange JWT for access token
    HttpRequest req = new HttpRequest();
    req.setEndpoint('https://login.salesforce.com/services/oauth2/token');
    req.setMethod('POST');
    req.setBody('grant_type=urn:ietf:params:oauth:grant-type:jwt-bearer&assertion=' + jwt);
    HttpResponse res = new Http().send(req);
    Map<String,Object> result = (Map<String,Object>) JSON.deserializeUntyped(res.getBody());
    return (String) result.get('access_token');
  }
}
How do you handle API versioning and backward compatibility in @RestResource endpoints?ADV INTEGRATION▼
// Version in URL path — cleanest approach
@RestResource(urlMapping='/v1/loans/*')
global class LoanResourceV1 {
  @HttpGet global static LoanResponseV1 doGet() { /* v1 response shape */ }
}

@RestResource(urlMapping='/v2/loans/*')
global class LoanResourceV2 {
  @HttpGet global static LoanResponseV2 doGet() { /* v2 adds new fields, never removes */ }
}

// Version in Accept header — single endpoint, versioned response
@RestResource(urlMapping='/loans/*')
global class LoanResource {
  @HttpGet global static Object doGet() {
    String version = RestContext.request.headers.get('API-Version') ?? 'v1';
    if (version == 'v2') return buildV2Response();
    return buildV1Response(); // default to v1
  }
}
// Rules for backward compatibility:
// NEVER remove a field from response — add deprecation notice in docs, set to null
// NEVER change a field's type — add a new field with the new type
// ALWAYS version breaking changes — new path or Accept header version
API contract

External systems depend on your response shape. Breaking changes without versioning = production incidents for your API consumers. Treat your API like a public interface — additive only.

What is idempotency in API design — how do you implement it in Salesforce?ADV INTEGRATION▼

Idempotency means calling the same API multiple times produces the same result as calling it once. Critical for retry safety.

// Idempotency key pattern — client sends unique key per logical operation
@RestResource(urlMapping='/v1/loans')
global class LoanCreateResource {
  @HttpPost
  global static LoanCreateResponse doPost() {
    String idempKey = RestContext.request.headers.get('Idempotency-Key');
    if (String.isBlank(idempKey)) {
      RestContext.response.statusCode = 400;
      return new LoanCreateResponse(null, 'Idempotency-Key header required');
    }

    // Check if we already processed this key
    List<Idempotency_Record__c> existing = [
      SELECT Response_Body__c FROM Idempotency_Record__c WHERE Key__c=:idempKey LIMIT 1
    ];
    if (!existing.isEmpty()) {
      // Return cached response — safe to call multiple times
      return (LoanCreateResponse) JSON.deserialize(existing[0].Response_Body__c, LoanCreateResponse.class);
    }

    // Process the request
    Loan__c newLoan = createLoan(RestContext.request.requestBody.toString());
    LoanCreateResponse response = new LoanCreateResponse(newLoan.Id, 'Created');

    // Store the idempotency record with TTL (clean up after 24 hours via scheduled job)
    insert new Idempotency_Record__c(Key__c=idempKey, Response_Body__c=JSON.serialize(response),
      Expires_At__c=DateTime.now().addHours(24));
    RestContext.response.statusCode = 201;
    return response;
  }
}
What is HMAC signature validation — how do you secure inbound webhooks?ADV INTEGRATION▼

Webhooks from external systems (DocuSign, Twilio, Stripe) can be spoofed. HMAC-SHA256 signature validation proves the request genuinely came from the expected sender.

@RestResource(urlMapping='/v1/docusignWebhook')
global class DocuSignWebhookResource {
  @HttpPost
  global static void doPost() {
    String payload = RestContext.request.requestBody.toString();
    String receivedSig = RestContext.request.headers.get('X-DocuSign-Signature-1');
    String sharedSecret = 'YOUR_HMAC_SECRET'; // stored in Custom Setting or Named Credential

    // Compute expected HMAC-SHA256
    Blob hmac = Crypto.generateMac('HmacSHA256', Blob.valueOf(payload), Blob.valueOf(sharedSecret));
    String expectedSig = EncodingUtil.base64Encode(hmac);

    // Compare — timing-safe comparison (avoid timing attacks)
    if (receivedSig != expectedSig) {
      RestContext.response.statusCode = 401;
      return;
    }

    // Signature valid — process the webhook
    Map<String,Object> body = (Map<String,Object>) JSON.deserializeUntyped(payload);
    String envelopeId = (String) body.get('envelopeId');
    String status = (String) body.get('status');

    if (status == 'completed') System.enqueueJob(new DocuSignCompletedQueueable(envelopeId));
  }
}
How do you implement request/response logging for all outbound callouts?ADV INTEGRATION▼
// Callout logger — wraps every outbound request
public class CalloutLogger {
  public static HttpResponse send(HttpRequest req, String integrationName) {
    DateTime startTime = DateTime.now();
    HttpResponse res;
    String error = null;

    try {
      res = new Http().send(req);
      return res;
    } catch (CalloutException e) {
      error = e.getMessage();
      throw e; // re-throw after logging
    } finally {
      // Always log — success or failure
      Callout_Log__c log = new Callout_Log__c(
        Integration__c = integrationName,
        Endpoint__c    = req.getEndpoint(),
        Method__c      = req.getMethod(),
        Request_Body__c = req.getBody()?.abbreviate(32768), // field length limit
        Status_Code__c  = res != null ? res.getStatusCode() : null,
        Response_Body__c = res != null ? res.getBody()?.abbreviate(32768) : null,
        Error__c        = error,
        Duration_Ms__c  = DateTime.now().getTime() - startTime.getTime()
      );
      // Use Database.insert to avoid rollback cancelling the log
      Database.insert(log, false);
    }
  }
}
// Usage:
HttpResponse res = CalloutLogger.send(req, 'Experian_Credit');
What is the outbox pattern — how do you guarantee exactly-once delivery?ADV INTEGRATION▼

The Outbox pattern ensures an event/message is never lost: write to an outbox table in the same transaction as your data change, then a separate process reliably delivers from the outbox.

// Step 1: Write loan update AND outbox record in same transaction (atomic)
trigger LoanTrigger on Loan__c (after update) {
  List<Integration_Outbox__c> outbox = new List<Integration_Outbox__c>();
  for (Loan__c l : Trigger.new) {
    if (l.Status__c != Trigger.oldMap.get(l.Id).Status__c) {
      outbox.add(new Integration_Outbox__c(
        Loan__c=l.Id, Event_Type__c='STATUS_CHANGE',
        Payload__c=JSON.serialize(new Map<String,Object>{'loanId'=>l.Id,'status'=>l.Status__c}),
        Status__c='Pending', Created_At__c=DateTime.now()
      ));
    }
  }
  if (!outbox.isEmpty()) insert outbox;
}

// Step 2: Scheduled job reliably delivers from outbox (runs every 5 min)
public class OutboxDeliveryJob implements Schedulable {
  public void execute(SchedulableContext ctx) {
    Database.executeBatch(new OutboxDeliveryBatch(), 10); // small scope for callout limit
  }
}
public class OutboxDeliveryBatch implements Database.Batchable<SObject>, Database.AllowsCallouts {
  public Database.QueryLocator start(Database.BatchableContext bc) {
    return Database.getQueryLocator([SELECT Id, Payload__c, Retry_Count__c FROM Integration_Outbox__c
      WHERE Status__c='Pending' AND Retry_Count__c < 3 ORDER BY Created_At__c ASC]);
  }
  public void execute(Database.BatchableContext bc, List<SObject> scope) {
    for (Integration_Outbox__c rec : (List<Integration_Outbox__c>)scope) {
      try { deliverToLOS(rec.Payload__c); rec.Status__c='Delivered'; }
      catch(Exception e) { rec.Retry_Count__c++; rec.Last_Error__c=e.getMessage(); }
    }
    update scope;
  }
  public void finish(Database.BatchableContext bc) {}
}

How do you test callouts in Apex — complete mock pattern?ADV INTEGRATION▼
// HttpCalloutMock interface
@isTest
global class MultiRequestMock implements HttpCalloutMock {
  Map<String, HttpCalloutMock> mocks = new Map<String, HttpCalloutMock>();

  global MultiRequestMock(Map<String, HttpCalloutMock> mocks) { this.mocks = mocks; }

  global HttpResponse respond(HttpRequest req) {
    String endpoint = req.getEndpoint();
    for (String key : mocks.keySet()) {
      if (endpoint.contains(key)) return mocks.get(key).respond(req);
    }
    throw new CalloutException('No mock for endpoint: ' + endpoint);
  }
}

// Single mock
@isTest
global class CreditBureauMock implements HttpCalloutMock {
  Integer score; Boolean shouldFail;
  global CreditBureauMock(Integer score, Boolean shouldFail) { this.score=score; this.shouldFail=shouldFail; }
  global HttpResponse respond(HttpRequest req) {
    HttpResponse res = new HttpResponse();
    if (shouldFail) { res.setStatusCode(503); res.setBody('Service Unavailable'); return res; }
    res.setStatusCode(200);
    res.setBody(JSON.serialize(new Map<String,Object>{'creditScore'=>score,'riskTier'=>'A'}));
    return res;
  }
}

// Test using mock
@isTest static void testCreditCheck_success() {
  Test.setMock(HttpCalloutMock.class, new CreditBureauMock(750, false));
  Test.startTest();
  CreditCheckService.run(loan.Id);
  Test.stopTest();
  System.assertEquals(750, [SELECT Credit_Score__c FROM Loan__c WHERE Id=:loan.Id].Credit_Score__c);
}
What is mutual TLS (mTLS) and when is it required in financial services integrations?ADV INTEGRATION▼

Mutual TLS requires BOTH sides to authenticate with certificates — server proves identity to client AND client proves identity to server. Standard TLS only authenticates the server.

  • When required — Banking regulators (OCC, FFIEC), PCI-DSS high-security APIs, Federal agency integrations, some credit bureau APIs
  • Setup in Salesforce — Generate a certificate in Setup → Certificate and Key Management. Configure Named Credential to use that certificate as client certificate.
  • Server certificate pinning — Some APIs also require pinning the server's exact certificate. Salesforce Named Credentials don't support pinning natively — requires a middleware proxy.
  • Certificate rotation — mTLS certificates expire. Build rotation reminders into your monitoring — typically 1 year. Expiry = instant outage for all callouts using that cert.
  • Testing — Use Salesforce Sandbox + named credential pointing to vendor's test environment with test certificates. Never test mTLS in production.
How do you design a reliable file transfer integration — large files to/from Salesforce?ADV INTEGRATION▼

Direct callouts have a 12MB response/request limit. For large files (loan documents, credit reports, closing packages), you need a multi-step approach.

// Pattern: Salesforce → S3 (or Azure Blob) for large files
// Step 1: Get pre-signed URL from AWS (short-lived, secure)
public class S3UploadService {
  public static String getPresignedUploadUrl(String fileName, String contentType) {
    HttpRequest req = new HttpRequest();
    req.setEndpoint('callout:AWS_API_GW/presigned-url');
    req.setMethod('POST');
    req.setBody(JSON.serialize(new Map<String,Object>{'fileName'=>fileName,'contentType'=>contentType}));
    HttpResponse res = new Http().send(req);
    Map<String,Object> result = (Map<String,Object>) JSON.deserializeUntyped(res.getBody());
    return (String) result.get('uploadUrl');
  }

  // Step 2: Upload file directly to S3 using the pre-signed URL
  public static void uploadFile(String presignedUrl, Blob fileContent, String contentType) {
    HttpRequest req = new HttpRequest();
    req.setEndpoint(presignedUrl);
    req.setMethod('PUT');
    req.setHeader('Content-Type', contentType);
    req.setBodyAsBlob(fileContent); // up to 12MB per callout
    req.setTimeout(120000); // 2 minutes for large files
    HttpResponse res = new Http().send(req);
    if (res.getStatusCode() != 200) throw new IntegrationException('S3 upload failed: '+res.getStatusCode());
  }
}
// For files >12MB: chunk the file into parts, upload each chunk, then send complete signal
What is the Bulk API 2.0 — when do you use it instead of REST API?ADV INTEGRATION▼
FeatureREST APIBulk API 2.0
Max records per call200 (composite) / 2000 (collection)Millions — async batch processing
Response timeSynchronous — immediateAsync — poll for job completion
ThrottlingCounts against API limitsSeparate Bulk API limits
Use forReal-time single/small batch operationsMass data loads, migrations, nightly syncs
FormatJSONCSV (more efficient for tabular data)
Salesforce toolsNamed Credentials + HTTP calloutData Loader, MuleSoft, custom job polling
When to use Bulk API

Loading 100,000+ records? Use Bulk API 2.0. Creating a Connected App integration that needs real-time response for individual records? Use REST. Rule: >1,000 records or batch nightly jobs → Bulk API. <1,000 records or user-triggered → REST.

Admin & Configuration — Interview Questions
14 questions — senior devs must know the admin layer deeply to architect correctly
What is the difference between Workflow Rules, Process Builder, and Flow — which do you use today? ADMIN▼
ToolStatusCapabilitiesWhen to Use
Workflow RulesLegacy — retire by 2025Field update, email alert, task, outbound messageDo NOT create new. Migrate existing to Flow.
Process BuilderLegacy — retire by 2026More complex than WF, can create/update related recordsDo NOT create new. Migrate existing to Flow.
Flow (Record-Triggered)Current — actively developedFull logic, loops, complex conditions, subflows, calls ApexALL new declarative automation goes here
Apex TriggerCurrentComplex logic, bulk processing, callouts, cross-objectWhen Flow cannot handle the complexity or performance
Interview answer

"Salesforce has officially retired Workflow Rules and Process Builder — all new automation should be built in Flow. I use Record-Triggered Flows for declarative automation and Apex triggers when I need bulk-safe operations, complex logic, callouts, or things Flow cannot handle efficiently. A good rule: if you can do it in Flow cleanly, do it in Flow. If it gets complex or has performance concerns with large data volumes, that's when I bring in Apex."

What is an approval process — explain all components and a real use case ADMIN▼

An Approval Process is a declarative tool that automates a sequence of steps for approving a record. When a record meets certain criteria, it enters the approval process and must be approved (or rejected) by designated users before it moves forward.

  • Entry Criteria — conditions that trigger the process (e.g., Loan Amount > $500,000)
  • Approval Steps — each step defines who approves (user, queue, related field user) and what they can do
  • Approver — specific user, manager field, queue, or based on formula
  • Approval Actions — what happens on approval (field update, email, task, outbound message)
  • Rejection Actions — what happens on rejection
  • Recall Actions — what happens when submitter recalls
  • Initial Submission Actions — happen when record is submitted (before approval)

Real use case in Mortgage: Loans above $750,000 required a senior underwriter to approve before closing. We built a 2-step approval process: Step 1 — Branch Manager approval. Step 2 — Senior Underwriter approval. Both must approve before the loan status could move to Clear to Close. The approval locked the record from editing during review.

// Submitting a record to an approval process via Apex
Approval.ProcessSubmitRequest submitReq = new Approval.ProcessSubmitRequest();
submitReq.setObjectId(loan.Id);
submitReq.setComments('High-value loan requiring senior approval');
submitReq.setSubmitterId(UserInfo.getUserId());
submitReq.setProcessDefinitionNameOrId('High_Value_Loan_Approval'); // process name

Approval.ProcessResult result = Approval.process(submitReq);
System.debug('Submitted: ' + result.isSuccess() + ' Status: ' + result.getInstanceStatus());
What are Record Types — when do you use them vs picklist values? ADMIN▼

Record Types allow you to offer different business processes, page layouts, and picklist values for different users on the same object. A Record Type is assigned to a profile or permission set — users with that assignment see those page layouts and picklist options.

  • Use Record Types when: Different user groups need different page layouts, different picklist values, or different approval processes on the same object
  • Don't use for: Simple categorisation that doesn't affect UI or process — a picklist field is enough
  • Mortgage example: Loan object with Record Types: Conventional, FHA, VA, USDA. Each shows different fields on page layout, has different picklist values for loan programs, and can have different approval processes
// Getting Record Type Id in Apex
Id fhaRecordTypeId = Schema.SObjectType.Loan__c
    .getRecordTypeInfosByDeveloperName().get('FHA_Loan').getRecordTypeId();

Loan__c fhaLoan = new Loan__c(
    RecordTypeId = fhaRecordTypeId,
    LoanAmount__c = 300000
);

// Better: use DeveloperName not Name (Name can change, DeveloperName is stable)
// Never hardcode the 15-digit Id directly
What is a Record Type — what does it control and not control?ADMIN▼
  • Controls: Page layout per profile, available picklist values, business processes (Lead/Opportunity/Case)
  • Does NOT control: Field-level security (that is Profiles/Permission Sets), which fields exist, record sharing
  • Use case: Loan__c has record types: Conventional, FHA, VA, USDA — each shows different layout and different Status__c picklist values
  • Assignment: Profiles assigned to record types. Users with multiple types see a selection when creating records.
Id fhaId = Schema.SObjectType.Loan__c.getRecordTypeInfosByDeveloperName().get("FHA").getRecordTypeId();
List<Loan__c> fhaLoans = [SELECT Id FROM Loan__c WHERE RecordTypeId = :fhaId];

What are Approval Processes and how do they interact with Apex?ADMIN▼

Approval Processes route records through multi-step approval chains with configurable entry criteria, approvers, and actions on approval/rejection.

// Submit for approval from Apex
Approval.ProcessSubmitRequest req = new Approval.ProcessSubmitRequest();
req.setComments('Submitting for underwriter review');
req.setObjectId(loan.Id);
req.setProcessDefinitionNameOrId('Loan_Approval_Process');
Approval.ProcessResult result = Approval.process(req);

// Approve programmatically (e.g., auto-approve high credit score)
Approval.ProcessWorkitemRequest approveReq = new Approval.ProcessWorkitemRequest();
approveReq.setComments('Auto-approved: score ' + loan.Credit_Score__c);
approveReq.setAction('Approve'); // or 'Reject'
approveReq.setWorkitemId(workItemId);
Approval.process(approveReq);
Approval and locking

When a record is pending approval it may be locked for editing. Check ProcessInstance.Status before allowing edits. Record save during approval still fires before/after triggers normally.

What are the key governor limits that admins must understand?ADMIN▼
  • SOQL queries: 100 sync / 200 async — complex reports can trigger Apex that hits this
  • DML statements: 150 per transaction — mass record operations can fire triggers multiple times
  • Storage limits: Data + file storage per org — monitor in Setup → Company Information
  • API request limits: 24-hour rolling window — excessive integration calls deplete daily allocation
  • Scheduled jobs: Max 100 — orgs with many scheduled processes approach this limit
  • Concurrent API requests: Long-running API calls can time out external integrations
Admin monitoring

Setup → Apex Jobs for batch/scheduled status. Setup → Event Log Files for API usage patterns. Setup → Company Information for storage. These are your first stops for performance investigations.

What is Data Loader vs Import Wizard — when to use each?ADMIN▼
FeatureData LoaderImport Wizard
VolumeUp to 5 million recordsUp to 50,000 records
ObjectsAll standard + customLimited standard objects only
Requires install?Yes — desktop Java appNo — browser-based
Triggers fired?Yes (by default)Yes
Best forLarge loads, automation, upserts by External IDSmall one-time imports of Contacts, Leads
Bulk API vs SOAP API

Data Loader uses Bulk API by default — parallel, async, recommended for large loads. For trigger-sensitive loads use Bulk API Serial mode. Never load large datasets with standard SOAP API — too slow and hits per-request limits.

Scenario-Based Questions — Architect Thinking
16 open-ended design questions — interviewers use these to test senior-level judgment. There is no single right answer — explain your reasoning clearly.
Design a notification system in Salesforce that sends emails, SMS, and push notifications based on loan status changes — how would you architect this? SCENARIO▼

Step 1 — Identify the trigger: Record-Triggered Flow or After Update trigger on Loan__c fires when Status__c changes.

Step 2 — Decouple with Platform Events: Don't call notification services directly from the trigger. Instead, publish a Loan_Status_Changed__e Platform Event. This decouples the loan record update from the notification logic — if the notification service is down, the loan record still saves successfully.

Step 3 — Notification Router: A Platform Event trigger subscribes to Loan_Status_Changed__e and calls a NotificationRouter class. The router reads Notification_Config__mdt (custom metadata) to determine which channels to use for each loan type + status combination.

Step 4 — Channel adapters: Each channel (email, SMS via Twilio, push via Salesforce Notification Builder) is implemented as a separate class behind an INotificationChannel interface. The router loops through the configured channels and calls each.

Step 5 — Error handling: Use a Notification_Log__c custom object to record every notification attempt — status, timestamp, error if any. A scheduled job retries failed notifications up to 3 times.

Key patterns demonstrated

Platform Events (decoupling), Custom Metadata (config-driven, no code changes for new rules), Strategy pattern (INotificationChannel), Error logging, Retry logic. This answer shows senior architect thinking — not just "call Messaging.sendEmail."

A batch job that processes 2 million loan records is timing out. How do you diagnose and fix it? SCENARIO▼

Step 1 — Diagnose first: Check the AsyncApexJob record to see which chunk failed. Enable debug logs for the batch user. Look at which line hits the time limit — use Limits.getCpuTime() at different points to narrow it down.

Common causes for slow batch on 2M records:

  • SOQL inside execute() loop — query runs per chunk instead of once
  • Scope size too large (default 200) — each chunk takes too long. Try smaller scope: Database.executeBatch(job, 50)
  • Triggers firing on DML inside execute() — your batch update fires a trigger that does expensive work per record
  • Complex formula fields being evaluated on every query
  • String concatenation or regex in a loop inside execute()

Fixes:

  • Reduce scope size — more chunks, less work per chunk
  • Move static SOQL lookups to start() — pass data into execute() via instance variables and Database.Stateful
  • Use TriggerHandler.bypass() before the update DML in execute() to skip expensive trigger logic during batch runs
  • Split into multiple smaller batch jobs chained from finish()
  • Use Database.QueryLocator with a more selective WHERE clause — process only records that actually need changes
How would you migrate 500,000 records from a legacy system into Salesforce without affecting production users? SCENARIO▼

Strategy: Phased migration with validation gates

  • Phase 1 — Mapping: Map source system fields to Salesforce fields. Identify data quality issues in source (nulls, duplicates, invalid formats). Clean data before migrating.
  • Phase 2 — Sandbox test: Load 1,000 sample records into Full Sandbox. Verify triggers, workflows, validation rules behave correctly. Fix any issues found.
  • Phase 3 — Disable automation: Before production load, bypass triggers using a custom Boolean field or TriggerHandler.bypass(). Disable validation rules that would block historical data (e.g., TRID date fields from 2010 don't meet today's rules). Use a Migration Mode custom setting to conditionally skip logic.
  • Phase 4 — Load in batches: Use Salesforce Bulk API 2.0 — it handles 500K records efficiently. Load in batches of 10,000 with error logs. Do not load everything in one job — if it fails mid-way, you need to know exactly which records succeeded.
  • Phase 5 — Validate after load: Run COUNT queries to verify record counts. Spot-check 50 random records against source system. Run data quality reports.
  • Phase 6 — Re-enable automation: Turn triggers and validation rules back on. Monitor for the first week post-migration for unexpected issues.
A batch job processing 2 million loan records is timing out — how do you fix it?SCENARIO▼

Diagnose first — different root causes need different fixes:

  • Check debug logs: Is it CPU limit (60s async)? SOQL limit? Heap? Each has a different fix.
  • Check scope size: If 200, reduce to 50 and see if chunks complete. Narrowing scope isolates the problem.
  • Query Plan on start() SOQL: If doing a TableScan on 2M records, the query itself times out before execute() runs.
  • Callouts in execute(): If making one callout per record in a 200-record chunk, that is 200 callouts hitting the 100/tx limit.

Fixes by root cause:

  • CPU timeout: Profile with Limits.getCpuTime() at key points. Replace nested loops with Map lookups. Move heavy logic to helper methods.
  • SOQL in execute(): Move queries to start() or cache in Database.Stateful variable.
  • Callouts per record: Collect all IDs in execute(), make one batched API call in finish() or chain to Queueable.
  • Table scan: Add indexed field to WHERE clause. Contact Salesforce for custom index on large objects.
  • Scope too large: Reduce to 50-100. More transactions but each completes within limits.
How do you design a notification system for loan status changes?SCENARIO▼

Requirements: email, SMS, push. Multiple loan types. Configurable rules per status transition.

Architecture — decoupled, configuration-driven:

  • Trigger: After update on Loan__c — if Status__c changed, publish Loan_Status_Changed__e Platform Event. Nothing else in trigger.
  • Configuration: Notification_Config__mdt rows defining: LoanType, FromStatus, ToStatus, Channels, RecipientType. No code change to add rules.
  • Router class: Platform Event trigger queries CMDT for matching rules. For each rule, calls INotificationChannel.send() — Strategy pattern.
  • Channel adapters: EmailAdapter (Salesforce Messaging), SMSAdapter (Twilio via Named Credential), PushAdapter (Notification Builder).
  • Error handling: Every attempt logged to Notification_Log__c. Failed sends retried by Scheduled Apex up to 3 times.
Patterns used

Platform Events (decoupling), Custom Metadata (configuration-driven), Strategy Pattern (INotificationChannel), Error logging, Retry logic. Naming all four patterns shows senior architect thinking.

How would you design bidirectional sync between Salesforce and a Loan Origination System?SCENARIO▼

Salesforce to LOS (outbound):

  • After-Save Record-Triggered Flow or trigger detects change on Loan__c
  • Publishes Loan_Sync_Request__e Platform Event — decouples loan save from LOS call
  • Platform Event subscriber trigger enqueues Queueable with Database.AllowsCallouts
  • Queueable makes REST callout to LOS API with idempotency key
  • On failure: writes to Sync_Retry_Queue__c. Scheduled Apex retries every 15 min up to 3 times

LOS to Salesforce (inbound):

  • LOS sends webhook: POST /services/apexrest/v1/loanSync
  • Apex @RestResource validates HMAC signature, upserts using LOS_ID__c External ID field
  • Returns HTTP 200 success, 400 bad payload, 500 error

Conflict resolution: Last_Sync_Source__c field (LOS or SFDC) + Last_Sync_Timestamp__c. Business rule: Salesforce wins for status changes, LOS wins for rate locks and financial figures.

30-Day Communication Training Plan
Daily structured exercises — follow these in order. Every day has a specific skill to build. Communication is trainable — it gets better every day you practice.
WEEK 1 — DAYS 1–7: Foundation — Voice, Pace, and Clarity
Day 1Record your baseline self-introductionRecord a 2-minute video of yourself introducing yourself in English as if in a real interview. Watch it fully without stopping. Write down every filler word you used (um, uh, basically, you know, right?) and every time you rushed. This is your starting point. Do not judge — just observe. Keep this recording.
Day 2Read aloud for 30 min — focus on PACEOpen a tech article. Read it out loud at HALF your normal speed. This feels uncomfortable — that is correct. Slow pace sounds like confidence. Fast pace sounds like nervousness. Record 2 minutes and check: can someone who speaks slowly understand every word clearly? Do this every morning before study.
Day 3Eliminate filler words — one day challengeFrom yesterday's recording, identify your top 2 filler words. Today's entire challenge: do not say those words even in casual conversation. If you catch yourself saying one, pause, breathe, and rephrase. Replace "basically" with nothing — just say the thing directly. Replace "um" with a 1-second pause and a breath.
Day 4Explain one technical concept to an imaginary non-technical personPick a Salesforce concept — governor limits, for example. Explain it out loud as if talking to someone who has never coded. Use an analogy. "Governor limits are like a restaurant that only serves 100 customers per day — once full, no more orders accepted." This trains clarity of thinking, which is what interviewers actually assess when they ask technical questions.
Day 5Answer 3 interview questions — record — watch — redo each onePick 3 technical questions from this guide. Answer each one recorded. Watch immediately. For each answer note: Did I start clearly? Did I give an example? Did I have a clear ending? Redo each answer once with the improvements applied. 3 answers × 2 attempts = 6 spoken answers today.
Day 6Practice the power pauseThe power pause is 2–3 seconds of silence before you answer a question. It signals you are thinking — not that you don't know. Practice this today: ask yourself a question out loud, then deliberately pause for 3 full seconds before starting your answer. It will feel very strange. Do it 10 times. In a real interview, this pause sounds like confidence.
Day 7Full self-introduction — record 5 times with different energy levelsRecord your 2-minute self-introduction 5 times in a row. Each time, change the energy level: bored → normal → confident → enthusiastic → warm and natural. Watch all 5. Which version sounds most like someone you would want to work with? That is the energy to aim for in interviews. Hint: it is usually version 4 — enthusiastic but controlled.
WEEK 2 — DAYS 8–14: Structure — Answers That Have a Point
Day 8Learn the PREP framework for every technical answerPREP: Point → Reason → Example → Point again. For every technical question, start with the direct answer (Point), then explain why (Reason), give a real code or project example (Example), then close by restating the key point (Point again). Practice with: "What is bulkification?" Point: "Bulkification means writing Apex that handles batches of records efficiently." Reason: "Because Salesforce triggers can receive up to 200 records at once." Example: "For example, in my trigger I collect all account IDs in a Set first, query once with IN clause, then process from a Map." Point again: "The rule is: never put SOQL or DML inside a for loop."
Day 9Practice connecting your answer to YOUR projectEvery technical answer should end with: "...and I used this in my project when..." Take 5 questions from this guide. Answer each using PREP, and force yourself to end with a real project connection. If you cannot connect it to your project, it becomes an abstract fact. If you can connect it, it becomes evidence of real experience.
Day 10Practice the STAR method — tell 2 stories completelyPick 2 STAR stories from the Behavioral section. Tell each one out loud fully, from memory, without reading. Use a stopwatch. Target: 2 to 2.5 minutes each. If you go under 90 seconds — add the Situation and Task with more detail. If you go over 3 minutes — cut the Action section. The Result must always be a number or measurable outcome.
Day 11Answer "Tell me about yourself" 10 different waysSay your self-introduction 10 times today. Each time change ONE thing: what you open with, the project you emphasize, what skill you highlight, how you end it. By attempt 10, you will know your introduction so well it will always sound natural, never memorized — because you will have practiced so many versions that you truly own it.
Day 12Practice handling "I don't know" gracefully — 5 exercisesPick 5 topics you are genuinely less confident about. For each, practice the "I don't know" recovery framework: 1. Acknowledge honestly. 2. Connect to what you DO know. 3. Reason through it. 4. Express willingness to learn. Example: "I haven't implemented Salesforce CDP directly, but based on my experience with streaming data via Platform Events and customer segmentation logic in Apex, I would approach it by..." Practice this 5 times today.
Day 13Vocabulary in context — use today's 3 new words 10 times eachPick 3 words from the Vocab section. For each word, make 10 different sentences using it in a Salesforce or interview context. WEEK 3 — DAYS 15–21: Depth — Sounding Like a Senior Developer
Day 15Practice "Yes, and..." instead of just "Yes"Senior developers don't just answer — they add context, tradeoffs, and alternatives. Practice upgrading short answers: Q: "Do you use with sharing in your Apex classes?" Bad: "Yes." Good: "Yes, I always declare sharing explicitly. I use 'with sharing' for service classes that run in user context, 'without sharing' for system-level batch jobs that need to access all records, and 'inherited sharing' for utility classes that should adapt to the caller's context. Being explicit makes the code's security intent clear to anyone reading it." Practice this upgrade for 5 answers today.
Day 16Practice explaining tradeoffs — "It depends, and here is why"Senior devs think in tradeoffs, not absolutes. Pick 5 questions with comparison answers (wire vs imperative, future vs queueable, Flow vs Trigger, etc). For each, practice saying "It depends on the situation, and here is how I decide..." then explain the tradeoffs. Never give a single answer when the correct answer is "it depends on X, Y, Z."

Practice doing this for 10 minutes without stopping. Clear verbal explanation of a diagram is a senior developer skill.

Then replay and see: did you cover the key points? Did you get to the example? This trains you to prioritize the most important information first — essential in real interviews where the interviewer may interrupt or redirect.

Practice starting your answers with these phrases today. Compare how your answers sound vs starting with "So basically..." or "Um, I think..."

Interviewer says "I don't think that approach would scale." 3. "Our team uses a different pattern — do you see any problems with your approach?" Practice responding calmly, not defensively: "That is a fair point — let me add more detail..." / "You raise a good concern. I should clarify..." / "I appreciate that perspective — the reason I chose this approach was..." Practice 10 minutes today.
