How do you handle a question when you do not know the answer?COMM PLAN▼
  • Never stop at I don't know: Always bridge to what you DO know and how you would solve it.
  • Acknowledge the gap directly: 'I have not used that specific feature directly in production'
  • Bridge to related knowledge: 'However, based on my experience with [similar concept], I would expect it to work by [reasoning]'
  • Show your process: 'I would verify this in documentation and test in sandbox before implementing'
  • Never guess confidently: If you are unsure, say you are unsure. Wrong confident answer damages credibility more than an honest gap.
Practice phrase

"That is a good area — I have not worked with it directly in production. Based on my experience with Platform Events and async Apex, I would expect it to work like... I would verify this in the documentation before building it."

How do you structure a technical answer to sound senior — the PREP framework?COMM PLAN▼

PREP: Point → Reason → Example → Point

POINT — Lead with the answer (not a warm-up):

"Bulkification means writing Apex that processes all records in a batch with the same number of SOQL and DML operations, regardless of how many records there are."

REASON — Why does it matter:

"Salesforce can pass 200 records to a trigger at once. If you query inside a for loop, 200 records means 200 SOQL queries. The limit is 100 — you hit it on record 101."

EXAMPLE — Your real project:

"In our mortgage platform, the Loan trigger processes Account updates in bulk. I collect all Account IDs first into a Set, query once with an IN clause, build a Map, then process all records from the Map — zero additional SOQL in the loop."

POINT — Close definitively:

"The rule: no SOQL or DML inside a for loop. Ever."

Clean close matters

Senior developers close answers definitively. Do not trail off or add '...and stuff like that'. End with a clear statement: 'That is the approach I use' or 'That is the rule I follow in production'.

What are the most powerful phrases to start technical answers?COMM PLAN▼

Replace 'So basically...' and 'Um, like...' with:

  • "In my experience building mortgage systems..."
  • "What I have found in production is..."
  • "The approach I take is..."
  • "Let me give you a concrete example from my project..."
  • "The key thing to understand here is..."
  • "In the Loan Origination Platform, we solved this by..."
  • "The tradeoff I always consider is..."

For tradeoff questions — the senior structure:

  • "It depends on the context. Here is how I decide..."
  • "Both have valid use cases. For [condition A] I use X because... for [condition B] I use Y because..."
  • "In our mortgage platform, we chose X specifically because our volume was [data]..."
Avoid these phrases

"To be honest" implies you were not honest before. "Actually" sounds defensive. "Basically" sounds junior. "You know" and "right?" are filler. Replace all of them with a pause and a direct statement.

How do you handle silence and the power pause technique?COMM PLAN▼
  • The Power Pause: After a question is asked, take one deliberate breath and count 2-3 seconds before speaking. This signals confidence — not confusion.
  • Thinking out loud: 'Let me think through this the way I would approach it in a real design session...' — buys thinking time while demonstrating senior process.
  • Clarify before answering: 'Just to confirm — you are asking about [X] in the context of [Y]? Good. So...' — demonstrates active listening, buys a moment.
  • When you feel the urge to say 'um': STOP. Pause. Breathe. Continue. Silence is always better than filler words.
  • Slow down: Nervous speech is fast speech. Confident speech is deliberate. Speak at 70% of your natural pace during answers.
Record yourself

Record video of 5 answers. Watch without sound first — does your body language look confident? Then watch with sound and count every filler word. This reveals habits you cannot hear in real time.

How do you end an interview answer strongly — clean close technique?COMM PLAN▼

Most candidates trail off at the end. Senior candidates close decisively.

Weak endings:

  • "...and stuff like that, you know..." (trailing off)
  • "...I think that is the answer, I guess..." (uncertain)
  • "...yeah, so, basically that is how it works..." (filler)

Strong endings:

  • "That is the approach I use in production — and it has served us well."
  • "The result was [specific metric]. That is how I measure the impact."
  • "Does that answer your question, or would you like me to go deeper on any part?"
  • "In summary: [one sentence]. That is the decision I would make."
  • "That is my experience with this specifically in the US mortgage domain."
Speaking Drills — Daily Practice Exercises
40 exercises grouped by skill. Do 2–3 from this page every day during your communication blocks. Each drill has a specific purpose.

Drill Group 1 — Pronunciation & Clarity (Do these first, Days 1–10)

Drill 1: Technical Word Pronunciation

Say each word 5 times, slowly and clearly:

  • Asynchronous → a-SIN-kro-nus
  • Governor → GUV-er-nor
  • Idempotent → eye-DEM-po-tent
  • Abstraction → ab-STRAK-shun
  • Inheritance → in-HEH-ri-tance
  • Polymorphism → poly-MOR-fizm
  • Serialization → see-ree-al-eye-ZAY-shun
  • Authentication → aw-then-ti-KAY-shun
Drill 2: Sentence Stress Practice

Read each sentence stressing the BOLD word — then change which word you stress and notice how meaning changes:

  • I designed the trigger handler pattern.
  • I designed the trigger handler pattern.
  • I designed the trigger handler pattern.
  • I designed the trigger handler pattern.

Each stress communicates something different about your contribution. In interviews, stress the action verb and the result.

Drill 3: Pause Instead of Filler

Read this script with intentional pauses (marked with | ) instead of filler words:

"Governor limits | are runtime restrictions | that Salesforce enforces | on every Apex transaction. | The most important ones | are 100 SOQL queries | 150 DML statements | and 10,000 milliseconds of CPU time. | The reason these exist | is that Salesforce is a multitenant platform | and shared resources must be protected."

Each | is a 1-second pause. Practice until the pauses feel natural.

Drill 4: Tongue Twisters for English Clarity

Say each 3 times, getting faster:

  • "The Salesforce system synchronously sends six SOQL statements."
  • "Bulkified batch jobs build better bulk-safe business logic."
  • "Trigger handlers thoroughly throttle the transaction through."
  • "Asynchronous Apex avoids all aggregate API access issues."

Drill Group 2 — Answer Structure (Days 11–20)

Drill 5: The 30-Second Answer

Answer each question in exactly 30 seconds — no more, no less. Set a timer. This trains conciseness. Questions to practice:

  • What is a trigger?
  • What is LWC?
  • What is bulkification?
  • What is a Named Credential?
  • What is Platform Events?

30 seconds forces you to give only the most important point. Build from there.

Drill 6: Start With the Answer

Most candidates explain background first, then give the answer. Interviewers want the answer FIRST.

Bad: "So in Salesforce there are these limits that exist because of the multitenant nature and basically governor limits are..."

Good: "Governor limits are runtime limits that Salesforce enforces on every Apex transaction. They exist because Salesforce is multitenant. The most critical ones are..."

Practice reversing 5 of your existing answers to start with the direct answer.

Drill 7: Add the "Why" to every answer

After every statement of fact, add "because..." or "the reason for this is..." Practice:

  • "I use with sharing in all service classes because it ensures the running user's record visibility is enforced, preventing data leakage."
  • "I prefer Queueable over Future because Queueable supports sObject parameters, job monitoring, and chaining — none of which Future provides."

The "because" is what separates a senior developer answer from a junior one.

Drill 8: The Project Bridge

After every technical answer, practice bridging to your project: "...and a real example of this from my work is..." or "...I used this exact pattern in the Mortgage Origination Platform when..."

Practice bridging these concepts to your real projects:

  • Named Credentials → Experian integration
  • Platform Events → DocuSign triggering
  • Queueable chaining → credit check → OFAC → DocuSign
  • Batch Apex → loan status updates
  • TriggerHandler pattern → ContactTrigger

Drill Group 3 — Confidence and Presence (Days 21–30)

Drill 9: Answer Tough Questions Without Apologising

Never say "I'm sorry, I don't know" or "I apologise, I haven't used that." It sounds weak and communicates lack of confidence.

Instead practice: "That is a great area — I haven't worked with that specific feature directly, but based on my understanding of [related concept], I would approach it by [reasoned thinking]."

Practice this pattern 10 times with topics you are genuinely less familiar with.

Drill 10: Mirror Practice

Stand in front of a mirror. Answer 3 questions while watching your own face. Notice:

  • Do you make eye contact with yourself? (In interviews: with the camera or interviewer)
  • Does your face look engaged and confident, or flat and worried?
  • Are you nodding slightly while speaking? (Natural, conversational signal)
  • Are your shoulders relaxed or raised with tension?

Body language affects how your voice sounds. Relaxed body = relaxed, confident voice.

Drill 11: Restate Complex Questions Before Answering

Practice this for complex scenario questions: "So if I understand correctly, you are asking me to design a system that does X and handles Y — is that right?" Then answer.

This signals clarity of thought, active listening, and ensures you answer the actual question. Interviewers appreciate this. Practice with 5 scenario questions from this guide today.

Drill 12: The Energy Reset

When you are tired or nervous before a mock interview session, do this: Stand up, take 5 deep slow breaths, say out loud "I know this material. I have built these systems. I am ready." Then sit down and start. This sounds simple but the physical act of standing, breathing, and speaking a confident statement before speaking literally changes your voice quality and composure. Use this before every real interview call too.

Common Communication Mistakes — Fix These
These are the exact patterns a senior technical interviewer notices. Identify which ones you do and practice eliminating them one by one.
Mistake 1: Starting every answer with "So..." or "Basically..." COMM▼

Why it hurts you: "So basically" signals an unconfident, casual answer coming. It also wastes 2 seconds before you have said anything meaningful. Senior developers are expected to answer directly and precisely.

What you say now: "So basically, governor limits are like... limits that Salesforce puts on your code so it doesn't use too many resources."

What to say instead: "Governor limits are runtime restrictions that Salesforce enforces on every Apex transaction. They exist because Salesforce is a multitenant platform and resources are shared across customers. The most critical limits are..."

Practice fix

Every time you catch yourself starting with "so" or "basically" today — stop, pause 1 second, and restart the sentence. Do this drill for 3 days straight. It disappears faster than you expect with deliberate practice.

Mistake 2: Answering without giving a concrete example from your work COMM▼

Why it hurts you: Textbook answers without examples sound like you memorised a definition but may never have actually used the concept. Senior developers are hired for real experience, not theoretical knowledge.

What you say now: "Bulkification is when you write code that processes multiple records at once instead of one at a time, to avoid hitting governor limits."

What to say instead: "Bulkification means writing Apex that handles batches of records safely within governor limits. In practice, the rule is: never put SOQL or DML inside a for loop. In my Mortgage Platform, when the Contact trigger needed to sync phone numbers to Account, I collected all Account IDs into a Set first, ran one SOQL query outside the loop, stored results in a Map, and then processed everything from that Map. One SOQL call handled 200 records safely."

The rule

Every technical answer must end with "...and I used this in my [project name] when [specific scenario]." No exceptions. You have 4 years of real experience — use it in every answer.

Mistake 3: Speaking too fast when nervous COMM▼

Why it hurts you: Fast speech in an interview signals nervousness to the interviewer. It also makes you harder to understand, especially on a video call where audio quality may not be perfect. And it causes you to skip important details and make your answers shallower than they should be.

Signs you are speaking too fast: You finish a 2-minute answer in 45 seconds. You cannot hear yourself think during the answer. You say "um" frequently (filling the spaces your brain needs to process at speed). You forget key points you wanted to make.

The fix — 3-step technique:

  • Before you answer: Take one slow breath. This physically slows your heart rate and resets your pace.
  • After every major point: Pause for 1 full second. This is not awkward silence — it is the natural rhythm of thoughtful speech.
  • Read your answer aloud at 70% of your normal speaking speed. What feels too slow to you sounds exactly right to a listener.
Daily drill

Read one paragraph of any article at what feels like uncomfortably slow speed. Record it. Play it back. You will be surprised how natural it sounds even though it felt slow. Do this for 5 minutes every morning during communication block 1.

Mistake 4: Saying "we did this" instead of "I did this" COMM▼

Why it hurts you: Interviewers cannot hire a team — they are hiring you. When you say "we built this" for everything, the interviewer wonders what YOUR specific contribution actually was. This is especially critical for your solo project — the Loan Officer Campaign Platform — where you did everything yourself.

Weak (hides your contribution): "We integrated Experian into the platform. We used Named Credentials and Queueable Apex for the callout."

Strong (clear ownership): "I designed and built the Experian credit bureau integration end-to-end. I chose Named Credentials for authentication to avoid hardcoding credentials, and I implemented it using Queueable Apex because we needed callout capability after trigger DML. I also wrote the HttpCalloutMock test class achieving 95% coverage."

The rule

Use "I" when describing your decisions, your code, your architecture. Use "we" only for team-level outcomes: "We delivered the project on time" or "Our team adopted LWC after I demonstrated the performance benefits." Own your individual contribution clearly.

Mistake 5: Ending answers with "...yeah" or letting the answer trail off COMM▼

Why it hurts you: When your answer trails off with "...yeah" or "...so that's basically it" or "...yeah that's all," it signals you are not sure you said enough. It invites the interviewer to judge whether your answer was complete. A strong, decisive close signals confidence.

Weak endings: "...and that's how you do bulkification, yeah." / "...so that's kind of how the trigger works, I think." / "...yeah so basically that's what SOQL injection is."

Strong endings — choose one per answer:

  • "...That is the core principle — and it is something I apply to every trigger I write."
  • "...The result was [outcome]. That approach has served me well across multiple projects."
  • "...Does that answer your question, or would you like me to go deeper on any part of that?"
  • "...That is how I approach it in production code." (then stop — do not add "yeah" or "so.")
Practice

Record 5 of your answers. Listen only to the last 10 seconds of each one. Fix every weak ending. The close of your answer is the last thing the interviewer hears before forming their judgment — make it count.

Mistake 6: Not asking clarifying questions on design/scenario questions COMM▼

Why it hurts you: When an interviewer asks "Design a loan notification system," jumping straight into one design without asking clarifying questions shows junior thinking. Senior developers always clarify requirements before designing.

What junior devs do: Immediately start describing a solution based on assumptions.

What senior devs do: Ask 3–4 clarifying questions first:

  • "What notification channels are needed — email only, or also SMS and push?"
  • "What is the expected volume — hundreds of loans per day or thousands?"
  • "Does this need real-time delivery or is a few-minute delay acceptable?"
  • "Are there existing external systems we need to integrate with, or is this Salesforce-native?"

After getting answers: "Based on what you have described, I would architect this as follows..." THEN give your design.

What this signals

Clarifying questions signal that you think before you build — exactly what a senior developer should do. Interviewers often intentionally leave design questions vague to see if candidates make assumptions or gather requirements first.

Objects & Fields — 10 Scenario Questions
Real interview scenarios on the Salesforce data model — know these cold for 4-year experience level
What is the difference between Lookup and Master-Detail? Give a scenario for each — with code.OBJECTS▼
FeatureLookupMaster-Detail
Parent required?No — child can exist without parentYES — mandatory, cannot be null
Delete parentChild stays, lookup goes nullChild records DELETED (cascade delete)
Rollup SummaryNOT availableAvailable — COUNT, SUM, MIN, MAX
SharingChild has own OWD/sharingChild inherits parent's sharing
ReparentingAllowed (change parent anytime)Restricted (controlled per MDR setting)
Max per object25 lookups2 master-detail
// Mortgage example: Lookup — Contact to Account
// Contact can exist without Account (orphan contact is valid)
Contact c = [SELECT Id, AccountId FROM Contact WHERE AccountId = null]; // valid query

// Master-Detail — Loan__c to Application__c
// Loan cannot exist without Application — Application__c field is required
// Rollup on Application: Total_Loan_Amount__c = SUM(Loan__c.LoanAmount__c)
Loan__c orphanLoan = new Loan__c(LoanAmount__c=100000);
// insert orphanLoan;  // FAILS — Application__c is required (master-detail)

// Delete cascade — delete Application deletes ALL its Loans automatically
delete [SELECT Id FROM Application__c WHERE Id = :appId];
// All child Loan__c records are now deleted too
Interview connection

Say: "In my Mortgage Platform, Loan__c had a Master-Detail to Application__c because a loan cannot logically exist without an application, and we needed a Rollup Summary to show total funded amount on the Application record. For the Contact-to-Account relationship we used Lookup because a loan officer (Contact) could exist in the system before being assigned to a broker office (Account)."

How do you implement a many-to-many relationship using a Junction Object — build one for the mortgage domain?OBJECTS▼

Salesforce does not support many-to-many natively. Use a Junction Object — a custom object with two Master-Detail fields pointing to each parent.

// Scenario: A Loan can have multiple Borrowers (Primary, Co-Borrower)
// A Contact (Borrower) can be on multiple Loans

// Junction Object: Loan_Borrower__c
// Field 1: Loan__c       — Master-Detail to Loan__c
// Field 2: Borrower__c   — Master-Detail to Contact
// Field 3: Role__c       — Picklist: Primary Borrower, Co-Borrower, Guarantor
// Field 4: CreditScore__c — Number (borrower's FICO at time of application)

// Query all borrowers on a loan:
List<Loan_Borrower__c> borrowers = [
    SELECT Borrower__r.Name, Borrower__r.Email, Role__c, CreditScore__c
    FROM Loan_Borrower__c
    WHERE Loan__c = :loanId
    ORDER BY Role__c
];

// Query all loans for a borrower (Contact):
List<Loan_Borrower__c> theirLoans = [
    SELECT Loan__r.Name, Loan__r.LoanAmount__c, Loan__r.Status__c, Role__c
    FROM Loan_Borrower__c
    WHERE Borrower__c = :contactId
    ORDER BY Loan__r.CreatedDate DESC
];

// Parent-to-Child subquery from Loan:
List<Loan__c> loansWithBorrowers = [
    SELECT Id, Name,
           (SELECT Borrower__r.Name, Role__c FROM Loan_Borrowers__r)
    FROM Loan__c WHERE Id = :loanId
];
What are External ID fields — when and how do you use them in integrations?OBJECTS▼

External ID fields store a unique identifier from an external system. Salesforce indexes them for fast lookups. They are critical for upsert operations in integrations — you can upsert by External ID instead of Salesforce Id.

// Field: Loan__c.LOS_Loan_ID__c (External ID, Unique, Text)
// Stores the ID from an external Loan Origination System

// UPSERT — matches on External ID, inserts if not found, updates if found
Loan__c loan = new Loan__c(
    LOS_Loan_ID__c = '2024-LOS-78542',
    LoanAmount__c = 350000,
    Status__c = 'Processing'
);
Database.upsert(loan, Loan__c.LOS_Loan_ID__c, false);
// false = partial mode — does not throw for single failures

// Bulk upsert from LOS integration:
List<Database.UpsertResult> results = Database.upsert(loansFromLOS, Loan__c.LOS_Loan_ID__c, false);

// REST API equivalent:
// PATCH /services/data/v59.0/sobjects/Loan__c/LOS_Loan_ID__c/2024-LOS-78542
// Body: {"LoanAmount__c": 350000, "Status__c": "Processing"}

// Best practices:
// Max 25 External ID fields per object
// Field types: Text, Number, Email, Auto Number only
// Mark as Unique to enforce no duplicates
// Do NOT use Id field as External ID — use a meaningful business identifier
Real use from your project

In the Mortgage Origination Platform, when syncing from the external LOS, we upserted on LOS_Loan_ID__c instead of Salesforce Id. This meant the integration never needed to query Salesforce first to find the record — just upsert with the LOS ID and Salesforce handled insert vs update automatically.

What is a Rollup Summary field — what are its limitations? How do you replicate it on a Lookup relationship?OBJECTS▼

Rollup Summary fields aggregate child record values (COUNT, SUM, MIN, MAX) onto a parent object. They only work on Master-Detail relationships.

  • Supported functions: COUNT, SUM, MIN, MAX
  • Can filter which child records to include (like a WHERE clause)
  • Update automatically when child records change — asynchronous on bulk loads
  • Cannot roll up formula fields or fields two levels away
  • Not available on Lookup relationships
// Replicating Rollup on a Lookup relationship using Apex trigger
// Scenario: Account (parent lookup) ← Contact (child)
// Goal: Count active contacts on Account.Active_Contact_Count__c

trigger ContactRollupTrigger on Contact (after insert, after update, after delete, after undelete) {

    Set<Id> accountIds = new Set<Id>();
    List<Contact> allContacts = Trigger.new != null ? Trigger.new : Trigger.old;
    for(Contact c : allContacts) {
        if(c.AccountId != null) accountIds.add(c.AccountId);
    }
    // Also include old AccountId on updates (contact moved to different account)
    if(Trigger.old != null) {
        for(Contact c : Trigger.old) {
            if(c.AccountId != null) accountIds.add(c.AccountId);
        }
    }
    if(accountIds.isEmpty()) return;

    List<AggregateResult> counts = [
        SELECT AccountId, COUNT(Id) cnt
        FROM Contact
        WHERE AccountId IN :accountIds AND IsActive__c = true
        GROUP BY AccountId
    ];

    Map<Id, Integer> countMap = new Map<Id, Integer>();
    for(AggregateResult ar : counts) countMap.put((Id)ar.get('AccountId'), (Integer)ar.get('cnt'));

    List<Account> toUpdate = new List<Account>();
    for(Id aId : accountIds) {
        toUpdate.add(new Account(Id=aId, Active_Contact_Count__c = countMap.containsKey(aId) ? countMap.get(aId) : 0));
    }
    update toUpdate;
}
How do field dependencies (controlling and dependent picklists) work — implement in LWC?OBJECTS▼

Field dependencies link two picklist fields. The Controlling field determines which values appear in the Dependent field. With lightning-record-edit-form, this works automatically. With custom lightning-combobox elements, you must filter manually in JavaScript.

// Custom LWC — filter dependent picklist manually
// Controlling: LoanType__c | Dependent: LoanProgram__c

handleLoanTypeChange(event) {
    this.selectedLoanType = event.detail.value;
    const programMap = {
        'Conventional': ['Conforming 30yr', 'Conforming 15yr', 'Jumbo'],
        'FHA': ['FHA 30yr', 'FHA 15yr', 'FHA Streamline'],
        'VA': ['VA 30yr', 'VA IRRRL', 'VA Jumbo'],
        'USDA': ['USDA Rural']
    };
    const programs = programMap[this.selectedLoanType] || [];
    this.loanProgramOptions = programs.map(p => ({ label: p, value: p }));
    this.selectedLoanProgram = '';
}

// Get picklist values from Salesforce metadata at runtime (best approach):
import { getPicklistValues, getObjectInfo } from 'lightning/uiObjectInfoApi';
import LOAN_PROGRAM from '@salesforce/schema/Loan__c.LoanProgram__c';

@wire(getObjectInfo, { objectApiName: 'Loan__c' }) objectInfo;

@wire(getPicklistValues, {
    recordTypeId: '$objectInfo.data.defaultRecordTypeId',
    fieldApiName: LOAN_PROGRAM
})
loanProgramPicklist({ data }) {
    // data.controllerValues maps controlling picklist value to index
    // data.values[i].validFor is a bitmask matching controller indices
    // Use this to filter dependent values based on selected controlling value
    this.allLoanPrograms = data;
}
How do you get all field metadata at runtime using Schema describe — use case: dynamic form builder?OBJECTS▼
// Get all fields and their properties at runtime
Map<String, Schema.SObjectField> fieldMap =
    Schema.SObjectType.Loan__c.fields.getMap();

List<Map<String,Object>> fieldData = new List<Map<String,Object>>();
for(String fieldName : fieldMap.keySet()) {
    Schema.DescribeFieldResult dfr = fieldMap.get(fieldName).getDescribe();
    if(!dfr.isAccessible()) continue; // skip inaccessible fields
    fieldData.add(new Map<String,Object>{
        'apiName' => dfr.getName(),
        'label' => dfr.getLabel(),
        'type' => String.valueOf(dfr.getType()),
        'required' => !dfr.isNillable() && dfr.isCreateable(),
        'updateable' => dfr.isUpdateable(),
        'length' => dfr.getLength()
    });
}

// Get picklist values at runtime — for dynamic dropdowns
List<Schema.PicklistEntry> statusVals = Loan__c.Status__c.getDescribe().getPicklistValues();
for(Schema.PicklistEntry pv : statusVals) {
    System.debug(pv.getValue() + ' — active: ' + pv.isActive() + ' default: ' + pv.isDefaultValue());
}

// Expose to LWC:
@AuraEnabled(cacheable=true)
public static List<Map<String,String>> getLoanStatusOptions() {
    List<Map<String,String>> opts = new List<Map<String,String>>();
    for(Schema.PicklistEntry e : Loan__c.Status__c.getDescribe().getPicklistValues()) {
        if(e.isActive()) opts.add(new Map<String,String>{'label'=>e.getLabel(),'value'=>e.getValue()});
    }
    return opts;
}
What are Record Types — when do you use them and how do you get the ID in Apex?OBJECTS▼

Record Types allow different page layouts, picklist values, and business processes per user type. In mortgage: Loan__c could have Record Types for Conventional, FHA, VA, USDA — each showing different fields and picklist values.

// Get Record Type Id — ALWAYS use DeveloperName, not Name
// Name can change (admin renames it), DeveloperName is stable
Id fhaRTId = Schema.SObjectType.Loan__c
    .getRecordTypeInfosByDeveloperName().get('FHA_Loan').getRecordTypeId();

Loan__c fhaLoan = new Loan__c(
    RecordTypeId = fhaRTId,
    LoanAmount__c = 300000
);

// Get all active record types for an object:
Map<String, Schema.RecordTypeInfo> rtMap =
    Schema.SObjectType.Loan__c.getRecordTypeInfosByDeveloperName();
for(String devName : rtMap.keySet()) {
    Schema.RecordTypeInfo rti = rtMap.get(devName);
    if(rti.isActive() && !rti.isMaster()) {
        System.debug(rti.getName() + ' → ' + rti.getRecordTypeId());
    }
}

// In LWC — use getObjectInfo wire adapter to get record types available to the user
import { getObjectInfo } from 'lightning/uiObjectInfoApi';
import LOAN_OBJECT from '@salesforce/schema/Loan__c';

@wire(getObjectInfo, { objectApiName: LOAN_OBJECT })
objectInfo;

get recordTypeOptions() {
    if(!this.objectInfo?.data) return [];
    return Object.values(this.objectInfo.data.recordTypeInfos)
        .filter(rt => rt.available && !rt.master)
        .map(rt => ({ label: rt.name, value: rt.recordTypeId }));
}
How do you implement a self-relationship (hierarchical data) in Salesforce — example with org chart?OBJECTS▼

A self-relationship is a lookup field on an object that points back to the same object. The Account.ParentId is the classic example. You can create custom self-lookups for hierarchical data like territory trees or category trees.

// Standard self-relationship: Account → ParentAccount
Account branch = [
    SELECT Id, Name, Parent.Name, Parent.Parent.Name
    FROM Account WHERE Id = :branchId
];
// Navigate up 5 levels max

// Custom self-relationship: Loan_Category__c → Parent_Category__c (Lookup to Loan_Category__c)
List<Loan_Category__c> hierarchy = [
    SELECT Id, Name, Parent_Category__r.Name, Parent_Category__r.Parent_Category__r.Name
    FROM Loan_Category__c
    WHERE Id = :categoryId
];

// Build full hierarchy path (all ancestors):
public static List<String> getCategoryPath(Id categoryId) {
    List<String> path = new List<String>();
    Id currentId = categoryId;
    while(currentId != null) {
        Loan_Category__c cat = [SELECT Id, Name, Parent_Category__c FROM Loan_Category__c WHERE Id = :currentId];
        path.add(0, cat.Name); // add at front for correct order
        currentId = cat.Parent_Category__c;
    }
    return path; // e.g. ['Real Estate', 'Residential', 'Single Family']
}
What are the different Salesforce ID formats — 15-digit vs 18-digit — when does it matter in code?OBJECTS▼

Salesforce IDs have two formats. Both refer to the same record — the difference is case sensitivity handling.

  • 15-character ID: Case-sensitive. Used in Salesforce UI URLs. Shorter. Comparison in Apex is case-sensitive for String comparison but case-insensitive when stored as Id type.
  • 18-character ID: Case-insensitive. Appends 3 checksum characters. Safe for use in Excel (which lowercases text), external systems, and case-insensitive string comparisons.
// Apex Id type handles both — comparison is always case-insensitive
Id id15 = '001Dn000009XYZa';
Id id18 = '001Dn000009XYZaIAG';
System.assertEquals(id15, id18); // TRUE — Apex Id type handles this

// Problem: String comparison is case-SENSITIVE
String strId = '001DN000009XYZA'; // different case
System.assertEquals((Id)strId, id15); // TRUE — cast to Id first

// Best practice: ALWAYS store and compare as Id type, not String
Id safeId = id15; // not String safeId = id15;

// Convert 15 to 18 digit in Apex:
String id18digit = (String) id15; // automatic when cast Id to String
System.debug(id18digit.length()); // 18

// Object prefix codes (first 3 chars) — useful for type checking:
// 001 = Account, 003 = Contact, 006 = Opportunity, 005 = User
// Custom objects vary by org — use Schema.getGlobalDescribe() to find them
String objType = Id.valueOf(someId).getSObjectType().getDescribe().getName();
How do you prevent a user from deleting a parent record if it has active child records — two approaches?OBJECTS▼
// APPROACH 1: Validation Rule on Parent
// Rule on Application__c object, fires on Delete:
// Condition: VLOOKUP("Loan__c Count", "Active_Loan_Count__c", Id) > 0
// (Requires a rollup summary field Active_Loan_Count__c on Application__c)
// Error: "Cannot delete Application with active Loans. Close or deny all loans first."

// APPROACH 2: Apex Before Delete Trigger (more flexible, checks any condition)
trigger ApplicationDeleteTrigger on Application__c (before delete) {
    Set<Id> appIds = Trigger.oldMap.keySet();

    // Check for active child loans
    Map<Id, Integer> activeLoans = new Map<Id, Integer>();
    for(AggregateResult ar : [
        SELECT Application__c, COUNT(Id) cnt
        FROM Loan__c
        WHERE Application__c IN :appIds
        AND Status__c NOT IN ('Closed', 'Denied', 'Withdrawn')
        GROUP BY Application__c
    ]) {
        activeLoans.put((Id)ar.get('Application__c'), (Integer)ar.get('cnt'));
    }

    for(Application__c app : Trigger.old) {
        if(activeLoans.containsKey(app.Id)) {
            app.addError(
                'Cannot delete Application "' + app.Name +
                '" — it has ' + activeLoans.get(app.Id) +
                ' active loan(s). Close or deny all loans first.'
            );
        }
    }
}
How does "Controlled by Parent" OWD work for Contact — explain the nuance?OBJECTS▼

"Controlled by Parent" is a special OWD setting for Contact. When set, Contact record access is determined by the parent Account's sharing. If a user can see the Account, they can see all its Contacts. If they cannot see the Account, they cannot see the Contacts.

  • Only available for Contact (standard) when Account is the parent
  • Contact inherits Account's OWD — if Account is Private, Contacts are effectively Private
  • If Account is Public Read Only → Contacts are Read Only to everyone
  • Sharing rules on Account automatically extend to its Contacts
  • Private Contacts (no AccountId) are only visible to the owner and admins regardless of this setting
  • This setting simplifies sharing model management — you only maintain Account sharing, not both Account and Contact separately
Practical implication

In a mortgage org: if Accounts (Broker Offices) are set to Private, and Contact uses "Controlled by Parent," then each loan officer only sees contacts at brokers they have access to. This prevents loan officer A from seeing loan officer B's borrower contacts — which is a FCRA/privacy best practice.

How do you add a custom LWC to a standard record page — what metadata is required?RECORD PAGE▼

    <apiVersion>59.0</apiVersion>

        <target>lightning__RecordPage</target>  <!-- record detail pages -->

        <target>lightning__HomePage</target>     <!-- home tab -->
    </targets>
    <targetConfigs>
        <targetConfig targets="lightning__RecordPage">
            <property name="recordId" type="String" />        <!-- auto-populated from page context -->
            <property name="objectApiName" type="String" />   <!-- auto-populated -->
            <property name="cardTitle" type="String" label="Card Title" default="Loan Summary"/>
            <!-- Admin can configure cardTitle in App Builder -->
        </targetConfig>
    </targetConfigs>
</LightningComponentBundle>
// loanSummaryCard.js

// After saving the component and deploying to org:

Two custom LWC components on the same record page — how do they communicate? Complete working code.RECORD PAGE▼

Sibling components on the same record page cannot use CustomEvent (that is only parent-child). Use Lightning Message Service (LMS).

<!-- STEP 1: Create Message Channel -->
<!-- force-app/main/default/messageChannels/LoanUpdate__c.messageChannel-meta.xml -->
<LightningMessageChannel xmlns="http://soap.sforce.com/2006/04/metadata">
    <masterLabel>Loan Update Channel</masterLabel>
    <isExposed>true</isExposed>
    <lightningMessageFields>
        <fieldName>loanId</fieldName><description>Loan record Id</description>
    </lightningMessageFields>
    <lightningMessageFields>
        <fieldName>status</fieldName><description>New status value</description>
    </lightningMessageFields>
</LightningMessageChannel>
// STEP 2: Component A — PUBLISHER (e.g., Status Update button component)
import { LightningElement, api, wire } from 'lwc';
import { publish, MessageContext } from 'lightning/messageService';
import LOAN_UPDATE_CHANNEL from '@salesforce/messageChannel/LoanUpdate__c';
import updateStatus from '@salesforce/apex/LoanController.updateStatus';
import { ShowToastEvent } from 'lightning/platformShowToastEvent';

export default class LoanStatusUpdater extends LightningElement {
    @api recordId;
    @wire(MessageContext) messageContext;
    newStatus = '';

    handleStatusChange(e) { this.newStatus = e.detail.value; }

    async handleSave() {
        try {
            await updateStatus({ loanId: this.recordId, status: this.newStatus });
            // Publish — all subscribers on this page will receive this
            publish(this.messageContext, LOAN_UPDATE_CHANNEL, {
                loanId: this.recordId,
                status: this.newStatus
            });
            this.dispatchEvent(new ShowToastEvent({ title: 'Updated', variant: 'success' }));
        } catch(e) {
            this.dispatchEvent(new ShowToastEvent({ title: 'Failed', message: e.body?.message, variant: 'error' }));
        }
    }
}
// STEP 3: Component B — SUBSCRIBER (e.g., Loan Timeline component)
import { LightningElement, api, wire } from 'lwc';
import { subscribe, unsubscribe, APPLICATION_SCOPE, MessageContext } from 'lightning/messageService';
import { refreshApex } from '@salesforce/apex';
import LOAN_UPDATE_CHANNEL from '@salesforce/messageChannel/LoanUpdate__c';
import getLoanTimeline from '@salesforce/apex/LoanController.getLoanTimeline';

export default class LoanTimeline extends LightningElement {
    @api recordId;
    @wire(MessageContext) messageContext;
    subscription = null;
    wiredTimelineResult;

    @wire(getLoanTimeline, { loanId: '$recordId' })
    wiredTimeline(result) { this.wiredTimelineResult = result; }

    connectedCallback() {
        this.subscription = subscribe(
            this.messageContext, LOAN_UPDATE_CHANNEL,
            (msg) => this.handleMessage(msg),
            { scope: APPLICATION_SCOPE }
        );
    }

    async handleMessage(msg) {
        if(msg.loanId !== this.recordId) return;
        // Status updated — refresh timeline data
        await refreshApex(this.wiredTimelineResult);
    }

    disconnectedCallback() {
        unsubscribe(this.subscription); // ALWAYS unsubscribe — prevent memory leaks
    }
}
Complete getRecord + getFieldValue implementation — all patterns explainedRECORD PAGE▼
// loanDetailsWidget.js
import { LightningElement, api, wire } from 'lwc';
import { getRecord, getFieldValue } from 'lightning/uiRecordApi';

// ALWAYS import fields using schema imports — never hardcode 'Loan__c.Status__c'
import LOAN_STATUS  from '@salesforce/schema/Loan__c.Status__c';
import LOAN_AMOUNT  from '@salesforce/schema/Loan__c.LoanAmount__c';
import LOAN_TYPE    from '@salesforce/schema/Loan__c.LoanType__c';
import OFFICER_NAME from '@salesforce/schema/Loan__c.LoanOfficer__r.Name'; // related field
import OFFICER_EMAIL from '@salesforce/schema/Loan__c.LoanOfficer__r.Email';

const FIELDS = [LOAN_STATUS, LOAN_AMOUNT, LOAN_TYPE, OFFICER_NAME, OFFICER_EMAIL];

export default class LoanDetailsWidget extends LightningElement {
    @api recordId;
    error;
    wiredRecord;

    // Pattern 1: Wire as function (gives you both data and error)
    @wire(getRecord, { recordId: '$recordId', fields: FIELDS })
    wiredLoan(result) {
        this.wiredRecord = result;  // store for refreshApex
        if(result.error) this.error = result.error.body?.message;
    }

    // Pattern 2: Wire as property (simpler syntax)
    @wire(getRecord, { recordId: '$recordId', fields: FIELDS })
    loan; // this.loan.data and this.loan.error

    // Use getFieldValue to safely extract values — handles null/undefined gracefully
    get loanStatus()  { return getFieldValue(this.loan.data, LOAN_STATUS); }
    get loanAmount()  { return getFieldValue(this.loan.data, LOAN_AMOUNT); }
    get officerName() { return getFieldValue(this.loan.data, OFFICER_NAME); }

    get formattedAmount() {
        const amt = this.loanAmount;
        if(!amt) return '--';
        return new Intl.NumberFormat('en-US', { style: 'currency', currency: 'USD' }).format(amt);
    }

    get statusBadgeClass() {
        const map = { 'Approved': 'slds-badge slds-theme_success', 'Denied': 'slds-badge slds-theme_error' };
        return map[this.loanStatus] || 'slds-badge';
    }

    get isLoading() { return !this.loan?.data && !this.loan?.error; }

    // Refresh after updateRecord or DML:
    async handleAfterUpdate() {
        await refreshApex(this.wiredRecord); // re-fetches from server
    }
}
// IMPORTANT: getRecord only fires when recordId is truthy
// '$recordId' — the $ prefix means reactive — wire refires when recordId changes
// If recordId is undefined (component placed on non-record page), wire does not fire
How do you use updateRecord from LWC without a form — complete pattern?RECORD PAGE▼
// Inline update without lightning-record-edit-form
import { LightningElement, api, wire } from 'lwc';
import { updateRecord } from 'lightning/uiRecordApi';
import { ShowToastEvent } from 'lightning/platformShowToastEvent';
import { refreshApex } from '@salesforce/apex';
import LOAN_STATUS from '@salesforce/schema/Loan__c.Status__c';
import LOAN_ID from '@salesforce/schema/Loan__c.Id';

export default class LoanApproveButton extends LightningElement {
    @api recordId;

    async handleApprove() {
        // Build fields object — Id field is REQUIRED
        const fields = {};
        fields[LOAN_ID.fieldApiName] = this.recordId;
        fields[LOAN_STATUS.fieldApiName] = 'Approved';

        try {
            await updateRecord({ fields });
            this.dispatchEvent(new ShowToastEvent({
                title: 'Approved', message: 'Loan approved successfully', variant: 'success'
            }));
            // Force page to reload record data
            this.dispatchEvent(new CustomEvent('recordupdated'));
        } catch(error) {
            // Trigger addError() messages surface here automatically
            const msg = error.body?.output?.errors?.[0]?.message || error.body?.message;
            this.dispatchEvent(new ShowToastEvent({
                title: 'Approval Failed', message: msg, variant: 'error', mode: 'sticky'
            }));
        }
    }
}
How do you use createRecord and deleteRecord from LWC — with confirmation dialog?RECORD PAGE▼
import { createRecord, deleteRecord } from 'lightning/uiRecordApi';
import LOAN_OBJECT from '@salesforce/schema/Loan__c';
import LOAN_AMOUNT from '@salesforce/schema/Loan__c.LoanAmount__c';
import APPLICATION_FIELD from '@salesforce/schema/Loan__c.Application__c';

// CREATE RECORD
async handleCreateLoan() {
    const fields = {};
    fields[LOAN_AMOUNT.fieldApiName] = this.loanAmount;
    fields[APPLICATION_FIELD.fieldApiName] = this.recordId; // parent Id
    const recordInput = { apiName: LOAN_OBJECT.objectApiName, fields };

    try {
        const newRecord = await createRecord(recordInput);
        this.dispatchEvent(new ShowToastEvent({
            title: 'Loan Created', message: 'ID: ' + newRecord.id, variant: 'success'
        }));
        // Navigate to new record:
        this[NavigationMixin.Navigate]({
            type: 'standard__recordPage',
            attributes: { recordId: newRecord.id, actionName: 'view' }
        });
    } catch(e) {
        this.dispatchEvent(new ShowToastEvent({ title: 'Create Failed', message: e.body?.message, variant: 'error' }));
    }
}

// DELETE RECORD with confirmation
showDeleteConfirm = false;
handleDeleteClick() { this.showDeleteConfirm = true; }
handleCancelDelete() { this.showDeleteConfirm = false; }

async handleConfirmDelete() {
    this.showDeleteConfirm = false;
    try {
        await deleteRecord(this.recordId);
        this.dispatchEvent(new ShowToastEvent({ title: 'Deleted', variant: 'success' }));
        // Navigate back to list view after delete
        this[NavigationMixin.Navigate]({ type: 'standard__objectPage',
            attributes: { objectApiName: 'Loan__c', actionName: 'list' }
        });
    } catch(e) {
        this.dispatchEvent(new ShowToastEvent({ title: 'Delete Failed', message: e.body?.message, variant: 'error' }));
    }
}
How do you use the lwc:ref directive and this.refs — what replaced querySelector in modern LWC?RECORD PAGE▼

In API version 59.0+, Salesforce introduced lwc:ref as a cleaner alternative to this.template.querySelector() for accessing DOM elements.

<!-- OLD pattern — still works but less elegant -->
<lightning-input data-id="loanAmount" label="Loan Amount"></lightning-input>
// In JS: this.template.querySelector('[data-id="loanAmount"]')

<!-- NEW pattern — lwc:ref (API 59.0+) -->
<lightning-input lwc:ref="loanAmountInput" label="Loan Amount"></lightning-input>
<lightning-input lwc:ref="ficoInput" label="FICO Score"></lightning-input>

// In JS — use this.refs.refName
handleValidate() {
    const loanInput = this.refs.loanAmountInput;
    const ficoInput = this.refs.ficoInput;

    let isValid = loanInput.checkValidity() && ficoInput.checkValidity();
    if(!isValid) {
        loanInput.reportValidity();
        ficoInput.reportValidity();
    }
    return isValid;
}

// querySelectorAll still needed for multiple elements:
handleValidateAll() {
    const allInputs = this.template.querySelectorAll('lightning-input');
    return [...allInputs].every(input => {
        const valid = input.checkValidity();
        if(!valid) input.reportValidity();
        return valid;
    });
}
What are the different LWC targets in js-meta.xml — what does each enable?RECORD PAGE▼

lightning__RecordPageObject record pages in App BuilderrecordId, objectApiName
lightning__AppPageLightning App home pagesNone auto-populated
lightning__HomePageSalesforce Home tabNone auto-populated
lightning__TabCustom tab (standalone page)None auto-populated
lightning__FlowScreenEmbedded in Screen FlowFlow variables mapped in builder
lightning__UtilityBarUtility bar at bottom of appNone auto-populated
lightning__InboxSalesforce Inbox (email)recordId
lightningCommunity__PageExperience Cloud sitesrecordId
Important

A component without isExposed=true and at least one target is invisible in App Builder — it can only be used programmatically (embedded in another component or called from code). Setting isExposed=true without targets makes it available globally but with no specific context.

How do you show a loading spinner correctly for all async scenarios in LWC?RECORD PAGE▼
// Handle all 3 async scenarios properly
isLoading = true;  // start as true — wire fires immediately on load
error = undefined;
data = undefined;

// Scenario 1: Wire adapter
@wire(getLoanData, { loanId: '$recordId' })
wiredLoan({ data, error }) {
    this.isLoading = false;  // wire resolved — either data or error
    if(data) { this.data = data; this.error = undefined; }
    else if(error) { this.error = error.body?.message; }
}

// Scenario 2: Imperative call — fully manual isLoading control
async handleSearch() {
    this.isLoading = true;
    this.error = undefined;
    try {
        this.data = await searchLoans({ term: this.searchTerm });
    } catch(e) {
        this.error = e.body?.message || 'Search failed';
    } finally {
        this.isLoading = false; // ALWAYS in finally — even if error occurs
    }
}

// Scenario 3: Multiple parallel async calls
async loadAllData() {
    this.isLoading = true;
    try {
        const [loans, borrowers, docs] = await Promise.all([
            getLoans({ appId: this.recordId }),
            getBorrowers({ appId: this.recordId }),
            getDocuments({ appId: this.recordId })
        ]);
        this.loans = loans; this.borrowers = borrowers; this.docs = docs;
    } catch(e) { this.error = e.body?.message; }
    finally { this.isLoading = false; }
}
<!-- Template: handle all states -->
<template>
    <lightning-card title="Loan Dashboard">
        <template lwc:if={isLoading}>
            <lightning-spinner alternative-text="Loading..." size="medium"></lightning-spinner>
        </template>
        <template lwc:elseif={error}>
            <div class="slds-notify slds-notify_alert slds-theme_error">{error}</div>
        </template>
        <template lwc:else>
            <!-- render data here -->
        </template>
    </lightning-card>
</template>
